Introduction
The note is a collection of concepts and results that I scrounged from many places that I think you
will find useful in navigating and completing your first year in the field of economics at Cornell
University. It is intended to be a self-contained reference for you as you progress in your first year.
The course itself prioritises practicality, rather than rigour. We will only go over proofs that deepens
your understanding of the relevant result and/or to help you familiarise yourselves with the proof
techniques that would be useful in your core classes. We will not be spending time to practice the
mathematics you cover—you will be doing that throughout the rest of the year. Finally, even if
some materials seem esoteric at times (especially at the beginning of the class), I promise you that
there is a good reason behind why we are going over them.

Tips for success Reading books and notes and listening to lectures (i.e. passing learning) won’t
work—you have to learn actively by working through the proofs and examples we go over in class
(active reading), taking notes during class (active listening), and solving practice questions. Working
in groups is strongly encouraged BUT always try to work through all problems on your own before
meeting with others. And check your understanding by explaining it to others!

Textbooks
   • Sundaram, “A First Course in Optimization Theory”.

   • Simon and Blume, “Mathematics for Economists”.

   • Sydsaester, Hammond, Seierstad and Strom, “Further Mathematics for Economic Analysis”.

   • Hansen, “Probability and Statistics for Economists”

If you find any mistakes and/or typos, please let me know by emailing me at takumahabu@cornell.edu.

Part I
Foundational concepts
1       Preliminaries
Throughout, I will use the symbol “:=” to define things.1 For example, if I want to define a function
f to be a constant function that always equals 1, I will write f (x) := 1. Note that I can write 1 := 2
and it wouldn’t technically be wrong—it just means that I will use 1 to mean 2 (of course, I will
not write such nonsense!).
    I denote N := {1, 2, . . .} as the set of natural numbers,2 Z := {. . . , −2, −1, 0, 1, 2, . . .} as the
set of integers, Q := { ab : a ∈ Z, b ∈ N} as the set of rational numbers, and R as the set of real
numbers.

1.1      Proofs
1.1.1     Propositional logic
A proposition is a statement that has either value true (T) or false (F) but not both. Let p and
q be two propositions. Two statements p and q are logically equivalent, written p ≡ q, if they
always have the same truth/false value; i.e., they have the same truth values. Write the negation
of proposition p as ¬p (read “not p”). The compound statement “p and q” is written p ∧ q. The
compound statement “p or q” is written p ∨ q. The table below sets is a truth table the truth values
of negation and compounding of proposition(s).

                                             Table 1: A truth table.

                                        p    q       ¬p   ¬q    p∧q   p∨q
                                        T    T       F    F      T     T
                                        T    F       F    T      F     T
                                        F    T       T    F      F     T
                                        F    F       T    T      F     F

Exercise 1 (De Morgan’s laws). When negating compound statements, prove that we can move
the negation “inside” and flip the “sign” as below:

                                               ¬ (p ∨ q) ≡ ¬p ∧ ¬q,
                                               ¬ (p ∧ q) ≡ ¬p ∨ ¬q.

     A statement that is always false is called a contradiction (e.g., p ∧ (¬p)). A statement that is
always true is called a tautology (e.g., p ∨ (¬p)).
     We say p implies q, denoted p ⇒ q , if it is the case that “if p (is true), then q (is true);”
importantly, no conclusion about q can be drawn if p is false. The converse of p ⇒ q is q ⇒ p (read
either as “if q, then p” or “p only if q”). A two-way implication, written p ⇔ q (read if and only if
(iff )), means p ⇒ q and q ⇒ p; i.e.,

                                            p ⇔ q ≡ (p ⇒ q) ∧ (q ⇒ p).

The contrapositive of p ⇒ q is ¬q ⇒ ¬p.
                              ∆
    1 Other notations include = and =def .
    2 Sometime people also include 0 as part of N.




                                                          -8-
Math Review                                                                                       2024


Exercise 2 (Truth table). Extend the truth table, Table 1, to include p ⇒ q, q ⇒ p, p ⇔ q, and
¬q ⇒ ¬p.

Exercise 3 (Modus Ponens). Suppose it is the case that “if it is raining, then there are clouds”
and “it is raining.” Can you conclude whether there are any clouds?

Exercise 4 (Modus Tollens). Suppose it is the case that “if it is raining, then there are clouds”
and “there are no clouds.” Can you conclude whether it is raining?

    A variable proposition, or predicates, are propositions whose truth value depends on some other
parameter, say x that can take values from a collection of values X. Write p(x) as the truth value
of statement p given parameter x. Say that p(x) is sufficient for q(x) if p(x) ⇒ q(x) is true for all
possible values of x, and say that p(x) is necessary for q(x) if the converse, q(x) ⇒ p(x), is true for
all possible values of x. The statement “∀x ∈ X, p(x)”, or equivalently, “p(x) ∀x ∈ X”, means that
p(x) is true for all possible parameter x in X. The symbol ∀ is called the universal quantifier and
can also be read as “for every.” The statement “∃x ∈ X, p(x)” means that p(x) is true for some
parameter x in X. The symbol ∃ is called the existential qualifier and can also be read as “there
exists” or “for at least one.” Write ∃! to mean “there exists unique”.

Proposition 1. When negating qualifiers, we can move the negation “inside” and swap the “sign”
as below:

                                ¬ (∀x ∈ X, p (x)) ≡ (∃x ∈ X, ¬p (x)) ,
                                ¬ (∃x ∈ X, p (x)) ≡ (∀x ∈ X, ¬p (x)) .

Proof. Note that
                                 ∀x ∈ X, p (x) ≡ p (x) ∧ p (x′ ) ∧ · · · .
Applying De Morgan law repeatedly to gives

                               ¬ (∀x ∈ X, p (x)) ≡ ¬p (x) ∨ ¬p (x) ∧ · · ·

and so there must be at least one x in X for which p(x) is false. The proof for the negation of the
existential qualifier is analogous.                                                              ■

Exercise 5. Read the following statement.

                    ∀ϵ > 0, ∃δϵ,x > 0, |y − f (s)| < ϵ ∀s ∈ S\ {x} : |x − s| < δϵ,x .

Convince yourself that the statement above can equivalently be written as

                   ∀ϵ > 0, ∃δϵ,x > 0, (|x − s| < δϵ,x , s ∈ S\ {x}) ⇒ |y − f (s)| < ϵ.

Finally, write and read the negation of the statement(s) above?

1.1.2   Proof methods
A proof of a statement shows that the statement is always true given some assumptions. An example
does not constitute a proof; however, a counterexample can be used to show that a statement is
false (i.e., can be used to disprove a statement). Following are the main methods to approach a
proof.

   • Deduction/direct proof: Start with what you know, set up a chain of argument to end up
     with what you wanted to show.


                                                   -9-
Math Review                                                                                        2024


   • Contraposition: direct proof of the contrapositive statement.

   • Contradiction: Assume that the required result is false then work to obtain a contradiction.

   • Induction: Prove the base case (e.g., n = 0) then the induction step while assuming the
     induction hypothesis (assume it holds for n, then show that this implies the result for n + 1).
     Then, invoke the Principle of Mathematical Induction.

Principle of Mathematical Induction If S is a subset of N such that 1 ∈ S and i + 1 ∈ S
whenever i ∈ S, then S = N.

Example 1. Suppose we wish to prove that the sum of the first n natural numbers is n(n+1)
                                                                                      2   for
any n ∈ N.

   • A direct proof is to fix n ∈ N, and let the sum be Sn :

                                       Sn = 1 + 2 + · · · + (n − 1) + n.

      Writing in reverse order:
                                       Sn = n + (n − 1) + · · · + 2 + 1.
      And os
                            2Sn = (n + 1) + (n + 1) + · · · + (n + 1) + (n + 1) .
      Rearranging gives Sn = n(n+1)
                                2   . Since we chose n arbitrarily, the result holds for all n ∈ N.

   • A proof by induction proceeds first by ensuring that the base case n = 1 holds—the sum of
     just the first natural number is just 1. From that formula, we also have S1 = 1(1+1)    2    = 1.
     Thus, the formula holds for n = 1. To proceed, now suppose that the formula holds for some
     specific value of n, say n = k for some k ∈ N. We ask now if the formula holds for n = k + 1.
     That is, suppose
                                                                 k (k + 1)
                                Sk = 1 + 2 + · · · + k − 1 + k =           .
                                                                      2
     Then,
                                            k (k + 1)               (k + 1) (k + 1 + 1)
                    Sk+1 = Sk + (k + 1) =              + (k + 1) =                      .
                                                 2                           2
     Because the last expression is the same as the formula for Sk when we replace k by k + 1, we
     can conclude that if the formula is correct for k, then it is also correct for k + 1. Finally, we
     invoke the Principle of Mathematical Induction to conclude that formula is correct for all n.

Example 2 (Proof by induction). We take the overlapping generation (OLG) model that you will
cover in Macroeconomics. An agent, or a generation, that lives for two periods is born in each time
period t ∈ N. Let xit ∈ R and eit ∈ R+ denote generation i’s consumption and endowment in period
t respectively in a pure exchange economy. Assume that there is an “initial old” generation in t = 1
whose consumption is denoted x01 . By assumption, xit = 0 and eit = 0 for all t ∈ / N\{i, i + 1}, and
each generation have strictly positive endowments while they are alive; i.e., for all i ∈ N, eit > 0 for
t ∈ {i, i + 1}. Market clearing (or feasibility) condition is that

                                     xt−1
                                      t   + xtt = et−1
                                                   t   + ett ∀t ∈ N.

Let pt ∈ R+ be the price of goods in period t, then the budget constraint for generation t ∈ N is

                                  pt xtt + pt+1 xtt+1 = pt ett + pt+1 ett+1 ,                      (1.1)



                                                    - 10 -
Math Review                                                                                        2024


where we have implicitly assumed that the preferences are strictly increasing so that the, budget
constraint binds. We will prove by induction that any competitive equilibrium is autarky (meaning
no trade); i.e., for any price vector,

                                       xtt−1 = et−1
                                                t   , xtt = ett ∀t ∈ N.                            (1.2)

  First, we prove the base case: Since the initial old care only about consumption in t = 1, it
must be that
                                           x01 = e01 .
Combining with the period-1 market-clearing condition, x01 + x11 = e01 + e11 , implies

                                                  x11 = e11 .

Thus, (1.2) holds for t = 1.
    Now, we make the inductive hypothesis that (1.2) holds for some period t > 1. The goal is
to show that (1.2) then holds for period t + 1. Since xtt = ett by the inductive hypothesis, budget
constraint, (1.1), implies that xtt+1 = ett+1 . Combining this with the period-t + 1 market clearing
condition, xtt+1 + xt+1             t+1               t+1    t+1
                     t+1 = et+1 + et+1 , yields that xt+1 = et+1 . Thus, (1.2) indeed holds for period
                            t

t + 1.
    Therefore, by induction, (1.2) holds for all t ∈ N.

Remark 1. I often write “TFU: [some statement]”. The TFU stands “True, False, or Uncertain,”
what I mean here is that I’m looking for a proof of the statement (the “T” case), or a counterexample
(the “F” case), or cases in which the statement is sometimes true and other times false (the “U”
case).

1.2    Sets
A set X is a collection of objects or elements. If x is an element of X, we write x ∈ X and if x is
not an element of X, then we write x ∈   / X. The symbol ∅ := {x : x ̸= x} denotes the empty set
and ∅ ⊆ X for any set X. A set X is nonempty if X ̸= ∅.
    Suppose X and Y are sets. Then, X is a subset of Y if every element of X is also an element
of Y ; i.e.,
                                     X ⊆ Y ⇔ ∀x ∈ X, x ∈ Y.
The set X is a proper subset of Y if X is a subset of Y and, in addition, there exists y ∈
                                                                                         / X such
that y ∈ Y . 3

                         X ⊂ Y ⇔ (∀x ∈ X, x ∈ Y ) ∧ (∃y ∈ Y, y ∈  / X) .
Two sets X and Y are equal if every element of one is contained in the other; i.e.,

                         X = Y ⇔ (X ⊆ Y ) ∧ (Y ⊆ X) ⇔ (z ∈ X ⇔ z ∈ Y ) .

The union of two sets X and Y are defined as

                                   X ∪ Y := {z : (z ∈ X) ∨ (z ∈ Y )} .

The intersection of two sets X and Y are defined as

              X ∩ Y := {z : (z ∈ X) ∧ (z ∈ Y )} ≡ {z ∈ X : z ∈ Y } ≡ {z ∈ Y : z ∈ X} .
  3 Rather confusingly, some write ⊂ to mean ⊆, in which case a proper subset is denoted ⊊ or ⫋.




                                                    - 11 -
Math Review                                                                                                                       2024


The intersection and union are both commutative (i.e., X ∩ Y = Y ∩ X and X ∪ Y = Y ∪ X) and
associative (i.e., (X ∩ Y ) ∩ Z = X ∩ (Y ∩ Z) and (X ∪ Y ) ∪ Z = X ∪ (Y ∪ Z)). Moreover,

                                                X ∩ Y = X ⇔ X ⊆ Y,
                                                X ∪ Y = X ⇔ Y ⊆ X.

Let I be some set, and Xi be sets for each i ∈ I. We refer to {Xi }i∈I as a collection of sets (i.e., a
set of sets) and I as an index set. Denote the union and intersection of all Xi ’s, respectively, as
                                    [

                                          i∈I
                                          \

                                                  !

The two sets X and Y are disjoint if they do not share elements; i.e., if X ∩ Y = ∅. The difference

                          X\Y := {z : (z ∈ X) ∧ (z ∈

                                                          X c := Z\X.

Given a set X, the power set of X, denoted 2X or P(X), is the collection of all subsets of X
(including the empty set); i.e.,

                                           2X ≡ P(X) := {S : S ⊆ X} .

Set of all sets is not well defined (google Russell’s paradox )—this is a potential issue that can arise
in, for example, information design when thinking about the set of all possible “signals” about an
unknown state of the world.

Example 3. Let X := {a, b, c}. Then,

                            2X = {∅, {a} , {b} , {c} , {a, b} , {b, c} , {a, c} , X} .

   The cardinality of a set X, denoted |X|, is the number of elements the set X contains. The set
X is finite if |X| < ∞ and infinite if it is not finite. If X is finite, then

                                             X ⊂ Y ⇒ |X| < |Y | .

    Given two sets X and Y , and x ∈ X and y ∈ Y , (x, y) is an ordered pair (or a tuple) such that
(x, y) is different from (y, x). The Cartesian product of two sets X and Y is the set of all ordered
pairs; i.e.,
                                  X × Y := {(x, y) : x ∈ X, y ∈ Y } .
More generally, given a finite index set I = {1, . . . , n}, the Cartesian product of sets X1 , . . . , Xn is
the set of all n-tuples; i.e.,5

                           ×ni=1 Xi := {(x1 , . . . , xn ) : xi ∈ Xi ∀i ∈ {1, . . . , n}} .

If Xi = X for all i ∈ I (and |I| = n), then we write

                                                 X n ≡ ×i∈I Xi .

   A partition of Y is a collection of disjoint sets whose union is Y ; i.e., a collection {Xi }ni=1 is a
partition of Y if                           !

(“∀i ̸= j” means ∀(i, j) ∈ {(x, y) ∈ {1, . . . , n}2 : x ̸= y}.)

1.3     Binary relations, partial and total orders
1.3.1    Binary relations
Given sets X and Y , R ⊆ X × Y is called a binary relation from X to Y . Write

                                                xRy ⇔ (x, y) ∈ R,
                                              ¬xRy ⇔ (x, y) ∈
                                                            / R.

The inverse of a binary relation R from X to Y is a binary relation from Y to X defined as

                                   R−1 := {(y, x) ∈ Y × X : (x, y) ∈ R} .

Say that a binary relation on X is:
   • reflexive if ∀x ∈ X, xRx;

   • symmetric if ∀x, y ∈ X, xRy ⇔ yRx;

   • transitive if ∀x, y, z ∈ X, (xRy ∧ yRz) ⇒ xRz;

   • antisymmetric if ∀x, y ∈ X, (xRy ∧ yRx) ⇒ x = y (i.e., rules out ties);

   • complete if ∀x, y ∈ X, either xRy or yRx (i.e., every pair is ordered).
  5 Sometimes, Cartesian product is also written as
                                                      Q
                                                          i∈I Xi .



                                                       - 13 -
Math Review                                                                                          2024


Note, in particular, that completeness implies reflexiveness.

1.3.2   Other relations
A binary relation ≥ on X (i.e., ≥⊆ X × X) is:

   • a preorder if it is reflexive and transitive;

   • an equivalence relation, denoted ∼, if it is reflexive, symmetric and transitive;

   • a partial order if it is reflexive, transitive and antisymmetric

   • a total order if it is complete, transitive and antisymmetric.6

Given a partial order on X, say that (X, ≥) is a partially ordered set (poset). Given a total order
on X, say that (X, ≥) is a totally order set (toset).
   For any x ∈ X, equivalence class of x relative to ∼ is defined as

                                            [x]∼ := {y ∈ X : y ∼ x} .

A quotient set of X relative to ∼, denoted X/ ∼, is the class of all equivalence classes relative to
∼; i.e.,
                                     X/ ∼:= {[x]∼ : x ∈ X} .
   Totally ordered set is a special case of partially ordered sets. Based on a partially ordered set
(X, ≥), we may define
                                     
                                 > := (x, y) ∈ X 2 : (x ≥ y) ∧ ¬ (y ≥ x) ,
                                 ≤ :=≥−1 ,
                                 < :=>−1 .

Observe that if ≥ is a binary relation on X, then ≥ is a binary relation on any S ⊆ X. A rational
preference relation on X is a binary relation on X that is complete and transitive. You will see
more of this in consumer theory.

Example 4. (R, ≥) is a total order defined as:
                                    
                                 ≥:= (x, y) ∈ R2 : y − x is nonnegative .

However, (Rn , ≥) with n ∈ N\{1} is a partial order defined as
                         n         n
             ≥:= {((xi )i=1 , (yi )i=1 ) ∈ Rn × Rn : yi − xi is nonnegative ∀i ∈ {1, . . . , n}} .

Observe that above is not complete (e.g., with n = 2, (1, 2) and (2, 1) are not ordered).

Example 5. Given a set X, (2X , ⊆) is a partial order.

   There are many other examples of partial orders: e.g., first-order and second-order stochastic
dominance of distributions, ordering of utility functions using absolute risk aversion.
  6 Some people refer to total orders as linear orders.




                                                          - 14 -
Math Review                                                                                     2024


1.3.3   Upper and lower bounds
Let (X, ≥) be a partially ordered set and S ⊆ X. An element u ∈ X is an upper bound of S if,
∀s ∈ S, u ≥ s. If such an u exists, then we say that the set S is bounded from above. An element in
S is a maximum of S if it is an upper bound of S and we denote it as max S. Similarly, ℓ ∈ X is a
lower bound of S if, ∀s ∈ S, ℓ ≤ s . If such an ℓ exists, then we say that the set S is bounded from
below. A set S is bounded if its both bounded from above and bounded from below. An element is
a minimum of S if it is a lower bound of S and we denote it as min S.
    An element is the least upper bound or the supremum of S, denoted supS, if (i) sup S is an
upper bound of S; and (ii) sup S ≤ u for any upper bound u of S. An element is the greatest lower
bound or the infimum of S, denoted inf S ∈ X, if (i) inf X is a lower bound of S; and (ii) inf X ≥ ℓ
for any lower bound ℓ ∈ X of S.
    Whenever a maximum or a minimum of S exists, they must be unique so that it make sense to
refer to them as the maximum or minimum.

Proposition 2. If a maximum or a minimum of S exists, then it must be unique.

Proof. By way of contradiction (BWOC), suppose that there are two distinct maxima of S, denoted
s1 and s2 , where s1 ̸= s2 . By definition of maximum, s1 , s2 ≥ s ∀s ∈ S. In particular, it must be
that
                                         s1 ≥ s2 and s2 ≥ s1 .
Since S is a partially ordered set, antisymmetry implies that s1 = s2 ; a contradiction.           ■

  By definition, the supremum is the minimum of the set of upper bounds and the infimum is the
maximum of the set of lower bounds.

Proposition 3. If a maximum exists, then it is also the supremum. If a minimum exists, then it
is also the infimum.

    The following tells us that taking supreme of a smaller set is lower and infimum of a smaller set
is greater.

Proposition 4. Suppose that S ⊆ X where (X, ≥) is a partially ordered set. If sup S and sup X
exist, then sup S ≤ sup X. If inf S and inf X exist, then inf S ≥ inf X.

Proof. Since sup X is an upper bound of X and S ⊆ X, sup X is also an upper bound of S. Since
sup S is the least upper bound of S, we must have sup S ≤ sup X. Since inf X is a lower bound of
X and S ⊆ X, inf X is also a lower bound of S. Since inf S is the greatest lower bound of S, we
must have inf X ≤ inf S.                                                                      ■

   Supremum or infimum may not exist even when upper or lower bounds exist. For example,
suppose X := R\{0} and S := {x ∈ R : x < 0}. Then, S ⊆ X, (X, ≤) is a partially ordered set,
and, for example, 1 ∈ X is an upper bound of S. However, there is no least upper bound. Thus,
we say that a partially ordered set (X, ≤) has the least upper bound (resp. greatest lower bound)
property if any nonempty subset of X bounded from above (resp. below) has a least upper (resp.
greatest lower) bound. We will study such sets in details in ECON 6701 in the context of lattices.
We also briefly mention a lemma that we will use a little later.

Lemma 1 (Zorn’s lemma). Suppose a partially order set (X, ≥) has the property that every totally
ordered subset of X has an upper bound in X. Then, the set X contains at least one maximal
element.




                                               - 15 -
Math Review                                                                                          2024


1.4     Functions
A function (or a mapping) f is a binary relation f from X to Y such that: ∀x ∈ X, (i) ∃y ∈ Y ,
(x, y) ∈ f ; and (ii) (x, y1 ), (x, y2 ) ∈ f ⇒ y1 = y2 . That is, a function assigns a single element in Y
to every element in X. A function from X to Y is denoted

                                            f : X → Y or f ∈ Y X

and the assigned element is denoted f (x). We call X as the domain and Y as the codomain of f .
If X = Y , then the function is a self-map on X.
    The image of a set S ⊆ X under a function f : X → Y is

                                    f (S) := {y ∈ Y : ∃s ∈ S, f (s) = y} .

The range of f : X → Y is the set of all values that f can generate in its codomain and is denoted
f (X). It is possible that f (X) ⊂ Y .
    The graph of a function f : X → Y is defined

                                   gr (f ) := {(x, y) ∈ X × Y : y = f (x)} ,

The preimage (or the inverse image) of f : X → Y is defined as subset of the set of all elements in
the domain that maps to members of the codomain; i.e.,

                                      f −1 (Y ) := {x ∈ X : f (x) ∈ Y } .

Proposition 5. Suppose f : X → Y , X1 , X2 ⊆ X, and Y1 , Y2 ⊆ Y .

  (i) X1 ⊆ X2 ⇒ f (X1 ) ⊆ f (X2 ) and Y1 ⊆ Y2 ⇒ f −1 (Y1 ) ⊆ f −1 (Y2 );

 (ii) f (X1 ∪ X2 ) = f (X1 ) ∪ f (X2 );

(iii) f (X1 ∩ X2 ) ⊆ f (X1 ) ∩ f (X2 ) (why not equal?);

 (iv) f −1 (Y1 ∪ Y2 ) = f −1 (Y1 ) ∪ f −1 (Y2 );

 (v) f −1 (Y1 ∩ Y2 ) = f −1 (Y1 ) ∩ f −1 (Y2 ) (why is this equal?);

 (vi) f −1 (Y \Y1 ) = X\f −1 (Y ).

Exercise 6. In the previous proposition, given an example in which (iii) holds “strictly.” Prove
(v).

   A function f : X → Y is:

   • one-one or injective if every x ∈ X is assigned to a unique element in Y ; i.e.,

                                     ∀x1 , x2 ∈ X, f (x1 ) = f (x2 ) ⇒ x1 = x2 .

   • onto or surjective if, for all y ∈ Y , there exists x ∈ X such that f (x) = y; i.e.,

                                                    f (X) = Y ;

   • bijective if it is injective and surjective so that every element in the domain is mapped to a
     unique element in the codomain;

   • invertible if the inverse image mapping is a function from Y to X.

    Suppose S ⊆ X ⊆ Z and f : X → Y . The restriction of f to S is a function f |S : S → Y

   A projection from X × Y onto X is a function f : X × Y → X defined by f (x, y) := x. Similarly,
a projection from X × Y onto Y is a function f : X × Y → Y defined by f (x, y) := y.

Remark 2 (Cardinality of sets). For example, X = {1, 2, 3, 4} is numerically equivalent to Y =
{4, 7, 10, 13} under the (bijective) function f (x) = 3x + 1. A set X is countable if it is numerically
equivalent to N and uncountable if it is not countable. Note Q is countable while R is uncountable.

    • multiplication by a scalar constant, denoted λf with λ ∈ R, is defined by (λf )(x) := λf (x);
    • the product, denoted f g, is defined by (f g)(x) := f (x)g(x);

1.4.2   Monotonicity
When both the domain and codomain of functions are ordered sets, we can talk about monotonicity
of a function.
    Suppose (X, ≥X ) and (Y, ≥Y ) are posets, then a function f : X → Y is

   • weakly increasing (or nondecreasing) if ∀x, x′ ∈ X, x ≥X x′ ⇒ f (x) ≥Y f (x′ );

   • weakly decreasing (or nonincreasing) if ∀x, x′ ∈ X, x ≥X x′ ⇒ f (x) ≤Y f (x′ );

   • strictly increasing if ∀x, x′ ∈ X, x >X x′ ⇒ f (x) >Y f (x′ );

   • strictly decreasing if ∀x, x′ ∈ X, x >X x′ ⇒ f (x) <Y f (x′ ).

A monotone function is a function that is either weakly increasing or weakly decreasing. A strictly
monotone function is either strictly increasing or strictly decreasing.

1.4.3   Correspondence
A correspondence F is a binary relation F from X to Y such that: ∀x ∈ X, ∃y ∈ Y , (x, y) ∈ F .
That is, a correspondence assigns not-necessarily single elements Y to every element in X; it is a
generalisation of a function by removing the uniqueness requirement. A correspondence F from X
to Y is denoted
                                           F : X ⇒ Y.
We will study correspondences in detail in ECON 6170.

1.5     Fields
The goal now is to formalise the idea of “numbers”. We want to allow for number system that allows
basic operations like addition and multiplication. We may define additions and multiplications as
binary operations, which are functions from X × X to X.
   A field is a 3-tuple (X, +, ·), where X is a set and +, · : X × X → X that satisfy the following
properties:

   • (Associativity) (x + y) + z = x + (y + z) and x · (y · z) = (x · y) · z ∀x, y, z ∈ X;

   • (Commutativity) x + y = y + x and x · y = y · x ∀x, y ∈ X;

   • (Distributivity) x · (y + z) = x · y + x · z ∀x, y, z ∈ X;

   • (Existence of identity elements) ∃!0, 1 ∈ X, x + 0 = 0 + x = x and x · 1 = 1 · x = x ∀x ∈ X;

   • (Existence of inverse elements) ∀x ∈ X, ∃!(−x) ∈ X, x + (−x) = (−x) + x = 0 and ∀x ∈
     X\{0}, ∃!x−1 . x · x−1 = x−1 · x = 1.

Given a field (X, +, ·), + is the addition operation and · is the multiplication operation. Given
a field (X, +, ·), we can define the subtraction operation as − : X × X → X such that x − y ≡
−(x, y) := x + (−y) and the division operation as ·· : X × X → X such that xy ≡ ·· (x, y) := x · y −1 .7
We often simply say that X is a field without specifying + and ·.
    Note that N is closed under the operations of addition and multiplication; i.e., the sum and
the product of any two natural numbers is a natural number. However, N is not closed under
subtraction and division (examples?). Z (unlike the natural numbers) is closed under subtraction,
but not division (check). Finally, the set of rational numbers, Q is closed under all four operations.
  7 Strictly speaking, · is not a binary operation since 1/0 is not defined in X.
                       ·




                                                       - 18 -
Math Review                                                                                        2024


Proposition 8. R and Q are fields while N and Z are not.

   An ordered field is a 4-tuple (X, +, ·, ≥) such that (X, +, ·) is a field and ≥ is a partial order on
X that is compatible with the operations + and · meaning

                                 x ≥ y ⇒ x + z ≥ y + z ∀x, y, z ∈ X,
                                  x · z ≥ y · z ∀x, y, z ∈ X : z ≥ 0.

Given an ordered field, we define

                         X+ := {x ∈ X : x ≥ 0} ,         X− := {x ∈ X : x ≤ 0} ,
                        X++ := {x ∈ X : x > 0} ,       X−− := {x ∈ X : x < 0} .

In particular, we write Rn+ to denote nonnegative real vectors with n ∈ N components, and Rn++
to denote the set of vectors with n ∈ N components whose individual components are all strictly
positive.
   Let (X, +, ·, ≥) be an ordered field. Define the absolute value function, | · | : X → X, as
                                                 (
                                                  x      if x ≥ 0,
                                        |x| :=
                                                  −x if x < 0.

The following proposition tells us that the Triangle Inequality is derived from the properties of an
ordered field.

Proposition 9 (Triangle Inequality). Let (X, +, ·, ≥) be an ordered field. Then,

                                    |x + y| ≤ |x| + |y| ∀x, y ∈ X.                                 (1.3)

Lemma 2. For any x, y, z ∈ X, (i) x + y = x + z ⇔ y = z; (ii) − (−x) = x; and (iii) − (x + y) =
−x + −y.

Proof. (i) For any w ∈ X, we have w = 0 + w = (−x + x) + w = −x + (x + w). Letting w = y, if
x + y = x + z, we obtain

                   y = −x + (x + y) = −x + (x + z) = (−x + x) + z = 0 + z = z.

For the converse, follow the steps in reverse.
   (ii)
                x = x + 0 = x + (−x − (−x)) = (x + −x) + − (−x) = 0 + − (−x) .
(iii) Since the additive inverse of x + y is unique, the claim follows since

                            (x + y) + (−x + −y) = (x + y) + (−y + −x)
                                                      = x + (y + (−y + −x))
                                                      = x + ((y + −y) + −x)
                                                      = x + (0 + −x)
                                                      = x + −x = 0.                                   ■

Proof of Proposition 9. We first show that (a) |x| ≥ x. If x ≥ 0, then |x| = x by definition. If
x < 0, then
                              |x| = −x ≡ 0 + −x ≥ x + −x ≡ 0 ≥ x.


                                                  - 19 -
Math Review                                                                                      2024


   Next, we show that (b) x ≥ 0 ⇒ −x ≤ 0 and x ≤ 0 ⇒ −x ≥ 0. Suppose x ≥ 0, then

                                      0 ≡ x + (−x) ≥ 0 + (−x) ≡ −x.

Suppose instead x ≤ 0, then

                               0 ≡ x + (−x) ≤ 0 + (−x) ≡ −x ⇔ −x ≥ 0.

Next, we show that (c) x ≥ −|x|. If x ≥ 0 so that x = |x|, then x ≥ 0 ≥ −x = −|x|, where the
second inequality follows from (b). If x < 0, then |x| = −x so that x = −(−x) = −|x|, where we
used (ii) from Lemma 2.
   We now show that (d) x ≥ y ⇒ −y ≥ −x .

                      x = x + 0 = x + (−y + y) ≥ y + (x + −x) = y + 0 = y
                                    ⇔ (x + y) − y ≥ (x + y) + −x = (y + x) + −x
                                              ⇔ −y ≥ −x,

where, in the last line, we used the (i) from Lemma 2.
   We are now ready to prove the triangle inequality. It suffices to show that

                               |x| + |y| ≥ x + y and |x| + |y| ≥ − (x + y) .

By applying (a) twice,
                                         |x| + |y| ≥ x + |y| ≥ x + y.
Apply (c) twice gives

                           x + y ≥ − |x| + y ≥ − |x| + − |y| = − (|x| + |y|) ,

where we used (iii) from Lemma 2 in the last equality. By (d) and (ii) from Lemma 2, we have

                   x + y ≥ − (|x| + |y|) ⇒ |x| + |y| = − (− (|x| + |y|)) ≥ − (x + y) .             ■

Corollary 1. Let (X, +, ·, ≥) be an ordered field. Then, for any n ∈ N,

                          |x1 + · · · + xn | ≤ |x1 | + · · · + |xn | ∀x1 , . . . , xn ∈ X.

Proof. The inequality trivially holds if n = 1. For n ≥ 2, we prove this by induction on n. For
n = 2, we have already shown that the inequality holds. We now make the inductive hypothesis
that the inequality holds some arbitrary n > 2. Our goal is to to show that the expression holds
for n + 1. Define zn := x1 + · · · + xn . By Proposition 9, since zn , xn+1 ∈ X,

                                        |zn + xn+1 | ≤ |zn | + |xn+1 | .

By the induction hypothesis, |zn | ≤ |x1 | + · · · + |xn | so that

                                |zn | + |xn+1 | ≤ |x1 | + · · · + |xn | + |xn+1 | .

Combining, we have that

                  |x1 + · · · + xn + xn+1 | = |zn + xn+1 | ≤ |x1 | + · · · + |xn | + |xn+1 | .

The desired result then follows by the Principle of Mathematical Induction.


                                                      - 20 -
Math Review                                                                                                2024


   We also have the following (the second is called the reverse triangle inequality).                         ■

Proposition 10. Let (X, +, ·, ≥) be an ordered field. Then, ∀x, y ∈ X, (i) |x · y| = |x| · |y|; and
(ii) |x − y| ≥ ||x| − |y||.

   We can define operations on correspondences too. For example, the sumset of two sets X and
Y is defined as
                              X + Y := {x + y : (x, y) ∈ X × Y } .

1.5.1    Real numbers, R
Recall the various sets of numbers:

                                                N ⊂ Z ⊂ Q ⊂ R ⊂ C,

where C is the set of complex numbers, R is the set of real numbers, Q is the set of rational numbers
(i.e., numbers that can be expressed as fractions), Z is the set of integers, and N is the set of natural
numbers.8 You may know that there is a sense in which Q has “holes” and the set of real number,
R, is obtained by filling in these holes. We will not attempt to construct numbers formally, and
only note that the crucial difference between Q and R is the following.

Axiom 1 (Completeness Axiom). Every nonempty subset S of R that is bounded from above (resp.
below) has a supremum (resp. infimum) in R.

   Given X ⊆ R, if X is not bounded above, we write sup X = ∞. If X is not bounded below, we
write inf X = −∞.
   We have already noted that, (R, ≥) is a total order and that (R, +, ·, ≥) is an ordered field.
Thus, above tells us that R is a complete ordered field.

Proposition 11 (The Archimedean Property). For any (x, y) ∈ R × R++ , there exists an n ∈ N
such that x < n · y.

Proof. By way of contradiction, suppose that the claim is false; i.e., ∃(x, y) ∈ R × R++ such that
x ≥ n·y for all n ∈ N. In other words, there must exist y ∈ R++ such that {n·y : n ∈ N} is bounded
from above (by x). But then s := sup{n · y : n ∈ N} would be a real number by the Completeness
Axiom. Since y > 0 and s is the least upper bound, it must be that s − y < s is not an upper
bound of {n · y : n ∈ N}; i.e., there exists an n∗ ∈ N such that s − y < n∗ · y ⇔ s < (n∗ + 1) · y,
which contradicts the choice of s as an upper bound.                                             ■

   We often use the Archimedean Property reformulated (check) as follows:

                                                                     1
                                               ∀ϵ > 0, ∃n ∈ N,         < ϵ.
                                                                     n
   We say that E ⊆ R is dense in R, if an element of E lies between any two real numbers. The
Archimedean Property is used to establish that rational numbers are dense in real numbers.

Corollary 2. For any x, y ∈ R such that x < y, there exists q ∈ Q such that x < q < y.

Proof. Fix x, y ∈ R such that y > x. By the Archimedean Property (Proposition 11)—setting
y = y − x > 0 and x = 1—there exists an n ∈ N such that n(y − x) > 1 ⇔ ny > nx + 1.
Let m := min{k ∈ Z : k > na}.9 By definition, na < m and na ≥ m − 1 (why?) and so
na < m ≤ 1 + na < nb. Letting q := m
                                   n and noting that q is rational completes the proof. ■
  8 I will use the convention that 0 ∈
                                     / N ≡ {1, 2, . . .}.
  9 One way to formally prove the existence of m is to prove that every nonempty subset of N that is bounded from

below has a minimum.

1.5.2   Extended real numbers
The extended real numbers is the set that contains R, −∞ and ∞, denoted

                                        R := R ∪ {−∞, ∞} .

We extend the total order ≥ of R to R by letting ∞ > −∞ and ∞ > x > −∞ for all x ∈ R.
Thus, (R, ≥) is a totally ordered set. The benefit from doing so is that every subset S in R has an
infimum and a supremum. We can extend the standard operations of addition and multiplication
to R: for any x ∈ R,

                 x + ∞ := ∞ =: ∞ + x,                                    ∞ + ∞ := ∞,
                  x − ∞ := −∞ =: −∞ + x,                    −∞ + −∞ := −∞,
                             (
                              ∞  if 0 < x ≤ ∞
                    x · ∞ :=                    =: ∞ · x,
                              −∞ if − ∞ ≤ x < 0
                             (
                              −∞ if 0 < x ≤ ∞
               x · (−∞) :=                      =: (−∞) · x.
                              ∞  if − ∞ ≤ x < 0

Note that +∞ and −∞ are not real numbers. Thus, statements on real numbers do not (automat-
ically) extend to them. Plausible facts like x + ∞ = ∞, (−∞) + (−∞) = −∞, etc. are true in R.
However, expressions like +∞ + (−∞), ∞ · 0, etc. are left undefined (just like 1/0 is undefined in
R). Consequently, R is not a field.

1.5.3   Intervals
For any x, y ∈ R, with x < y, we can define the following intervals:

                       (x, y) {z ∈ R : x < z < y} ,      (x, y] := (x, y) ∪ {y} ,
                        [x, y] := {z ∈ R : x ≤ z ≤ y} , [x, y) := {x} ∪ (x, y) .

We refer to (x, y) as an open interval, (x, y] and [x, y) as semi-open (or half-open) intervals, and
[x, y] as a closed interval. A set S ⊆ R is open if, for each x ∈ S, there exists r > 0 such that
(x − r, x + r) ⊆ S. We will generalise these concepts when we go over topology.
    Real intervals are bounded and of length given by the difference. An interval is nondegenerate
if y − x > 0. We assume that intervals are nondegenerate whenever we write (x, y), (x, y] or [x, y).
Unbounded intervals are defined as

                        (x, ∞) := {z ∈ R : z > x} , [x, ∞) := {x} ∪ (x, ∞) ,
                      (−∞, y) := {z ∈ R : z < y} ,(−∞, y] := (−∞, y) ∪ {y} .

Note that

                             sup (−∞, y) = sup (x, y) = sup (x, y] = y,
                               inf (x, ∞) = inf (x, y) = inf [x, y) = x.

The Completeness Axiom says that every nonempty subset S of R that fits in an interval of finite


                                                - 22 -
Math Review                                                                                                            2024


length has both an infimum and a supremum. Conversely, if S does not fit in any interval of the
form (−∞, b), then sup S does not exist and we write sup S = ∞. Similarly, if S does not fit in the
interval of the form (x, ∞), then inf S does not exist and we write inf S = −∞.
    As we will study more in ECON 6701, an interval that is both closed and bounded are compact.

1.5.4     Rn
Consider R2 and define the additive and multiplicative operator as

                                x + y ≡ + (x, y) := (x1 + y1 , x2 + y2 ) ,
                                   x · y ≡ · (x, y) := (x1 y1 − x2 y2 , x1 y2 + x2 y1 ) ,

we can show that (R2 , +, ·) is a field, with the additive identity (0, 0), the multiplicative identity
(1, 0), the additive inverse (of x) as (−x1 , −x2 ), and the multiplicative inverse (of x) ((x1 + x2 · x2 ·
x−1

      −1
         , −x2 x−1                 −1 −1 10
                1 (x1 + x2 · x2 · x1 )  ). Note that this allows complex numbers, C, to be a field.
    However, it turns out that generalisation of the example above is not possible. We will simply
state the following result.

Proposition 12. Rn cannot be a field for any n > 2 with n ∈ N.

    This fact motivates us to generalise fields to linear spaces so that we can deal with Rn .

1.6     Complex numbers
While it is somewhat rare, complex numbers do pop up in economics such as when studying the
stability of dynamic systems in macroeconomics and in econometrics. Let us go over complex
numbers briefly.
    Consider the equation x2 + 1 = 0 which has no solutions in real numbers. We know that a
quadratic equations generally have 0, 1 or 2 solutions. What complex numbers allows us to do
(among other things!) is to think that quadratic equations always have two solutions. We do so
by extending the of real numbers. Let i denote the solution to i2 = −1. With this notation, it is
clear that the equation x2 + 1 = 0 has exactly two solutions, x = i and x = −i. More generally, a
complex number is a number of the form a + ib, for any a, b ∈ R. The set of all complex numbers
if denoted C.
    Two complex numbers are added or multiplied term by term using the usual rules of algebra,
remembering that i2 = −1.

Example 6. Let z := a + bi and w := c + di. Then,

                                z + w = (a + c) + (b + d) i,
                                  −w = −c − di
                                z − w = (a − c) + (b − d) i
                                   zw = (a + bi) (c + di) = ac + adi + bci + bdi2
                                        = (ac − bd) + (adi + bc) ,
  10 The multiplicative operator cannot be defined as a dot product operator (i.e., x · y ̸= x y + x y ) since the

result would be in R and not R2 (recall multiplicative operator is a function from R2 × R2 to R2 ). Similarly,
x · y = (x1 · y1 , x2 · y2 ) is not a valid operator since (0, 1) would not have a multiplicative inverse. To see this, first
observe that the multiplicative identity must be (1, 1). Then, for (y1 , y2 ) to be the multiplicative inverse of (0, 1), it
implies a contradiction since
                                            (1, 1) = (0, 1) · (y1 , y2 ) = (0, y2 ) ⇒ 1 = 0.

and finally
                                         z = w ⇔ (a = c) ∧ (b = d) .

    Every complex number z = a + bi can be represented by a point (a, b) in the (x, y) plane called
complex plane, where the x-axis corresponds to the real part (a) and y-axis corresponds to the
imaginary part (b). The point (a, b) can be expressed in polar coordinates; i.e., by a pair ⟨r, θ⟩,
where r is the distance from the origin to the point (a, b) and θ is the angle, measured in radians,
relative to the x-axis. Recalling some trigonometry gives that

                                          a = r cos θ and b = r sin θ.

                                         sin x = x −    +     − ··· .
                                                     3!    5!
For z ∈ C, define
                                                             z2
                                            ez := 1 + z +       + ··· .
                                                             2!
If we now write z = iθ (where θ ∈ R), then
                                                       2
                                                  (iθ)
                                 eiθ = 1 + iθ +        + · · · = cos θ + i sin θ,
                                                    2!
which is known as the Euler’s formula. In this way, any complex number z = r(cos θ + sin θ) can be
written as z = reiθ which allows us to use the usual laws of algebra to obtain the rules of algebra
above (with polar coordinates).

2     Structures on spaces
A space is another word for a set, which itself is just a collection of objects. Here, we will introduce
various structures we can place on sets. We will first introduce the concept of a linear structure and
study linear spaces (sometimes called vector spaces). We also briefly introduce a metric structure
to introduce distance between objects into a space (not necessarily linear). We then explore further
structure that we can place on linear spaces: a norm structure, which gives us the concept of size of
objectives, and an inner product structure, which gives us the concept of angles. We will find that
every inner product space is a normed space, which, in turn, is a metric space. Note that a metric
space need not be linear while normed and an inner product spaces are defined on a linear spaces.
Finally, we introduce topology, which gives us a way to think about neighbourhoods of elements of
the space.

2.1    Linear space
We have already seen that Rn can be endowed with an order structure so that any two elements can
be meaningfully compared. We also saw that R has a “linear” (i.e., algebraic) structure meaning
that we can add and multiply any two elements.11 However, we saw that Rn is not a field in
general which makes us wonder whether Rn has a linear structure. We will see here that Rn is also
endowed with a linear structure in the sense that we can “add” and “scale” any two elements. We
will formalise the “linearity” using the concept of a linear space (of which Rn would be an example).
    A linear space (or a vector space) over a field F is a 4-tuple (V, (F, +F , ·F ), +, ·), where V is the
set of elements called vectors, (F, +F , ·F ) is a field, a binary operation + : V × V → V called vector
addition, and a binary operation · : F × V → V called scalar multiplication such that

    • (Closure) x + y ∈ V ∀x, y ∈ V and λ · x ∈ V ∀x ∈ V ∀λ ∈ F ;

    • (Associativity) (x + y) + z = x + (y + z) ∀x, y, z ∈ V , and (λ ·F β) · x = λ · (β · x) ∀λ, β ∈ F
      ∀x ∈ V ;

    • (Commutativity) x + y = y + x ∀x, y ∈ V ;

    • (Distributivity) λ · (y + z) = λ · y + λ · z ∀x, y ∈ V ∀λ ∈ F , and (λ +F β) · x = λ · x + β · x
      ∀λ, β ∈ F ∀x ∈ V ;

    • (Existence of identity) ∃!0 ∈ V , x + 0 = 0 + x = x and ∃1 ∈ F , 1 · x = x ∀x ∈ V ;

    • (Existence of additive inverse) ∀x ∈ V , ∃!(−x) ∈ V , x + (−x) = 0.

We express elements of linear space V in bold,12 and say that V is a linear space without specifying
F , +F and ·F . We also often do not distinguish between +F and + or ·F and ·. We refer to 0 as
the origin or the zero vector, and refer to any x ∈ V \{0} as a nonzero vector.
    We can define vector subtraction as − : V × V → V such that x − y ≡ −(x, y) := x + (−y) and
scalar division as ·· : F × V → V such that xλ ≡ ·· (λ, x) := λ−1 · x. Note also that: (i) 0 is unique
(why?); (ii) (−x) is unique for all x ∈ V ; (iii) −x = (−1) · x ∀x ∈ V ; and (iv) 0 · x = 0 ∀x ∈ V and
λ · 0 = 0 ∀λ ∈ F .

Example 7. A trivial example of a linear space is the set {0}, which is a single set consisting of
the origin. Any linear space that contains more than one vector is nontrivial.
 11 By linear, we mean that we can add and scale elements.
 12 You might see ⃗
                  x instead of x.




                                                    - 25 -
Math Review                                                                                              2024


Example 8. A familiar example of a linear space is the Euclidean space, (Rn , +, ·), where the
vector addition and scalar multiplication are defined in a component-by-component manner. i.e.,
∀x, y ∈ Rn , λ ∈ R ,

                     (x1 , . . . , xn ) + (y1 , . . . , yn ) ≡ x + y := (x1 + y1 , . . . , xn + yn ) ,
                                     λ · (x1 , . . . , xn ) ≡ λ · x := (λ · x1 , . . . , λ · xn ) .

However, note that Rn++ is not a linear space (does not contain the origin) nor is Rn+ a linear space
(since, under the usual operations, it does not contain additive inverse for nonzero vectors).

Example 9. Given a nonempty set X, and the set of all real-valued functions on X,
                                                    
                                                V := f : f ∈ RX ,

is a linear space with

                                           (f + g) (x) := f (x) + g (x) ,
                                              (λf ) (x) := λ · f (x) ,
                                                (0) (x) := 0,
                                              (−f ) (x) := −f (x) .

We will see that sequences and and matrices are both real-valued functions, it follows that the set
of all sequences and the set of all matrices are both linear spaces.

    Given a linear space (V, (F, +F , ·F ), +, ·), a (resp. proper ) subset S of V is a (resp. proper )
linear subspace of (V, (F, +F , ·F ), +, ·) if (S, (F, +F , ·F ), +|S , ·|S ) is a linear space. For an example
of S ⊆ V that is not a linear subspace, let V = Rn and S be any subset such that 0 ∈                  / S. The
following gives us a quick way to check whether a subspace is linear.

Proposition 13. Let (V, (F, +F , ·F ), +, ·) be a linear space and S be a nonempty (resp. proper)
subset of V . Then, (S, (F, +F , ·F ), +|S , ·|S ) is a (resp. proper) linear subspace of V if and only if
α · x + β · y ∈ S ∀α, β ∈ F ∀x, y ∈ S.

   From the proposition above, it is immediate that [0, 1] is not a linear subspace of R (why?)
whereas {x ∈ R2 : x1 + x2 = 0} is a linear subspace of R2 .
   Given Y and Z that are subspaces of a linear space (V, (F, +F , ·F ), +, ·), the sum space of Y
and Z, denoted Y + Z, is given by

                                       Y + Z := {y + z : y ∈ Y, z ∈ Z} .

Further, if Y ∩ Z = {0}, then the sum space is called a direct sum of Y and Z and is denoted
Y ⊕ Z. Any x ∈ Y ⊕ Z has a unique representation as x = y + z where y ∈ Y and z ∈ Z.

Proposition 14. Intersections and sum space of linear subspaces are a linear subspaces.

Exercise 11. Show that a union of linear subspaces need not be a linear subspace.

   From now on, unless otherwise specified, assume if F = R, +R and ·R are the usual addition and
multiplication operators on R. Whenever the underlying field of a linear space is R (i.e., a linear
space is over R), we will simply write (V, +, ·).

Math Review                                                                                                  2024


2.1.1    Linear, affine and convex combinations
Given a linear space (V, +, ·) and (xi ) ∈ V ∞ , define, ∀n ∈ N,

                               n
                               X                               n
                                                               X             n−k+1
                                                                              X
                                     xi := x1 + · · · + xn ,         xi :=           xi+k−1 .
                               i=1                             i=k            i=1

Given a linear space (V, +, ·) and vectors x1 , . . . , xn ∈ V for some n ∈ N, a linear combination of
x1 , . . . , xn is a vector λ1 · x1 + · · · + λn · xn for some coefficients of linear combination λ1 , . . . , λn ∈
R. An affine combination of x1 , . . . , xn is a linear combination of x1 , . . . , xn for some coefficients
                                                                      Pn
λ1 , . . . , λn ∈ R (i.e., weights can be negative) such that i=1 λi = 1. A convex combination of
x1 , . . . , xn is a linear combination of x1 , . . . , xn for some coefficients λ1 , . . . , λn ∈ R+ (weights must
                                  Pn
be nonnegative) such that i=1 λi = 1.

2.1.2    Span, affine, and convex hull
Let (V, +, ·) be a linear space. Fix a nonempty subset S of V .
    The span of S, denoted span(S), is the set of all linear combinations of (finitely many) elements
of S; i.e.,13
                            ( n                                                           )
                              X
                span (S) :=       λi xi : n ∈ N and (xi , λi ) ∈ S × R ∀i ∈ {1, . . . , n} .
                                  i=1

Note that span(∅) = span({0}) = {0}, span(V ) = V and span({x}) = {λ · x : λ ∈ R}. Say that a
set S spans a set E if E = span(S). It follows from Proposition 13 that span(S) is a linear subspace
(why?) and we refer to it as the linear subspace spanned (or generated ) by S.
    The affine hull of S, denoted aff(S), is the set of all affine combinations of (finitely many)
elements of S; i.e.,
                  ( n                                                                 n
                                                                                              )
                     X                                                                X
        aff(S) :=       λi xi : n ∈ N and (xi , λi ) ∈ S × R ∀i ∈ {1, . . . , n} with   λi = 1 .
                      i=1                                                                        i=1

Note aff(∅) = {0}. Note that aff(S) need not be a linear subspace. However, for all x ∈ S,

                                aff (S) = span (S − {x}) + {x}
                                         ≡ {y + x : y ∈ span ({s − x : s ∈ S})} .

That is, if we translate vectors in S by a vector −x ∈ S, then its span is equal to the affine hull
of S translated by −x. Since span(S − {x}) is a linear subspace, we also realise that aff(S) is a
translation away from being a linear subspace.
   The convex hull of S, denoted co(S), is the set of all convex combinations of (finitely many)
elements of S; i.e.,
                 ( n                                                                 n

                     i=1                                                                         i=1

2.1.3   Convex sets
A subset S of a linear space (V, +, ·) is convex if it contains all convex combinations of pairs in S;
i.e.,
                         S ⊆ V convex ⇔ λS + (1 − λ) S = S ∀λ ∈ [0, 1] .
Here are some properties of convex sets.

Proposition 15. Suppose (Sα )α∈A is a collection of convex sets in a linear space (V, +, ·). Then,
T
  α∈A Sα is convex.
                              T
Proof. Take any s1 , s2 ∈ S := α∈A Sα so that s1 , s2 ∈ Sα for all α ∈ A. Since each Sα is convex,
any convex combination of s1 and s2 are also in each Sα and are also in S.                      ■

Exercise 12. Suppose S1 and S2 are both convex subsets in a linear space (V, +, ·). Then, S1 + S2
is convex.

Proposition 16. Let S ⊆ X be a subset in a linear space (V, +, ·). S is convex if and only if it
contains all convex combinations of elements in S.

Proof. We prove this by induction on n. For n = 1, then since s = 1 · s ∈ S for all s ∈ S, convex
combination of n = 1 element is contained in S. Make the induction hypothesis that S contains
convex combinations of n elements. We wish to show that S also contains convex combinations of
n + 1 elements. The Principle of Mathematical Induction will take care of the rest.

2.1.4   Algebraic and relative interior
Consider the interval (0, 1) in R and a point x ∈ (0, 1). Observe that some part of the line segment
between x and any other point in R (excluding the endpoint x) is contained (0, 1); i.e., for any
x ∈ (0, 1) and any y ∈ R, there exists z ∈ co({x, y})\{x} such that co({x, z}) ⊆ (0, 1). In this
sense, all points of (0, 1) are in the “interior” of (0, 1). However, we could not do the same for
(0, 1]—the line segment between x = 1 and z = 2, say, intersects (0, 1] at only at the end point 1.
    To formalise this idea, let S be a subset of a linear space (V, +, ·). An element x ∈ S is an
algebraic interior point of S (in V ) if, for any y ∈ V , there exists an αy > 0 such that

                                 (1 − α) x + αy ∈ S ∀0 ≤ α ≤ αy .


                                                   - 28 -
Math Review                                                                                           2024


The set of all algebraic interior points of S in V is called the algebraic interior of S (in V ) and
is denoted al-intV (S). If S ⊆ al-intV (S), then we say that S is algebraically open in V . If x ∈
al-intV (S), then one can move linearly from x towards any direction in the linear space V without
leaving the set S immediately.
    The natural “mother space” of a convex set is its affine hull. In particular, if S is a subset of
a linear space V with 0 ∈ S, then we do not need vectors in V \span(S). But nothing changes
if we drop the assumption that 0 ∈ S except that we should now consider aff(S) (why?). Thus,
the algebraic interior of a subset S of a linear space relative to its affine hull, aff(S), is of primary
importance in convex analysis.
    An element x ∈ S is an relative interior point of S if, for any y ∈ aff(S), there exists an αy > 0
such that
                                  (1 − α) x + αy ∈ S ∀0 ≤ α ≤ αy .
The set of all relative interior points of S in V is called the relative interior of S (in V ) and is
denoted ri(S). If S ⊆ ri(S), then we say that S is relatively open. If x ∈ ri(S), then one can move
from x towards any direction in aff(S) without leaving the set S immediately.
   Note that                                  (
                                               ri (S) if aff (S) = V
                                al-intV (S) =                         .
                                               ∅       if aff (S) ⊂ V
The first case is obvious (is it?) and so we focus on the latter case. So suppose aff(S) ⊂ V and
take any x∗ ∈ S and let Y := span(S − {x∗ }) = aff(S) − {x∗ }. That aff(S) is a proper subset of V
means that Y is a proper linear subspace of V . Thus, we can find w ∈ V \Y and notice that

                                (1 − α) z + αw ∈
                                               / Y ∀z ∈ Y ∀α ∈ (0, 1];

otherwise, we would contradict w ∈
                                 / Y . But then

              (1 − α) (z + x∗ ) + α (w + x∗ ) ∈
                                              / Y + {x∗ } = aff (S) ∀z ∈ Y ∀α ∈ (0, 1].

Letting y = w + x∗ , therefore, we find (1 − α)x + αy ∈
                                                      / Y for any x ∈ S and any 0 < α ≤ 1; i.e.,
al-intV (S) = ∅.

2.1.5   Ray, half lines, cones, and conical hull
A subset of a linear space (V, +, ·) is a ray if it is a set of nonnegative multiples of some vector; i.e.,
{αx : α ∈ R+ ) for some x ∈ V . A half line is a translation of a ray; i.e., {αx : α ∈ R+ } + {x0 } for
some x, x0 ∈ V . A cone is a set that contains all of its rays; i.e.,

                               S ⊆ V cone ⇔ αx ∈ S ∀α ∈ R+ ∀x ∈ S.

A set S ⊆ V is convex cone if it is both convex and cone.

Proposition 17. A convex cone contains all nonnegative (finite) linear combinations of its ele-
ments.

Proof. Let S be a convex cone in a linear space (V, +, ·). Take an arbitrary nonnegative linear
combinations of elements in S,

2.1.6   Linear dependence and independence
Fix a linear space (V, +, ·). A subset S ⊆ V is linearly dependent in V if it either equals {0} or
at least one of the vectors, say x ∈ S, can be expressed as a linear combination of finitely many
vectors in S\{x}. For any n ∈ N, any distinct vectors x1 , . . . , xn ∈ V are linearly dependent if
{x1 , . . . , xn } is linearly dependent in V . The following is immediate from the definition of span.

Proposition 18. Given a linear space (V, +, ·), a subset S of V \{0} is linearly dependent in V if
and only if there exists an x ∈ S such that x ∈ span(S\{x}).

    A subset S ⊆ V is linearly independent in V if no finite subset of it is linearly dependent in
V . For any n ∈ N, the vectors x1 , . . . , xn ∈ V are linearly independent if {x1 , . . . , xn } is linearly
independent in V .

Proposition 19. Given a linear space (V, +, ·), for any n ∈ N, vectors x1 , . . . , xn ∈ V are linearly
independent if and only if ∀(λi )ni=1 ∈ Rn ,

                                    i=1

    The following fundamental result about linear dependency is that there cannot be more than n
linearly independent vectors in a linear space spanned by n vectors.

Proposition 20. Suppose (V, +, ·) is a linear space and X, Y ⊆ V . If Y is linearly independent in

2.1.7     Basis
We want to think of a minimum set of vectors that allows us to construct a given linear space. We
call such a set a basis. Formally, a (Hamel) basis of a linear space (V, +, ·) is the smallest subset of
V that spans V ; i.e., S is a basis of (V, +, ·) if (i) V = span(S); and (ii) V = span(T ) implies T ⊂ S
is false. The following proposition allows us to replace condition (ii) with linear independence, and
this is the definition you often find.

Proposition 21. A subset S of a linear space (V, +, ·) is a basis for V if and only if S is linearly
independent and V = span(S).

Proof. (Sufficiency) Suppose that S ≡ {s1 , . . . , sn } is linearly independent and V = span(S). To
show that S is a basis for V , it suffices to show that for any T ≡ {t1 , . . . , tm } such that V = span(T ),
we have that ¬(T ⊂ S). Toward a contradiction, suppose that ti ∈ S for all i = {1, . . . , m} and
suppose for some sj ∈     / T —without loss, we may assume that s1 ∈          / T . These imply that every
ti equals some sj ̸= s1 ; i.e., there exists f : {1, . . . , m} → {2, . . . , n} such that ti = sf (i) for all
i ∈ {1, . . . , m}. Since span(S) = V = span(T ), s1 ∈ span(T ); i.e., there exists nonzero (γi )ni=1 ∈ Rn
such that
                                               Xm         X m
                                         s1 =     γi ti =     γi sf (i) .
                                                  i=1            i=1

But this implies that s1 is a linear combination of {s2 , . . . , sn } = S\{s1 }, which contradicts the fact
that S is linearly independent.
   (Necessity) Suppose that S is a basis for V . Then, by definition, V = span(S) and so it
remains to show that S is linearly independent. Toward a contradiction, suppose that S is linearly
dependent; i.e., there exists x ∈ S such that x ∈ span(S\{x}). But this implies that span(S\{x}) =
span(S) = V and we also have S\{x} ⊂ S; the latter contradicts condition (ii) in the definition of
basis.                                                                                                    ■

    Since V = span(S), any x ∈ V can be expressed as a linear combination of vectors in S :=
{s1 , . . . , sn }. That is,
                                                              n
                                                              X
                             ∀x ∈ V, ∃(λi (x))ni=1 ∈ Rn , x =   λi (x)si .
                                                                        i=1

We think of (x)S := (λi (x))ni=1 as the coordinate of the vector x relative to the basis S. Moreover,
the fact that S is linearly independent means, by Corollary 3, that coordinate of each x ∈ V is
unique.
    For any n ∈ N and i ∈ {1, . . . , n}, the ith unit vector in Rn , denoted by, ei , is the n-vector
whose ith term equals 1 and whose all other terms equal 0. Since S = {ei : i ∈ {1, . . . , n}} is
linearly independent and span(S) = Rn , it is a basis, and in fact, we refer to it as the canonical
basis of the linear space (Rn , +, ·). However, basis of Rn is not unique, and, in fact, any linearly
independent set of vectors of cardinality n is a basis of Rn . Nevertheless, the following result tells



                                                        - 32 -
Math Review                                                                                         2024


us that any basis has the same number of elements; i.e., coordinate of the vector any x ∈ V has
the same number of elements.

Exercise 13. Any two bases of a finite-dimensional linear space have the same number of elements.

    We define the rank of S ⊆ V , denoted rank(S), as the cardinality of any one if its basis. Rank
of the whole linear space is the dimension, denoted dim(V ) := rank(V ). We say that a linear space
(V, +, ·) is dim(V ) dimensional. If dim(V ) is not finite, we refer to V as being infinite dimensional
and write dim(X) = ∞. The trivial linear space {0} has a dimension of 0.

Theorem 1. Every linear space has a basis.

Proof. When the linear space has a finite dimension, the result follows from the definition. The
proof is a little more complicated when the linear space is infinite dimensional and relies on Zorn’s
lemma (Lemma 1). Let (*) denote the property of a set that any finite subset of itself is linearly
independent. Define a partial orderer (P, ⊇) as
                                        
                                    P := S ∈ 2V : S satisfies (*) .

To be able to apply Zorn’s lemma, we show that every totally ordered subset of P has an upper
                                                                       S
bound (in P ). Fix any T ⊆ P that is a totally ordered set. Define M := S∈T S. Then, M is an
upper bound of T since
                                        M ⊇ B ∀B ∈ T.
It remains to show that M ∈ P ; i.e., we must verify that M satisfies (*). Take any m1 , . . . , mn ∈ M
for some n ∈ N. By definition of M , there exists B1 , . . . , Bn ∈ T such that

                                        mi ∈ Bi ∀i ∈ {1, . . . , n} .

Since T is a totally ordered set, there exists Bk for some k ∈ {1, . . . , n} such that

                             Bk ⊇ Bi ∀i ∈ {1, . . . , n} ⇒ m1 , . . . , mn ∈ Bk .

Moreover, since Bk ∈ T ⊆ P , Bk satisfies (*). Thus, m1 , . . . , mn are linearly independent. Since
m1 , . . . , mn was arbitrary, it follows that M ∈ P .
    By Zorn’s lemma, we may conclude that there exists S ∈ P that is maximal and, by construction,
S satisfies (*). To show that S is a basis, it suffices to show that any vector in V can be written as
a finite linear combination of vectors in S. Fix any x ∈ V and let S̃ = S ∪ {x}.

   • Case 1: x ∈ S. Then, x is a linear combination of elements of S trivially and we are done.

   • Case 2: x ∈ V \S. Then, S ⊂ S̃, which implies that S̃ ∈   / P by the maximality of S. Thus,
     there exists a finite subset of S that is not linearly independent. By Proposition 19, this
     means that there exists s1 . . . , sn ∈ S̃ such that
                                                   n
                                                   X
                                                         λi si = 0                                 (2.1)
                                                   i=1

      for some (λi )ni=1 ∈ Rn with λj ̸= 0 for some j ∈ {1, . . . , n}.

         • Subcase a: s1 . . . , sn ∈ S. This is not possible since S satisfies (*) so that (2.1) implies
           λ1 = · · · = λn = 0 by Proposition 19.
         • Subcase b: for some sj , x = sj ∈

Note that a Hamel basis need not be countable.                                                            ■

Corollary 4. Let (V, +, ·) be a finite-dimensional linear space and let (S, +|S , ·|S ) be its linear
subspace. Then,
                                         dimS ≤ dimV
and the inequality is strict if S is a proper subspace.

Proof. Suppose S ⊆ V . By Theorem 1, V and S have bases and denote them, respectively, by BV
and BS . Since BS is linearly independent by Proposition 21 and every s ∈ S can be expressed as
a linear combination of BV , we have BS ⊆ span(BV ). Then, by Proposition 20,

                                   dim (S) = |BS | ≤ |BV | = dim (V ) .

Now suppose that S ⊂ V . Thus, there exists x ∈ V such that x ∈ / S. Letting BS denote a basis
for S. The claim is that BS ∪ {x} is linearly independent. Suppose not, then x must be a linear
combination of vectors in BS , which implies that x ∈ span(BS ) = S, contradicting that x ∈/ S.
Hence, dim(S) = |BS | ≤ |BV | = dim(V ) in this case.                                        ■

Proposition 22. Suppose (V, +, ·) is a linear space with dimV < ∞. Then, any linearly independ-
ent set W ⊆ V with |W | = dimV is a basis of V .

basis of V which exists by Theorem 1. Let W := {w1 , . . . , wn } ⊆ V be a linearly independent set.

proof of Proposition 20. Since w1 ∈ span(S) = V , there exists nonzero (λ1i )ni=1 ∈ Rn such that

   From now on, I will generally refer to a linear space (V, +, ·) as V .

2.1.8   Linear transformations
In a linear space, we talk about linear combination of vectors. An important class of functions
are ones that maps from one linear space into another linear space in a way that preserves linear
combinations. We refer to them as linear transformations (or linear operators or linear maps). A
real-valued linear transformation is a linear functional on V .
    Formally, let (V, +V , ·V ) and W = (W, +W , ·W ) be two linear spaces. A function L : V → W is
a linear transformation if

                     L (λ ·V x +V y) = λ ·W L (x) +W L (y) ∀x, y ∈ V ∀λ ∈ R.                        (2.2)

For brevity, we will not be explicit about the sets that + and · operators relate. The set of all
linear transformations from a linear space V to a linear space W is denoted L(V, W ). A linear
maps preserves the origin since

                                L (0V ) = L (0 ·V v) = 0 ·W L (v) = 0W ,

where 0V and 0W are the zeros in linear spaces V and W respectively.

Exercise 14. Show that L : V → W is a linear transformation if and only if, for any x, y ∈ V and
λ ∈ R,
                      L (x + y) = L (x) + L (y) and L (λx) = λL (x) .

Example 10. Let V = W = R2 and f : V → W .

  (i) f (x1 , x2 ) := (x1 + 2x2 , x2 − x1 ) is a linear transformation (check).

 (ii) f (x1 , x2 ) := (2, −1) is not a linear transformation because (0, 0) is mapped to (2, −1).

(iii) f (x1 , x2 ) := (0, 0) is a linear transformation (check).




                                                    - 35 -
Math Review                                                                                                    2024


(iv) f (x1 , x2 ) := (x21 , x2 ) is not a linear transformation because

                 f (λ (x1 , x2 ) + (y1 , y2 )) = f (λx1 + y1 , λx2 + y2 )
                                                                                  
                                              = λ2 x21 + y12 + 2λx1 y1 , λx2 + y2
                                                                       
                                              ̸= λx21 + y12 , λx2 + y2
                                                                     
                                               = λ x21 , x2 + y12 , y2 = λf (x1 , x2 ) + f (y1 , y2 ) .

Example 11. Let V be a linear space of all functions with continuous first derivatives defined on
R (why is this a linear space?), and W the linear space of all real-valued functions defined on R.
Let D : V → W be the function that maps a function f ∈ V into its derivative; i.e.,

                                                  D (f ) := f ′ .

You should know that

                          D (f + g) = D (f ) + D (g) and D (λf ) = λD (f ) .

Hence, D is a linear transformation.

                                                 Z x
