   The image of L ∈ L(V, W ) is given by

                                 im (L) := {y ∈ W : ∃x ∈ V, L (x) = y} .

As usual, it is the values in the codomain that the linear transformation L can take values in. The
null space (or the kernel ) is the subset of the domain that is mapped to the zero element in the
codomain:
                               null (L) ≡ ker (L) := {x ∈ V : L (x) = 0} .
The null space is a linear subspace of V since L(λx + y) = λL(x) + L(y) = 0 so that λx + y ∈ S
for all λ ∈ R and all x, y ∈ S.

Exercise 15. Consider f : R3 → R2 defined as f (x1 , x2 , x3 ) := (x1 + x2 , x3 ). Find its image and
null space.

   The rank of L is given by
                                         rank (L) := dim (im (L)) .



                                                       - 36 -
Math Review                                                                                       2024


The nullity of L is the rank of the null space; i.e.,

                                        nullity (L) := dim (null (L))

Proposition 23. Let V and W be linear spaces. Then, L ∈ L(V, W ) is injective if and only if
null(L) = {0V }.

Proof. Suppose that L ∈ L(V, W ) is injective; i.e., every element x ∈ V is mapped to a unique
element in W . As noted above, linear maps preserves the origin so L(0V ) = 0W . Thus, null(L) =
{0V }.
   Conversely, suppose that null(L) = {0V }. Suppose that there exist x, y ∈ V such that L(x) =
L(y). Since L is linear, L(x) = L(y) implies that

                                     L (x − y) = L (x) − L (y) = 0W .

Since null(L) = {0V }, it must be that x − y = 0 ⇔ x = y.                                            ■

Theorem 2 (Rank-nullity theorem). Let V and W be two finite-dimensional linear space and
L ∈ L(V, W ). Then,
                             dimV = nullity (L) + rank (L) .

Corollary 5. Suppose dimV = dimW . Then, L ∈ L(V, W ) is surjective if and only if L is injective.
In particular, null(L) = {0V } if and only if L is bijective.

Proof. Suppose n = dimV = dimW . By Proposition 23 and the definition of nullity, L is injective
if and only if nullity(L) = 0. Then, by the Rank-nullity theorem (Theorem 2), L is injective if
and only if n = dimV = rank(L) ≡ dim(im(L)). That is, the image of L is a n-dimensional
subspace of the n-dimensional linear space W . But the only full-dimensional subspace of a finite-
dimensional linear space is itself and so this happens if and only if the image is all of W (i.e., when
im(L) ≡ L(V ) = W ). Thus, for any w ∈ W , there must exist x ∈ V such that L(x) = w; i.e., L is
surjective.                                                                                          ■

   A linear transformation L ∈ L(V, W ) is invertible if there exists L−1 : W → V such hat

                                         L−1 (L (x)) = x ∀x ∈ V,
                                                   
                                        L L−1 (w) = w ∀w ∈ W ;

i.e., L is invertible if it is surjective and injective.

Exercise 16. Let V and W be two linear spaces. If L ∈ L(V, W ) is invertible, then L−1 ∈ L(W, V );
i.e., L−1 is linear.

2.1.9    Isomorphism
Two finite-dimensional linear spaces (V, +V , ·V ) and W = (W, +W , ·W ) are isomorphic if there is
an invertible linear transformation L ∈ L(V, W ) called isomorphism.
    Isomorphic linear spaces are essentially indistinguishable. We can think of them as relabelling
of each other because L being invertible means that L is surjective and injective; i.e., every element
of V is mapped to a unique element in W , and every element of W is mapped to a unique element
in V .

Proposition 24. Two finite-dimensional linear spaces (V, +V , ·V ) and W = (W, +W , ·W ) are iso-
morphic if and only if dimV = dimW .


                                                    - 37 -
Math Review                                                                                       2024


   The following tells us that any finite-dimensional linear space over R is essentially indistinguish-
able from Rn .

Theorem 3. Every n-dimensional linear space (V, (R, +, ·)+V , ·V ) is isomorphic to Rn .

Proof. Let V be an n-dimensional linear space over R and let S = {s1 , . . . , sn } denote a basis of
V . Recalling that S is linearly independent (Proposition 22), by Corollary 3, for each x ∈ V , there
                                           Pn
exists unique (λi )ni=1 ∈ Rn such that x = i=1 λi si . Define L : V → Rn by
                                            n
                                                          !
                                            X
                               L (x) = L          λi si       := (λ1 , . . . , λn ) .
                                            i=1

Since (λi )ni=1 is unique for each x ∈ V , L is injective. To show that L is surjective, take any
                                                Pn
(αi )ni=1 ∈ Rn , then since V is a linear space, i=1 αi si ∈ V .                                ■

2.1.10   Dual spaces
Let (V, +, ·) be a linear space (over the field R). The dual (linear) space of V is given by
(L(V, R), +, ·); i.e., the collection of all linear functionals on V along with addition and multi-
plication operator in the field R. The dual space of V is often denoted V ∗ . When applied to vector
spaces of functions (which are typically infinite-dimensional), dual spaces are used to describe meas-
ures and distributions. Consequently, the dual space is an important concept in (some parts of)
economics.

2.1.11   Convex, concave, quasiconcave and quasiconvex functions
Let (V, +, ·) be a linear space (over the field R). Given a convex X ⊆ V , a function f : X → R is

   • concave if f (αx + (1 − α)y) ≥ αf (x) + (1 − α)f (y) for any x, y ∈ X and α ∈ [0, 1].

   • convex if f (αx + (1 − α)y) ≤ αf (x) + (1 − α)f (y) for any x, y ∈ X and α ∈ [0, 1].

   • strictly concave if f (αx + (1 − α)y) > αf (x) + (1 − α)f (y) for any two distinct x, y ∈ X and
     α ∈ (0, 1).

   • strictly convex if f (αx + (1 − α)y) < M αf (x) + (1 − α)f (y) for any two distinct x, y ∈ X
     and α ∈ (0, 1).

   • quasi-concave if f (αx + (1 − α)y) ≥ min{f (x), f (y)} for any x, y ∈ X and α ∈ [0, 1].

   • quasi-convex if f (αx + (1 − α)y) ≤ max{f (x), f (y)} for any x, y ∈ X and α ∈ [0, 1].

   • strictly quasi-concave if f (αx + (1 − α)y) > min{f (x), f (y)} for all distinct x, y ∈ X and
     α ∈ (0, 1).

   • strictly quasi-convex if f (αx + (1 − α)y) < max{f (x), f (y)} for all distinct x, y ∈ X and
     α ∈ (0, 1).

If f is concave and convex, then f is affine. Concavity implies quasiconcavity, and convexity
implies quasiconvexity. Sums of concave functions are concave and sums of convex functions are
convex; however, sums of quasiconcave (resp. quasiconvex) functions need not be quasiconcave
(resp. quasiconvex).




                                                  - 38 -
Math Review                                                                                      2024


2.2    Metric spaces
A vector x ∈ V represents a point in the linear space V . Given two vectors x, y ∈ V , we can define
a notion of distance, called a metric, between these two points. A metric on an nonempty set X is
a function ρ : X 2 → R that satisfies the following conditions:

   • (nonnegativity) ρ(x, y) ≥ 0 ∀x, y ∈ X;

   • (identity of indiscernibles) ρ(x, y) = 0 ⇔ x = y;

   • (symmetry) ρ(x, y) = ρ(y, x) ∀x, y ∈ X;

   • (triangle inequality) ρ(x, y) ≤ ρ(x, z) + ρ(y, z) ∀x, y, z ∈ X.

A metric space is a pair (X, ρ), where X is a nonempty set and ρ is a metric on X. Observe that
metrics can be defined on any nonempty set X; in particular, X need not be a linear space.

Proposition 25. If (X, ρ) is a metric space, then any subspace (Y, ρ|Y ) with ∅ ̸= Y ⊆ X is a
metric space and is called a metric subspace of (X, ρ).

Example 13 (Examples and non-examples of metric spaces). ‌

      where p and q denote the probability densities of P and Q respectively. KL divergence is not
      a metric because it is not symmetric and does not satisfy the triangle inequality.

Proposition 26 (Equivalent metrics). Let (X, ρ1 ) and (X, ρ2 ) be two metric spaces. The following
are equivalent.

  (i) ρ1 and ρ2 are equivalent;

 (ii) ∃α, β ∈ R+ , αρ1 (x, y) ≤ ρ2 (x, y) ≤ βρ1 (x, y) ∀x, y ∈ X;

(iii) ∃c > 0 such that c−1 ρ1 (x, y) ≤ ρ2 (x, y) ≤ cρ1 (x, y) ∀x, y ∈ X.

Proof. Define (i) as (ii). We show equivalence between (ii) and (iii).
   (ii) ⇒ (iii). Suppose that (ii) holds and let α, β be the constant. We must have α ≤ β.

   • Suppose α ≤ β ≤ 1. Then, 1/α ≥ 1 ≥ β so that we may set c := 1/α.

   • Suppose α ≤ 1 ≤ β. Then, set c := β/α. Observe that c ≥ β and c−1 ≤ α.

   • Suppose 1 ≤ α ≤ β. Then, set c := β. Then, 1/c ≤ 1 ≤ α.

(iii) ⇒ (ii). Let c > 0 satisfy the inequality. Define α := 1/c and β := c.                                ■

    For example, if X = R2 , then ρ1 (x, y) := max{|x1 − y1 |, |x2 − y2 |} is equivalent to ρ2 (x, y) :=
|(x1 , x2 ) − (y1 , y2 )|. However, these are not equivalent to the discrete metric.
    Let (X, ρX ) and (Y, ρY ) be two metric spaces. A function f : X → Y is an isometry if

                               ρX (z, z ′ ) = ρY (f (z) , f (z ′ )) ∀z, z ′ ∈ X.

An isometric function can be thought of as relabelling of points in X . Note that isometry f : X → Y
must be injective as, otherwise, two distinct points in X could be mapped to the same point which


                                                    - 40 -
Math Review                                                                                                 2024


would contradict the identity of indiscernibles property of the metric ρX . An isometry that is
bijective is called an isometric isomorphism. Metric spaces X and Y are isometric, denoted X = Y ,
if there exists an isometric isomorphism between them.

2.3    Normed spaces
We now introduce a concept of “size” to vectors in a linear space. A norm on a linear space V is a
function ∥ · ∥ : V → R that satisfies the following conditions:

  (i) (positivity) ∥x∥ ≥ 0 ∀x ∈ V and ∥x∥ = 0 ⇔ x = 0;

 (ii) (homogeneity) ∥αx∥ = |α| · ∥x∥ ∀x ∈ V ∀α ∈ R;

(iii) (triangle inequality) ∥x + y∥ ≤ ∥x∥ + ∥y∥ ∀x, y ∈ V .

A normed space (sometimes called the normed linear space) is a pair (V, ∥ · ∥), where V is a linear
space and ∥ · ∥ is a norm on V . Let S be a linear subspace of V (recall Proposition 13). Then,
(S, ∥ · ∥|S ) is a normed subspace of (V, ∥ · ∥).
    The natural metric, ρ, on a normed space (V, ∥ · ∥) is defined as

                                             ρ (x, y) := ∥x − y∥ .

Two norms ∥ · ∥1 and ∥ · ∥2 on the same linear space V are equivalent if the induce equivalent natural
metrics. A linear transformation, L ∈ L(V, W ) between two normed space (V, ∥ · ∥V ) and (W, ∥ · ∥W )
is isometric if
                                      ∥L (x)∥W = ∥x∥V ∀x ∈ V.                                    (2.4)

2.3.1   Geometric interpretation
Consider a point in z := (x, y) ∈ R2 . The length of this vector (from the origin) in Cartesian
coordinate system, ∥z∥ is given by the Pythagorean theorem:
                                   2
                                                       p
                               ∥z∥ = x2 + y 2 ⇔ ∥z∥ = x2 + y 2 .

Observe that this corresponds exactly to the Euclidean norm of vector z.




                                                             - 42 -
Math Review                                                                                                       2024


2.4     Inner product spaces
Consider the linear space R2 . We can think of any vectors x ∈ R2 as representing direction (from
the origin) or lines that connects the x and the origin. Given any two vectors x, y ∈ R2 —or two
lines x, y ∈ R2 —we can think about the angle between them. For example, we think of the x-axis
(represented by (1, 0)) and y-axis (represented by (0, 1)) as having an angle of 90 degrees between
them, and call them as being orthogonal. The inner product captures the notion of angle between
any two vectors of a linear space.
    An inner product on a linear space V is a function ⟨·, ·⟩ : V × V → R that satisfies the following
conditions:

   • (positivity) ⟨x, x⟩ ≥ 0 ∀x ∈ V and ⟨x, x⟩ = 0 ⇔ x = 0;

   • (symmetry) ⟨x, y⟩ = ⟨y, x⟩ ∀x, y ∈ V ;

   • (linearity) ⟨α · x + β · y, z⟩ = α · ⟨x, z⟩ + β · ⟨y, z⟩ ∀x, y, z ∈ V ∀α, β ∈ R.

An inner product space is a pair (V, ⟨·, ·⟩), where V is a linear space and ⟨·, ·⟩ is an inner product
on V . Two vectors x and y in an inner product space (V, ⟨·, ·⟩) are orthogonal if ⟨x, y⟩ = 0. Since

                                            ⟨x, 0⟩ = ⟨x, 0 · y⟩ = 0 · ⟨x, y⟩ = 0,

any nonzero vector is orthogonal to the zero vector. Every inner product space (V, ⟨·⟩) is a normed
space with the following induced norm:
                                                                  p
                                                        ∥x∥ :=        ⟨x, x⟩.

Example 15. An example of a inner product space is the Euclidean dot product defined on Rn ,
where · : Rn × Rn → R is given by
                                             n
                                             X
                                    x · y :=   xi yi .
                                                                   i=1

The induced norm of the Euclidean dot product is indeed the Euclidean norm. Many inner product
can be defined on the same linear space V . For example, with V = Rn , with (wi )ni=1 ∈ Rn++ ,
                                                                  n
                                                                  X
                                                      ⟨x, y⟩ :=          wi xi yi
                                                                   i=1

is an inner product on Rn (called weighted Euclidean inner product). Some other examples:
                                                             R
    • (L2 (X), ⟨·, ·⟩) for some measurable X, where ⟨f, g⟩ = X f (x)g(x)dx.
                                              P∞
    • (ℓ2 (N), ⟨·, ·⟩), where ⟨(xn ), (yn )⟩ = n=1 xn yn .15

Theorem 4 (Cauchy-Schwarz Inequality). Let (V, ⟨·, ·⟩) be an inner product space and ∥·∥ be the
induced norm. Then,
                             |⟨x, y⟩| ≤ ∥x∥ ∥y∥ ∀x, y ∈ V.

Exercise 17. Prove the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality.

Proposition 29 (Generalised Pythagorean theorem). Let (V, ⟨·, ·⟩) be an inner product space and
∥·∥ be the induced norm. Then,
                                              2          2                          2
                                   ∥x + y∥ = ∥x∥ + 2 ⟨x, y⟩ + ∥y∥ ∀x, y ∈ V.
 15 Holds also for ℓ2 (Z) which is a space of sequences of the form (. . . , b
                                                                              −2 , b−1 , b0 , b1 , b2 , . . .).



                                                               - 43 -
Math Review                                                                                      2024


In particular, if x and y are orthogonal, then
                                                         2            2   2
                                        ∥x + y∥ = ∥x∥ + ∥y∥ .

Proof. We use the linearity and symmetry property of the inner product:
                                        2
                            ∥x + y∥ = ⟨x + y, x + y⟩
                                            = ⟨x, x + y⟩ + ⟨y, x + y⟩
                                            = ⟨x, x⟩ + ⟨x, y⟩ + ⟨y, x⟩ + ⟨y, y⟩
                                                     2                        2
                                            = ∥x∥ + 2 · ⟨x, y⟩ + ∥y∥ .

Finally, recall that x and y are orthogonal if ⟨x, y⟩ = 0.                                          ■

   The following results says that sum of the squares of the lengths of the four sides of a parallel-
ogram equals the sum of the squares of the lengths of the two diagonals.

Corollary 6 (Parallelogram identity). Let (V, ⟨·, ·⟩) be an inner product space and ∥·∥ be the induced
norm. Then,
                              2            2           2        2
                       ∥x − y∥ + ∥x + y∥ = 2 ∥x∥ + 2 ∥y∥ ∀x, y ∈ V.

Proof. By the previous proposition,
                                   2                                  2
                          ∥x − y∥ = ∥x + (−1) · y∥
                                                 2                                    2
                                       = ∥x∥ + 2 · ⟨x, (−1) · y⟩ + ∥(−1) · y∥
                                                 2                        2       2
                                       = ∥x∥ − 2 · ⟨x, y⟩ + |−1| ∥y∥
                                                 2                        2
                                       = ∥x∥ − 2 · ⟨x, y⟩ + ∥y∥ .

Thus,
                                                                            
                 2         2     2                  2     2                  2
          ∥x − y∥ + ∥x + y∥ = ∥x∥ − 2 · ⟨x, y⟩ + ∥y∥ + ∥x∥ + 2 · ⟨x, y⟩ + ∥y∥
                                             2                2
                                = 2 ∥x∥ + 2 ∥y∥ .                                                   ■

2.4.1   Geometric interpretation
Let x, y and z represents the length of the three sides of a triangle and θ the angle between sides
of lengths x and y. The law of cosine, which is a generalisation of the Pythagorean theorem to
non-right triangles, says that
                                     z 2 = x2 + y 2 − 2xy cos θ.
Since “length” of vector in R2 is represented by the Euclidean norm ∥ · ∥, abusing notation slightly
and letting x, y, z ∈ R2 , we can rewrite the law of cosine as
                                   2             2                2
                               ∥z∥ = ∥x∥ + ∥y∥ − 2 ∥x∥ ∥y∥ cos θ.

Using the fact that x = y + z ⇔ z = x − y,
                                        2            2                    2
                2 ∥x∥ ∥y∥ cos θ = ∥x∥ + ∥y∥ − ∥x + (−y)∥
                                                                   
                                     2     2      2               2
                                = ∥x∥ + ∥y∥ − ∥x∥ − 2 ⟨x, y⟩ + ∥y∥ = 2 ⟨x, y⟩
                                    ⟨x, y⟩     ⟨x, y⟩
                       ⇔ cos θ =           =p              .
                                   ∥x∥ ∥y∥   ⟨x, x⟩ ⟨y, y⟩


                                                             - 44 -
Math Review                                                                                         2024


Hence, the angle between two vectors can be computed using the inner product. Observe that if
two vectors are orthogonal, i.e., ⟨x, y⟩ = 0, then the angle between them is cosθ = 0 so that θ is 90
degrees.

2.4.2   Orthogonal complements
Given an inner product space (V, ⟨·, ·⟩) and W ⊆ V , (W, ⟨·, ·⟩|W ) is a subspace of the inner product
space and is itself an inner product space. A vector x ∈ V is orthogonal to subspace W if it is
orthogonal to every vector in W ; i.e.,

                                         ⟨x, w⟩ = 0 ∀w ∈ W.

The set of all vectors in V that are orthogonal to W is the orthogonal complement of W and denoted

                                W ⊥ := {x ∈ V : ⟨x, w⟩ = 0 ∀w ∈ W } ,

where ⊥ is read “perp.” Note W ⊥ is a subspace of V , (W ⊥ )⊥ = W , and the only common vector
between W and W ⊥ is 0. The last observation means that V is a direct sum of W and W ⊥ ; i.e.,

                                            V = W ⊕ W ⊥.

Hence, any x ∈ V can be uniquely expressed as x = w + w⊥ , where w ∈ W and w⊥ = W ⊥ .

Proposition 30. Let (V, ⟨·, ·⟩) be an inner product space. Then, {0}⊥ = V and V ⊥ = {0}.

Proof. Recall that ⟨0, x⟩ = 0 for all x ∈ V and hence {0}⊥ = V and V ⊥ = {0}.                          ■

Example 16. The orthogonal complement of a line W through the original in R2 is the line
perpendicular to W . The orthogonal complement of a line W in R3 is the plane that is perpendicular
to W .

2.4.3   Orthonormal bases
A set of vectors {x1 , . . . , xn } ⊆ V of an inner product space (V, ⟨·, ·⟩) is an orthogonal set if all
pairs of distinct vectors in the set are orthogonal. An orthogonal set in which each vector has a
unit norm is called orthonormal. Since any vector x ∈ V can be normalised to have a unit norm by
dividing by ∥x∥, it follows that any orthogonal set of nonzero vectors can always be converted to
an orthonormal set via such normalisation. A basis consisting of orthonormal vectors in an inner
product space is an orthonormal basis, and a basis consisting of orthogonal vectors is an orthogonal
basis.
    If V = Rn , the canonical basis, {ei }ni=1 is orthonormal since ∥ei ∥ = 1 under the Euclidean norm.
In fact, it is an orthogonal basis since ⟨ei , ej ⟩ = 0 for any i ̸= j.
    We are often interested in finding orthonormal basis as it allows vectors to be expressed in a
simple manner.

Proposition 31. Suppose S := {s1 , . . . , sn } is an orthogonal basis for an inner product space
(V, ⟨·, ·⟩) and let ∥·∥ be the induced norm. Then,

   Of course, the usefulness of orthonormal bases may be vacuous if they do not exist. Luckily, we
have the following result. The proof involves constructing an orthonormal basis from an arbitrary
basis (which we know exists by Theorem 19). The algorithm is called the Gram-Schmidt process.

   We first show that orthogonality implies linear independence.

Proposition 33. Suppose that S := {s1 , . . . , sn } is an orthogonal set of nonzero vectors in an inner
product space (V, ⟨·, ·⟩). Then, S is linearly independent.
                                           Pn
Proof. Take any (λi )ni=1 ∈ Rn such that i=1 λi si = 0. By Proposition 19, it suffices to show that
λ1 = · · · = λn = 0. Since S is an orthogonal set, ∀i ∈ {1, . . . , n},
                                      * n           +    n
                                       X                X
                       0 = ⟨0, si ⟩ =     λj sj , si =      λj ⟨sj , si ⟩ = λi ⟨si , si ⟩ .
                                            j=1                j=1


Since each si is a nonzero vector ⟨si , si ⟩ > 0 by positivity. Therefore, λi = 0 ∀i ∈ {1, . . . , n}.     ■

   Next we introduce the concept of orthogonal projections. This generalises the fact that each
vector x ∈ R2 can be expressed as sum of vectors along the x- and y-axes, which are orthogonal.

Proposition 34. Suppose W is a finite-dimensional subspace of an inner product space (V, ⟨·, ·⟩)
and that S := (s1 , . . . , sn ) is an orthogonal basis for W . Then, for any x ∈ V ,
                                                        n

                                            projW x ∈ span (S) = W,

where the last equality follows by the fact that S is a basis and Proposition 21.
   (ii) projW ⊥ x ∈ W ⊥ . Define x⊥ := projW ⊥ x. We wish to show that ⟨x⊥ , w⟩ = 0 ∀w ∈ W .
Using definitions and linearity of ⟨·, ·⟩,

    We refer to projW x as an orthogonal projection of x on W and orthogonal projection of x on W
as a component of x orthogonal to W . By Proposition 21 and Corollary 3, projections are unique
relative to each orthogonal basis. Put differently, each distinct orthonormal basis of W gives rise
to a unique projection of x on W .

Theorem 5. Every nonzero finite dimensional inner product space has an orthonormal basis.



                                                        - 47 -
Math Review                                                                                                   2024


Proof. Let (V, ⟨·, ·⟩) be a nonzero inner product space. Since V is a linear space, by Theorem 19,
there exists a basis S = {s1 , . . . , sn } so that V is n dimensional. The goal is to construct an
orthonormal basis, V = {v1 , . . . , vn }, from S (note that, if it exists, V must have the same number
of elements by Corollary 13.
Step 1 Define v1 := s1 .

Step 2 Define v2 that is orthogonal to v1 by computing the component of s2 that is orthogonal
     to W1 := span(v1 ). Note that v1 is the (trivially orthogonal) basis of W1 by construction, by
     Proposition 34,
                                                        ⟨s2 , v1 ⟩       ⊥
                              v2 := projW1⊥ s2 = s2 −          2 · v1 ∈ W1
                                                         ∥v1 ∥
      For v2 to be a basis, we must have v2 ̸= 0. To show this, by way of contradiction, suppose
      that v2 = 0, then
                                        ⟨s2 , v1 ⟩      ⟨s2 , s1 ⟩
                                   s2 =        2 · v1 =        2 · s1 .
                                         ∥v1 ∥           ∥s1 ∥
      This implies that s2 is a scalar multiple of s1 so that s1 and s2 are linearly dependent.
      However, this contradicts the fact that any basis is linearly independent (Proposition 21).

Step 3 Define v3 that is orthogonal to v1 and v2 by computing the component of s3 that is ortho-
     gonal to W2 = span({v1 , v2 }). Since {v1 , v2 } is an orthogonal basis of W2 by construction,
     by Proposition 34,

                                                           ⟨s3 , v1 ⟩             ⟨s3 , v2 ⟩
                          v3 := projW2⊥ s3 = s3 −                   2    · v1 −          2     · v2 ∈ W2⊥ .
                                                            ∥v1 ∥                  ∥v2 ∥

      If v3 = 0, then

                                     ⟨s3 , v1 ⟩             ⟨s3 , v2 ⟩
                              s3 =            2   · v1 +             2   · v2
                                      ∥v1 ∥                  ∥v2 ∥
                                                                                                        !
                                     ⟨s3 , v1 ⟩            ⟨s3 , v2 ⟩               ⟨s2 , v1 ⟩
                                 =            2   · s1 +            2    ·   s2 −            2   · s1
                                      ∥v1 ∥                 ∥v2 ∥                    ∥v1 ∥

      so that s3 is a linear combination of s1 and s2 , contradicting the fact that any basis is linearly
      independent (Proposition 21).
Continuing in this way, after n steps, we will obtain an orthogonal set of vectors {v1 , . . . , vn }. To
conclude the proof, we must show that {v1 , . . . , vn } is a basis. By Proposition 21, it suffices to show
that {v1 , . . . , vn } is linearly independent and that span{v1 , . . . , vn } = V . Linear independence
follows from the fact that {v1 , . . . , vn } are orthogonal and Proposition 33. That span{v1 , . . . , vn } =
V follows from Proposition 22 because dimV = n and {v1 , . . . , vn } is linearly independent.               ■

   We can, in fact, apply the Gram-Schmidt process to any linearly independent set of vectors in
an inner product space to obtain an orthonormal set of vectors.
Corollary 7 (Projection Theorem). Suppose W is a finite dimensional subspace of an inner product
space (V, ⟨·, ·⟩). Then, for any x ∈ V , there exists an orthogonal projection of x on W , denoted
projW x ∈ W , and component of x orthogonal to W , denoted projW ⊥ x ∈ W ⊥ , such that

                                          x = projW x + projW ⊥ x.

Proof. Given Proposition 34, it suffices to show that W has an orthogonal basis. But we know that
W has an orthogonal basis from Theorem 5.                                                      ■


                                                           - 48 -
Math Review                                                                                                  2024


Remark 3. Note that we can write

                                        x = projW x + (x − projW x) .

Does this look familiar?

Theorem 6 (Approximation Theorem). Suppose W is a finite dimensional subspace of an inner
product space (V, ⟨·, ·⟩). For any x ∈ V , projW x is the best approximation to x from W in the sense
that it is the element in W that minimises the distance between x and itself; i.e.,

                             ∥x − projW x∥ < ∥x − w∥ ∀w ∈ W \ {projW x} .

Proof. Adding and subtracting projW x, we can write

                                 x − w = (x − projW x) + (projW x − w) .

By construction, projW x ∈ W so that projW x − w ∈ W , and x − projW x = projW ⊤ x ∈ W ⊤ . That
is, the two terms in the expression above are orthogonal. Then, by the Generalised Pythagorean
Theorem (Theorem 29),
                                        2                           2                 2
                             ∥x − w∥ = ∥x − projW x∥ + ∥projW x − w∥ .

By positivity, the second term is strictly positive unless projW x = w.                         Thus, for any w ∈
W \{projW x},
                                          2                 2
                                 ∥x − w∥ > ∥x − projW x∥ ,
which implies the desired result.                                                                              ■

Example 17 (Fourier series). A function of the form

                         τ (x) = c0 + c1 cos (x) + c2 cos (2x) + · · · + cn cos (nx)
                                      + d1 sin (x) + d2 sin (2x) + · · · + dn sin (nx)

is called a trigonometric polynomial, and if cn and dn are not both zero, then τ (x) is said to have
order n. It can be shown that

                S := {1, cos (x) , cos (2x) , . . . , cos (nx) , sin (x) , sin (2x) , . . . , sin (nx)}

are linearly independent so that, for any interval [a, b] ⊆ R, they form a basis for a (2n + 1)-
dimensional subspace of C([a, b])] (i.e., continuous functions in the interval [a, b]). Let f ∈ C([0, 2π]),
where (C(0, 2π), ⟨·, ·⟩) is an inner product space with

2.5      Topologies
Let X be a nonempty set. A topology T for X is a collection of subsets of X, called open sets, that
have the following properties.

  (i) The entire set, X, and the empty set, ∅, are open—i.e. ∅, X ∈ T .

  (ii) The intersection of any finite collection of open sets is open—i.e. for any O = {O1 , O2 , . . . , On } ⊆
          Tn
       T , i=1 Oi ∈ T ;
                                                                                      S
 (iii) The union of any collection of any open set is open—i.e. for any O ⊆ T , O∈O O ∈ T .

The pair (X, T ), where X is an nonempty set and T is a topology for X, is a topological space. For
any x ∈ X, an open set that contains x is a neighbourhood of x. A collection of neighbourhoods
of x, Bx , is called a base for the topology at x if, for any neighbourhood O of x, there exists a set
B ∈ Bx such that B ⊆ O. A collection of open sets B is called a base for the topology T if it
contains a base for the topology at each point in X. A base determines a unique topology on X
that consists of ∅, and the union of sets belonging to the base.

Proposition 35. A subset E ⊆ X is open if and only if, for each point x ∈ E, there is a neigh-
bourhood of x that is contained in E. That is,

                                 E ∈ T ⇔ ∀x ∈ E, ∃Ox ∈ T , x ∈ Ox ⊆ E.

Proof. Let E ⊆ X, where (X, T ) is a topological space. Suppose that ∀x ∈ E, ∃Ox ∈ T , x ∈ Ox ⊆
E. Then,                                [
                             x ∈ O :=       Ox′ ∀x ∈ E ⇒ E ⊆ O.
                                                 x′ ∈E


                                                         - 50 -
Math Review                                                                                           2024


Moreover, since Ox ⊆ E for all x ∈ E, it follows that O ⊆ E. That is, O = E. Finally, since O is a
union of open sets, by (iii) of the definition, O = E is an open set. Conversely, suppose that E is
open. Then, E is a neighbourhood of any x that is contained in E.                                ■

Proposition 36. Every open set is a union of neighbourhoods.

Proof. Let E ⊆ X be an open set, where (X, T ) is a topological space. For each x ∈ E, let Nx
be the open neighbourhood in E containing x, which must exist by proposition above. Consider
      S
N := x∈E Nx . Since for every x, x ∈ Nx ⊆ N , it follows that U ⊆ N . Since every Nx ⊆ E, it also
follows that N ⊆ E. Thus, U = N .                                                              ■

    For a subset E ⊆ X, a point x ∈ X is a point of closure of E provided that every neighbourhood
of x contains a point in E. The collection of points of closure of E is the closure of E, denoted by
E. Since E ⊆ E, if E contains all of its points of closure, i.e., E = E, then E is closed.

Proposition 37. A subset of a topological space X is open if and only if its complement in X is
closed.

Proof. First, suppose that E ⊆ X is open. Let x ∈ X be a point of closure of E c = X\E; i.e.,
every neighbourhood of x contains a point in E c . Then, x cannot belong to E because, otherwise,
there would be a neighbourhood of x that is contained in E and therefore does not intersect E c .
Thus, x belongs to E c and hence E c is closed. Now suppose that E c is closed; i.e., E c contains all
of its points of closure. Let x ∈ E. Then, there must be a neighbourhood of x that is contained in
E, for otherwise, every neighbourhood of x would contain points in E c and therefore x would be a
point of closure of E c . Since E c is closed, x would belong to E c , which is a contradiction.    ■

Remark 4. Above proposition combined with De Morgan’s law gives an alternative characterisation
of topological spaces using closed sets. A topology T for X is a collection of subsets of X, called
closed sets, that have the following properties.

  (i) The entire set, X, and the empty set, ∅, are closed;

 (ii) The union of any finite collection of closed sets is closed—i.e. for any C = {C1 , C2 , . . . , Cn } ⊆
         Sn
      T , i=1 Ci is closed;
                                                                                              T
(iii) The intersection of any collection of any closed set is closed—i.e. for any C ⊆ T , C∈C C is
      closed.

   Given a topology (X, T ), the interior of S ⊆ X, denoted int(S), is the union of all subsets of S
that are open in X. A point that is in the interior of S is an interior point of S. If X is in fact a
metric space, then x ∈ X is an interior point of S if there exists r > 0 such that y is in S whenever
ρ(x, y) < r. The boundary of S ⊆ X, denoted bd(S) or ∂S, is the set of points in the closure of S
not belonging to the interior of S. A point that is in the boundary of S is a boundary point of S.

Example 18 (Examples of topologies). ‌

   • Discrete topology. Let X be any nonempty set. The discrete topology for X is the collection
     of all subsets of X. For discrete topology, every set containing a point is a neighbourhood of
     that point. The discrete topology is induced by the discrete metric.

   • Trivial topology. let X be any nonempty set. Then, {∅, X} is the trivial topology for X. The
     only neighbourhood of a point is the whole set X.

   • Topological subspaces. Given a topological space (X, T ) and a nonempty subset E ⊆ X, we
     define the inherited topology S for E to consists of all sets of the form E ∩ O,where O ∈ T .
     We call the topological space (E, S) a subspace of (X, T ).


                                                  - 51 -
Math Review                                                                                       2024


   • Product topology. Let (X, T ) and (Y, S) be two topological spaces. In X × Y , consider the
     collection of sets B consisting of O1 × O2 , where O1 is open in X and O2 is open in Y . Then,
     (X × Y, B) is the product topology for X × Y .

   • Metric topology. Let (X, ρ) be a metric space. Define an ball centred at x ∈ X with radius
     r > 0 as
                                   Br (x) := {y ∈ X : ρ (z, x) < r} .
        Define a subset O ⊆ X to be open if, for every x ∈ O, Br (x) ⊆ O for some r > 0. The
        collection of such open sets is the metric topology for X induced by the metric ρ.

   • Strong (or norm) topology. Let V be a normed space. The metric topology for V induced by
     the natural metric ρ is called the strong (or norm) topology for X.

Let X and Y be topological spaces. A function f : X → Y is continuous if, for any open set V ⊆ Y ,
f −1 (V ) ≡ {x ∈ X : f (x) ∈ V } is an open subset of X.16

2.5.1     Order over topologies
Let T1 and T2 be two topologies for a nonempty set X. We say that T1 is weaker than T2 (or,
equivalently, T2 is stronger than T1 ) if T1 ⊆ T2 . Thus, any open set in the weaker topology is also
open in the stronger topology. The converse is not necessarily true.

2.5.2     Compactness
Let (X, T ) be a topological space. A collection of sets S = {Sλ }λ∈Λ is a cover of a set S ⊆ X if
      S
S ⊆ λ∈Λ Sλ . Let S be a cover of S. A subcover is a subset of S that is also a cover of S. If each
set Sλ is open, then S is an open cover. If Λ is a finite set, then S is a finite cover (note that each
set Sλ need not be finite). A subset S ⊆ X is compact (in X) if every open cover of S has a finite
subcover.
    If X = Rn , then a subset S ⊆ X is compact if and only if S is closed and bounded (Heine-Borel
theorem). In a metric space, while compactness implies closedness and boundedness, the converse
only holds if the space is complete (i.e., if every Cauchy sequence is convergent). The usefulness
of compactness comes from the fact that any finite set has a maximum—thus, existence of finite
covers allows us to take a maximum among the finite subcovers.

2.5.3     Homeomorphism
Let (X, T ) and (Y, S) be two topological spaces. A function f : X → Y is a homeomorphism if f is
a bijection, f is continuous, and the inverse function f −1 is continuous. If such a homeomorphism
exists between X and Y , then they are homeomorphic and the two spaces have the same topolo-
gical properties (e.g., if a set is compact in one space, it is also compact in the other). In fact,
homeomorphic spaces induces an equivalence relation on topological spaces.

Example 19. An open interval (a, b) is homeomorphic to R. To see this, define f : (a, b) → R as
          1
f (x) := a−x    1
             + b−x . Note that Rm and Rn are not homeomorphic if m ̸= n.

 16 Equivalently, f is continuous if inverse image of any closets set in Y   are closed in X.

Math Review                                                                                                     2024


3       Linear algebra
3.1     Matrices
For any m, n ∈ N, an m × n matrix (in R), denoted X, is a real-valued function

                                       X : {1, . . . , m} × {1, . . . , n} → R,

which we often represent as
                                                                            
                                                     x11          ···    x1n
                                                    ..           ..      ..  ,
                                      X ≡ [xij ] ≡  .               .     . 
                                                    xm1           ···    xmn

where xij := X(i, j) for each (i, j) ∈ {1, . . . , m} × {1, . . . , n}. We may write [xij ]m×n to stress its
dimension. Thus, xij represents the ith row and jth column—or the ijth entry—of the matrix X.
Matrices are usually written with uppercase letters and its element with corresponding lowercase
letters.
    Real numbers are 1 × 1 matrices, and a vector in Rn can be seen as either n × 1 or 1 × n
matrices. We often refer to n × 1 matrices as column vectors and 1 × n matrices as row vectors.
Unless otherwise specified, a vector is taken to be a column vector. A matrix whose entries all zero
is called a zero matrix. We call any matrices with m = n as (n × n) square matrices. A square
matrix X is diagonal if all its nondiagonal elements are zero; i.e., X is a diagonal matrix if aij = 0
∀(i, j) ∈ {1, . . . , m} × {1, . . . , n} : i ̸= j, and we write X = diag(a11 , . . . , ann ). An n × n square
matrix X is an identity matrix if X = diag(1, . . . , 1) and we denote it as In . A square matrix
X is upper (resp. lower ) triangular if all its elements below its diagonals are zeros; i.e., aij = 0
∀(i, j) ∈ {1, . . . , m} × {1, . . . , n} : i > j (resp. aij = 0 ∀(i, j) ∈ {1, . . . , m} × {1, . . . , n} : i < j). A
diagonal matrix is both upper and lower triangular.

3.1.1    Space of matrices as a linear space
The set of all m × n matrices is denoted
                                       n                         o
                             Rm×n := X : X ∈ R{1,...,m}×{1,...,n} .

We will define matrix addition and scalar multiplications so that (Rm×n , (R, +R , ·R ), +, ·) is a linear
space.

Proposition 38. Given X, Y ∈ Rm×n and λ ∈ R, define + : Rm×n × Rm×n → Rm×n and
· : R × Rm×n → Rm×n as

                                       X + Y ≡ + (X, Y ) := [xij + yij ],
                                         λ · X ≡ · (λ, X) := [λ ·R xij ].

Then, (Rm×n , (R, +R , ·R ), +, ·) is a linear space of dimension m × n with zero given by the zero
matrix, the additive inverse of any X ∈ R is −1 · X, and the multiplicative identity is 1.

    Let Eij ∈ Rm×n be a matrix whose entries are all zero except the ijth entry which is equal to
1 . The set {Eij }i=1,...,m,j=1,...,n is a basis of Rm×n and is the canonical basis of the linear space
Rm×n .
    Given any X ∈ Rm×n , a matrix Xcan be viewed as either a collection column vectors, {xj }j∈{1...,n} ,

or a collection of row vectors, {xi }i∈{1,...,m} ; i.e.,

Given X ∈ Rm×n and Y ∈ Rn×r , their matrix multiplication (or product), denoted XY , is defined

Thus, matrix multiplication is defined only for matrices whose number of columns and rows are the

                                                       XY ̸= Y X.

We therefore distinguish between pre- and post-multiplications (or left- and right-multiplication)—pre-
multiplying (or left-multiplying) X by Y means Y X and post-multiplying (or right-multiplying) X
by Y means XY .

and so

These are useful to keep in mind in econometrics (and especially when coding).
    Another important fact is that XY = 0 need not imply that A = 0 or B = 0 unlike multiplication
of real numbers.

   • Matrix product is distributive with respect to addition; i.e., X(Y + Z) = XY + XZ and
     (X + Y )Z = XZ + Y Z.

   • Matrix product is associative; i.e., X(Y Z) = (XY )Z.

   • Conformable identity matrix is the multiplicative identity; i.e., Im X = XIn = X.

   • If X and Y are upper (resp. lower) triangular, then XY = Y X is also upper (resp. lower)
     triangular.

  For square matrices, we can define repeated products or powers of matrices. Given any X ∈

Matrix product as a linear transformation Let V = Rn×r and W = Rm×r and define
LX : Rn×r → Rm×r as LX (Y ) := XY for some X ∈ Rm×n ; i.e., we pre-multiply “vectors” in Rn×r
by X. Then, for any Y, Z ∈ V and α ∈ R, we have

              LX (α · Y + Z) = X (α · Y + Z) = αXY + XZ = α · LX (Y ) + LX (Z) .

Thus, matrix X ∈ Rm×n is a linear transformation that maps vectors from linear space Rn×r to
linear space Rm×r . Since we can write XY as a linear combination of columns, xj , of X,

Geometric interpretation of matrix product Any (z1 , z2 ) ∈ R2 can be expressed as a point
on a graph. Suppose we consider linear transformations L : R2 → R2 defined as
                                                                                          

Consider the linear transformation of the unit square by pre-multiplication using matrix X. The
vertices in the unique square is then mapped to a parallelogram:

                          (0, 0) 7→ (0, 0) ,                     (1, 0) 7→ (x11 , x21 ) ,
                          (1, 1) 7→ (x11 + x12 , x21 + x22 ) , (0, 1) 7→ (x12 , x22 ) .

Thus, linear transformation here can be thought of as a change from the Cartesian coordinate
system to a new grid system with axes that are generally not at right angels, and each unit square
of the grid is transformed into a parallelogram.

3.1.3   Span
Recall that any matrix X ∈ Rm×n can be viewed as a collection of (row or column) vectors. Thus,
we can consider the linear space that the vectors span. The linear space spanned by the columns
of X is the column space (or image) of X; i.e.,
                                                                                     
                               1              Xn                                   
              im (X) := span x , . . . , xn ≡       λj xj : λj ∈ R ∀j ∈ {1, . . . , n} .
                                                                                     
                                                         j=1


The column rank of X is the rank of the column space of X (recall that rank of a linear space
equals the cardinality of (any) basis of that space). The linear space spanned by the rows of X, i.e.,
span({x1 , . . . , xm }), is the row space of X. The row rank of X is the rank of the row space of X.


                                                       - 56 -
Math Review                                                                                                 2024


Proposition 41. For any X ∈ Rm×n , rank(span({x1 , . . . , xn })) = rank(span({x1 , . . . , xm })).

    The proposition above means that it is unambiguous to the rank of matrix X without reference
to row or column ranks. Hence, we simply write rank(X) to mean the rank of the column (or row)
space of matrix X.
    Observe that rank(In ) = n and each column (resp. row) are bases of column (resp. row) spaces.

Proposition 42. For any X ∈ Rm×n and Y ∈ Rn×r ,

                                rank (XY ) ≤ min {rank (X) , rank (Y )} .

Proof. Suppose X ∈ Rm×n and Y ∈ Rn×r and recall that each column of Z := XY is a linear
combination of columns of X. Hence, im(Z) ⊆ im(X) and im(Z) is a linear subspace of im(X).
Therefore, by Corollary 4,

                             rank (XY ) = dim (Z) ≤ dim (X) = rank (X) .

Similarly, recall that each row of XY is a linear combination of the rows in Y . Hence,

                               span ({z1 , . . . , zm }) ⊆ span ({y1 , . . . , ym })

and span({z1 , . . . , zm }) is a linear subspace of span({y1 , . . . , ym }). Therefore, by Corollary 4,

                              rank (XY ) = dim (Z) ≤ dim (Y ) = rank (Y ) .

Together, these imply that the desired inequality.                                                            ■

Corollary 8. Rank of an m × n matrix is always smaller than both m and n.

Proof. By the previous proposition and the fact that rank(In ) = n,

             rank (X) = rank (XIn ) ≤ min {rank (X) , rank (In )} = min {rank (X) , n} ,
             rank (X) = rank (Im X) ≤ min {rank (Im ) , rank (X)} = min {m, rank (X)} .

Together, these imply that
                                          rank (X) ≤ min {m, n} .                                             ■

   We say that matrix X ∈ Rm×n has full rank if rank(X) = min{m, n}.

Proposition 43. Suppose X ∈ Rm×n has rank k ≤ min{m, n}. Then, X has k linearly independent
columns and rows.

    The null space (or the kernel) of matrix X ∈ Rm×n is the solutions to the homogenous system
of equations, Xz = 0; i.e.,
                                            
                                 null (X) = z ∈ Rn×1 : Xz = 0 .
The dimension of the null space of X is the nullity of X, denoted nullity(X) := dim(null(X)).

Theorem 7 (Rank-nullity theorem). Suppose X ∈ Rm×n , then

                                        rank (X) + nullity (X) = n.

Math Review                                                                                            2024


3.1.4   Inverse
An n × n square matrix X is invertible (or nonsingular ) if there exists a matrix Y ∈ Rn×n such
that
                                       XY = Y X = In .
If it exists, we call Y the inverse matrix of X and denote it as X −1 . Inverse matrices are unique
whenever they exist and has the property that (X −1 )−1 = X.17 Matrices that are not invertible
are singular.

Proposition 44. Suppose X, Y ∈ Rn×n are invertible, then their matrix product is also invertible
and
                                       −1
                                  (XY ) = Y −1 X −1 .

   We will not go over the general method of computing inverse matrices.18

Proposition 45. A triangular matrix (upper or lower) is nonsingular if and only if its diagonal
entires are nonzero. The inverse of an upper (resp. lower) triangular matrix is also upper (resp.
lower) triangular whenever they exist.

   Above implies that a diagonal matrix is invertible if and only if the diagonal elements are
nonzero. Thus, I is always invertible.

Proposition 46. Suppose X ∈ Rn×n is an invertible, diagonal matrix. Then,
                                                        
                                            1         1
                             X −1 = diag       ,...,       .
                                           a11       ann

Proposition 47. A square matrix X ∈ Rn×n is invertible if and only if rank(X) = n.

3.1.5   Transpose
Given X ∈ Rm×n , the transpose of X, denoted X ⊤ or X ′ , is the matrix obtained by placing the
ijth entries into the jith positions instead; i.e.,

                                             X ⊤ := [aji ]n×m .

Note (X ⊤ )⊤ = X, transpose of a real number (i.e., a 1 × 1 matrix) is itself, and transpose of a
row vector is a column vector (and vice versa). We say that a matrix X is symmetric if it equals
its transpose; i.e., X = X ⊤ . Symmetric matrices must be square matrices. A diagonal matrix is
symmetric. Symmetries is preserved under matrix addition (and subtraction).

Proposition 48. Suppose X ∈ Rm×n , Y ∈ Rn×r and λ ∈ R.
            ⊤
   • (λX) = λX ⊤ .

   • (X + Y )⊤ = X ⊤ + Y ⊤ .

   • (XY )⊤ = Y ⊤ X ⊤ .

   • (X −1 )⊤ = (X ⊤ )−1 whenever X is invertible.

   • XX ⊤ and X ⊤ X are symmetric matrices.

   • If X is upper (resp. lower) triangular, then X ⊤ is lower (resp. upper) triangular.
 17 Toward a contradiction, suppose X has two distinct inverses Y and Z. But Y = Y I = Y (XZ) = (Y X)Z = IZ =

Z; a contradiction.

3.1.6     Trace
Given X ∈ Rn×n , the trace of X, denoted tr(X) is the sum of its diagonals; i.e.,
                                                               n
                                                               X
                                               tr (X) :=             xii .
                                                               i=1

Proposition 49. Suppose X, Y ∈ Rn×n , λ ∈ R,
   • Trance is a linear transformation: tr(λX + Y ) = λtr(X) + tr(Y ).
   • tr(X) = tr(X ⊤ ).
   • If AB and BA are square matrices (but not necessarily A and B), then tr(AB) = tr(BA).
   • Invariant under cyclic permutation: tr(ABCD) = tr(BCDA) = tr(CDAB) = tr(DABC).

  (i) for a 1 × 1 matrix X = x11 , define its determinant as det(X) := x11 ;
 (ii) for an n × n matrix with n ≥ 2, define its determinant via the cofactor of expansion of X
      along the first row)

Geometric interpretation of determinant Recall that a linear transformation from R2 to R2
reshapes a unit square to a parallelogram in Cartesian coordinate system. It turns out that the area
of the parallelogram produced by transformation the unit square via matrix X is equal to |det(X)|
(the sign of det(X) tells us about the orientation of the parallelogram compared to the original
square). All of the standard transformations in the example are associated with matrices with
determinant of one. This means that these transformations preserve area. In contrast, consider the
following matrix, for some k1 , k2 ∈ R,
                                                   
                                          k1   0
                               X=                       ⇒ |det (X)| = |k1 k2 | .
                                          0    k2

This linear transformation scales area by a factor k1 k2 ; it scales in the x-axis direction by a factor
k1 and in the y-axis direction of a factor k2 .
    Observe also that the matrices associated with the aforementioned standard linear transform-
ations are all invertible/nonsingular. This means that each point is mapped to a unique point so
that the transformations are reversible. In contrast, linear transformation via singular matrices
cannot be reversed uniquely. For example, consider the matrix
                                                       
                                               1    2
                                   X=                       ⇒ |det (X)| = 0.
                                               2    4

Observe that every point in (x, y) is mapped to some point on the same line y = 2x. This means
that there are many points that are mapped to the same point on the line y = 2x. For example,
                                                   
                                                  1
                                      (2, 2) , 3,     7→ (4, 8) .
                                                  2

3.1.9   Space of matrices as an inner product space
Suppose y, z ∈ Rn×1 and X ∈ Rn×n is invertible. First, observe that Euclidean dot product can
be written as matrix product:
                                                                          
                               n                                        y1
                               X                                     . 
                      y·z=            yi zi =       z1   ···       zn  ..  = z⊤ y = y⊤ z.
                                i=1
                                                                        yn

Since Xy, Xz ∈ Rn×1 , we may consider the Euclidean dot product between the two:
                                                               ⊤
                                      Xy · Xz = (Xy) Xz = y⊤ X ⊤ Xz.

The function ⟨·, ·⟩X : Rn × Rn → R defined by

                                       ⟨y, z⟩X := Xy · Xz = y⊤ X ⊤ Xz.

is an inner product and is called an inner product on Rn generated by X. The Euclidean dot
product is a special case in which X = In . Weighted Euclidean dot product is a special case in
which X = diag(w1 , . . . , wn ).

Proposition 52. (Rm×n , ⟨·, ·⟩) is an inner product space where
                                                                   
                                                ⟨X, Y ⟩ := tr Y ⊤ X .

    The following provides a geometric connection between the null space and row/column spaces
of a matrix.




                                                          - 62 -
Math Review                                                                                   2024


Proposition 53. Given X ∈ Rm×n ,
                                                                            ⊥
                           null (X) = (span ({x1 , . . . , xm })) ,
                                                             ⊥            ⊥
                         null X ⊤ = span x1 , . . . , xn            = (im (X)) ,

where the orthogonal complement is taken with respect to the Euclidean inner product.

Proof. Suppose X ∈ Rm×n and the we take the inner product to be the Euclidean dot product.
   Take any y ∈ null(X). We first show that y is orthogonal to every vector in the row space of
X. By definition of null space,

                           Xy = 0 ⇔ xi · y = ⟨xi , y⟩ = 0 ∀i ∈ {1, . . . , m} .

Thus, each xi (i.e., each row of matrix X) is orthogonal to y. By definition, for any x ∈
                                                                   Pm
span({x1 , . . . , xm }), there exists (λi )m
                                            i=1 ∈ R
                                                    m
                                                      such that x = i=1 λi xi . Thus,
                                          *m                 +       m
                                           X                         X
                              ⟨x, y⟩ =           λi xi , y       =         λi ⟨xi , y⟩ = 0.
                                           i=1                       i=1

Hence, y is orthogonal to any element in the row space of X. Sine y was arbitrary, it follows that
every element of null (X) is orthogonal to the row space of X. Conversely, take any y⊥ that is
orthogonal to every row vector of X; i.e.,

                                   xi , y⊥ = xi · y⊥ = 0 ∀i ∈ {1, . . . , m} .

This implies that y⊥ ∈ null(X) since
                                         ⊥   Pm            ⊥
                                                                              
                x11         ···     x1n     y1         i=1 x1i yi      x1 · y ⊥
           ⊥   ..          ..       ..   ..  =       ..            ..    
         Xy =  .              .      .  .             .       =     .     = 0.
                                             ⊥
                                                     Pm          ⊥            ⊥
               xm1          ···     xmn     yn        i=1 xmi yi       xm · y

Since y⊥ was chosen arbitrary, it follows that any vector that is orthogonal to every tow vector of
X is in the null space of X. We have now shown the first equality. To show the second equality,
observe that the column space of X is equal to the row space of X ⊤ .                            ■

    Recall that in any inner product space V , the zero space ({0}) and V are orthogonal complement
of one another. Thus, if X ∈ Rn×n , to say that Ax = 0 has only the trivial solution (of x = 0)
is equivalent to saying that the orthogonal complement of the null space of X is all of Rn , or,
equivalently (by the proposition above), that the row space of X is all of Rn . This gives the
following characterisation.

Proposition 54. The following are equivalent.

   • A matrix X ∈ Rn×n is invertible.

   • (null(X))⊥ = Rn .

   • (span({x1 , . . . , xm }))⊥ = {0}.

Theorem 8 (QR-decomposition). Suppose X ∈ Rm×n has linearly independent column vectors;
i.e., {x1 , . . . , xn } are linearly independent. Then, X can be decomposed as

                                                    X = QR,


                                                       - 63 -
Math Review                                                                                                              2024


where Q ∈ Rm×n is a matrix with orthonormal column vectors and R ∈ Rn×n is an invertible upper
triangular matrix.
Proof. Let X ∈ Rm×n be a matrix with linearly independent column vectors {x1 , . . . , xn }. Then,
(im(X), ⟨·, ·⟩) is an inner product space with ⟨·, ·⟩ being the Euclidean dot product; moreover,
{x1 , . . . , xn } is a basis of im(X). Since {x1 , . . . , xn } is linearly independent, we can apply the
Gram-Schmidt process to obtain orthonormal an basis, S := {s1 , . . . , sn }. By Proposition 31, we
can write
                                          n
                                          X
                                     xi =     xi , sj sj ∀i ∈ {1, . . . , n} .
                                                 j=1

Thus,

That is, R is an upper triangular matrix.                                                                                  ■

   Since X ∈ Rn×n is invertible if and only if it has linearly independent columns, it follows that
every invertible matrix has a QR-decomposition.

3.2     System of linear equations
A system of a linear equations take the form:

                                          a11 x1 + a12 x2 + · · · + a1n xn = b1 ,
                                          a11 x1 + a12 x2 + · · · + a1n xn = b2 ,
                                                                        ..                 ..
                                                                         .                  .
                                        am1 x1 + am2 x2 + · · · + amn xn = bm ,

where aij , bj ∈ R for all i, j ∈ {1, . . . , n} × {1, . . . , m} and the unknowns are x1 , . . . , xn ∈ R. The
system of linear equations can be written succinctly as

                                                              Ax = b,

where A = [aij ]m×n , b = (b1 , . . . , bm ) and x = (x1 , . . . , xn ) (remember the convention that unspe-
cified vectors are row vectors). The following proposition characterised the existence as well as the
unique of the solutions to a system of linear equations.
Proposition 55. Suppose A ∈ Rm×n and b = Rm×1 . Then, the system of equation Ax = b has a
solution if and only if
                               rank ([A|b]) = rank (A) ,                            (3.1)


                                                                   - 64 -
Math Review                                                                                         2024


where [A|b] := [a1 , . . . , an , b] ∈ Rm×n+1 is the augmentation of matrix A by b (called the augmented
matrix).
    If the system has a solution, then (i) the solution is unique if and only if A is full rank; (ii) the
system has infinitely many solutions if and only if rank(A) < n.
    Recall that Ax is a linear combination of columns of X. The condition (3.1) means that b is a
linear combination of the columns of A; i.e., the existence of a solution is equivalent to b belonging
in the column space of A. Recalling Proposition 7, uniqueness of a solution (when one exists)
depends on the dimension of the null space of A. In fact, one can think of nullity(A) as the number
of free parameters in the system of equations.
Proposition 56 (General solution of a linear equation). Given A ∈ Rm×n and b ∈ Rm×1 , suppose
x∗ ∈ Rn×1 satisfies Ax∗ = b. Then, the set of all solutions satisfying the Ax = b is given by

                                      {x∗ + xh : xh ∈ null (A)} .

    A system of linear equations Ax = b is homogenous if b = 0 and non-homogenous if b ̸= 0. A
vector x∗ satisfying Ax∗ = b is a particular solution of the linear equation Ax = b. The result above
tells us that the general solution of a non-homogenous system of linear equations, {x : Ax = b}. is
the sum of any particular solution (x∗ ) and the general solution of the related homogenous system
(xh such that Axh = 0).
    When solving linear equations by hand, you can use the Gauss-Jordan elimination, which we
will not cover here. In the special case in which A is an invertible square matrix, the (unique)
solution is given by x∗ := A−1 b and we have the following theorem to characterise the solution.
Theorem 9 (Cramer’s Rule). Suppose A ∈ Rn×n is invertible and b ∈ Rn×1 . The ith entry of the
vector x∗ := A−1 b ∈ Rn×1 is given by

                                            det (Ai )
                                    x∗i =             ∀i ∈ {1, . . . , n} ,
                                            det (A)

where Ai ∈ Rn×n is the matrix obtained by replacing the ith column of A by b.

3.3    Least square solution as orthogonal projections
Consider a linear system of equations Xb = y, where X ∈ Rm×n , b ∈ Rn×1 and y ∈ Rm×1 .
Suppose that this system of equation has no solution. That is, by our discussion about Proposition
55, y ∈ / im(X). We might find ourselves in such situation if Xb = y represents a theoretical
relationship that is only observed in real data with some noise. One way to solve such an inconsistent
system of equation is to look for b that comes “as close as possible” to being a solution in the sense
that it minimises the the Euclidean distance
                                         p between Xb ∈ im(X) and y. Thinking of e := Xb−y
                                                                                          b

as errors, we wish to minimise ∥e ∥ = (e1 )2 + · · · + (ebn )2 with respect to b. The solution to such
                                   b         b

a problem is called the least squares solution of Xb = y since it minimises the squares errors.
    Define W to be the column space of X; i.e., W := im(X). For any b ∈ Rn×1 then Xb is a
linear combination of column vectors of X; i.e., Xb ∈ W for any b ∈ Rn×1 . By the Approximation
Theorem (Theorem 6), the best approximation to y in W is the orthogonal projection of y on W ,
ŷ := projW y. We now wish to find b̂ ∈ Rn×1 such that

                                              X b̂ = projW y.

We will do so without computing projW y explicitly. Observe that

                             y − X b̂ = y − projW y = projW ⊤ y ∈ W ⊥ .


                                                    - 65 -
Math Review                                                                                   2024


That is, y − X b̂ is orthogonal to W , which is the column space of X. Then, by Proposition 53,
                                                      
                   y − X b̂ ∈ null X ⊤ ⇔ X ⊤ y − X b̂ = 0 ⇔ X ⊤ y = X ⊤ X b̂.

The last expression is called the normal system associated with Xb = y. Recall that we are
concerned with a linear system of equations Xb = y with no solutions. The point of all of this is
the following result.

Proposition 57. A solution to a normal system associated with Xb = y always exists.

   We first prove the following lemma.

Lemma 3. For any matrix X ∈ Rm×n ,
                                                                  
                           null X ⊤ X = null (X) , im X ⊤ X = im X ⊤ .

Proof. For any b ∈ null(X), then Xb = 0 so that X⊤ Xb = X⊤ 0 = 0 and b ∈ null(X ⊤ X). Thus,
null(X) ⊆ null(X ⊤ X). Conversely, take any b ∈ null(X ⊤ X), then
                                                           ⊤                        2
         X ⊤ Xb = 0 ⇒ b⊤ X ⊤ Xb = b⊤ 0 = 0 ⇒ (Xb) (Xb) = ⟨Xb, Xb⟩ = ∥Xb∥ = 0.

By positivity, it follows that Xb = 0. Hence, null(X ⊤ X) ⊆ null(X). Together, we have null(X ⊤ X) =
null(X). Since X ⊤ X is symmetric,

                               ⊤            ⊤ ⊥              ⊥
             im X ⊤ X = im X ⊤ X     = null X ⊤ X        = null X ⊤ X     .

where the penultimate equality uses Proposition 53. Since null(X ⊤ X) = null(X),
                                                   ⊥        
                               im X ⊤ X = (null (X)) = im X ⊤ ,

where the last equality uses Proposition 53 again.                                                ■

Proof of Proposition 57. By Proposition 55, we know that a solution to the normal system exists
if X ⊤ y ∈ im(X ⊤ X). Since X ⊤ y is a linear combination of columns of X ⊤ , X ⊤ y ∈ im(X ⊤ ).
The lemma above tells us that X ⊤ y ∈ im(X ⊤ X). Hence, the solution to the normal system must
exist.                                                                                       ■

    Although we know that a solution to normal system exists, we do not know whether the solution
is unique. But, by Proposition 55 again, if X ⊤ X ∈ Rn×n is invertible, we know that the solution
must be unique. In this case, we have
                                                     −1
                                       b̂ = X ⊤ X          X ⊤y

and it also follows that
                                                           −1
                                   projW y = X X ⊤ X             X ⊤ y.
You may recall that b̂ is the OLS estimator. We see here that OLS gives us the vector in the
column space of X that minimises the distance (under the metric induced by the Euclidean norm)
between it and vector y.

3.4    Eigenvalues and eigenvectors
Recall that C is the set of complex numbers. Let Cm×n be the set of all m × n matrices whose
entries are complex numbers. Given any X ∈ Cn×n , a scalar λ ∈ C is an eigenvalue of X if

                                         ∃z ∈ Cn \{0}, Xz = λz.

   A vector z ∈ Cn \{0} is an eigenvector of X if

                                           ∃λ ∈ C, Xz = λz.

Proposition 58. λ ∈ C is an eigenvalue of X ∈ Cn×n if and only if

                                           det (λIn − X) = 0.

Proof. By definition, λ ∈ C is an eigenvalue of X if Xz = λz ⇔ (X − λIn )z = 0 has a nonzero
solution z. Since (X −λIn )z is a linear combination of the columns of X −λIn , that there is a nonzero
solution means that the columns of the matrix λIn − X are linearly dependent by Proposition 19.
By Proposition 43, this means that rank(X) < n, which implies that λIn − X is not invertible
(Proposition 47). This, in turn, is equivalent to det(λIn − X) = 0 by Proposition 51.                ■

   Since                                                                              
                                     λ − x11          −x12          ···         −x1n
                                    −x21            λ − x22        ···         −x2n 
                                                                                      
                         λIn − X =     ..              ..          ..            ..   ,
                                        .               .             .           .   
                                      −xn1            −xn2          ···        λ − xnn
the determinant of λIn − X is a polynomial in λ of degree n; i.e., ∃(ci )ni=0 ∈ Cn+1 with cn ̸= 0,
                                                           n
                                                           X               i
                                    det (λIn − X) =               ci · (λ) .
                                                           i=0

We refer to PX (λ) := det (λIn − X) as the characteristic polynomial of X. The Fundamental
Theorem of Algebra tells us that we can find all eigenvalues of X by setting the characteristic
polynomial of X to 0 and solving for all its roots.

Theorem 10 (Fundamental Theorem of Algebra). Let P : C → C be a polynomial of degree n; i.e.,
       Pn
P (λ) = i=0 ci · (λ)i for (ci )ni=0 ∈ Cn+1 with cn ̸= 0. Then, P has exactly n roots in C (counted
with multiplicity); i.e.,
                                                              n
                                                              Y
                                        n
                                 ∃ (λi )i=1 ∈ Cn , P (λ) = cn   (λ − λi )
                                                                    i=1
                                            n×n
Proposition 59. Suppose matrix X ∈ C              has eigenvalues (λi )ni=1 ∈ Cn . Then,
                                                     n
                                                     Y
                                          PX (λ) =         (λ − λi ) ,
                                                     i=1
                                                     Yn
                                         det (X) =         λi ,
                                                     i=1
                                                     Xn
                                          tr (X) =         λi .
                                                     i=1

   It follows from above that a matrix is singular (not invertible) if at least one eigenvalue is 0.

3.4.1   Geometric interpretation
Recall that X ∈ R2×2 can be thought of as mapping points to new points (except for the origin).
However, it may be that some straight lines remains fixed before and after the transformation.
That is, suppose what happens if Xz = λz for some λ ∈ R so that every point (z1 , z2 ) on a given
line is mapped to another point on the same straight line through the origin. The equation or the
direction of the straight line, z, is the eigenvector, and the corresponding value of the constant λ
(the scaling in the direction of the associated eigenvector) is the eigenvalue.
    For example, consider the following variant of the matrix that reflects about the y axis:
                                                                
                                                        −2   0
                                            X=                       .
                                                         0   1

This matrix has eigenvalues of −2 and 1 and corresponding eigenvectors of (1, 0) and (0, 1) respect-
ively. This means that X scales by a factor of 2 in the direction of the x-axis but leaves the scale
in the y-axis unchanged. Indeed, observe that

                          (0, 1) 7→ (0, 1) , (1, 0) 7→ (−2, 0) , (1, 1) 7→ (−2, 1) .

Thus, this linear mapping doubles the area and indeed |det(X)| = 2.
  Note that eigenvalue of zero means that all points on the line associated with the eigenvector is
mapped to the origin.

3.5     Diagonalisation
A matrix X ∈ Rn×n is diagonalisable (in R) if there exists an invertible matrix P ∈ Rn×n and a
diagonal matrix Λ ∈ Rn×n such that
                                        Λ = P −1 XP.
The diagonal entries of Λ = diag(λ1 , . . . , λn ) are the n eigenvalues of X since
                                                   
                          det (λIn − X) = det P −1 det (λIn − X) det (P )
                                                                
                                        = det P −1 (λIn − X) P
                                                                   
                                        = det P −1 λIn P − P −1 XP
                                           = det (λIn − Λ)
                                             Yn
                                           =    (λ − λi ) ,
                                              i=1


where, in first line, we used the fact that det(P −1 ) = 1/ det(P ). Moreover, a diagonalisable matrix
X can be decomposed as follows:
                                                    
                       X = P P −1 X P P −1 = P P −1 XP P −1 = P ΛP −1 .

Proposition 60. A matrix X ∈ Cn×n is diagonalisable in C if and only if X has n linearly
independent eigenvectors.

Proposition 61. Suppose matrix X ∈ Cn×n has n distinct eigenvalues (λi )ni=1 ∈ Cn . Then, the
corresponding eigenvectors are linearly independent.

Proposition 62. A matrix X ∈ Cn×n has n distinct eigenvalues in C, then X is diagonalisable
in C so that X = P ΛP −1 , where P is a matrix of eigenvectors and Λ is a diagonal matrix of
eigenvalues.


                                                    - 68 -
Math Review                                                                                        2024


Proof. Let pi denote eigenvectors corresponding to eigenvalue λi ; i.e.,

                                     Xpi = λi pi ∀i ∈ {1, . . . , n} .

Define P := ( p1    ...   pn ) and Λ := diag(λ1 , . . . , λn ), we can succinctly write the above set of
equation as
                                               XP = P Λ.
By the previous proposition, eigenvectors of X are linearly independent, which implies that P is
invertible. Thus, post-multiplying both sides by P −1 yields X = P ΛP −1 as desired.          ■

   An matrix X ∈ Rn×n is orthogonal if X ⊤ X = In , which, in turn, implies that X −1 = X ⊤ .

Proposition 63. A symmetric matrix X ∈ Rn×n has all real eigenvalues, and there exists an
orthogonal matrix P ∈ Rn×n and a diagonal matrix Λ ∈ Rn×n such that Λ = P −1 XP = P ⊤ XP .

Remark 5. The characterisation of when a matrix can be diagonalised is an example of spectral
theorem. More generally, a spectral theorem characterises when a linear transformation can be
diagonalised. Note that a spectrum of a linear transformation is a generalisation of the set of
eigenvalues of a matrix.

Proposition 64. A matrix X is idempotent if and only if its eigenvalues are either 0 or 1, and
the number of eigenvalues equal to 1 is tr(X).

Proof. Since X is idempotent it has to be symmetric. Suppose that X is idempotent, λ is an
eigenvalue and z ̸= 0 the corresponding eigenvector, i.e., λz = Xz, then

                             λz = Xz = XXz = X (λz) = λXz = λ2 z.

Since z ̸= 0, above implies λ = λ2 and hence either λ = 0 or λ = 1.
   Conversely, by Proposition 63, we may write X = P ⊤ ΛP , where Λ is a matrix of eigenvalues
and P is the matrix of corresponding eigenvectors. Then,

                          X 2 = P ⊤ ΛP P ⊤ ΛP = P ⊤ ΛP P −1 ΛP = P ⊤ Λ2 P,

where we used the fact that P is orthogonal. Sine each diagonal entry on Λ is either 0 or 1, it
follows that Λ2 = Λ. That is,
                                      X 2 = P ⊤ ΛP = X.
Finally,
                                                         
                            tr (X) = tr P ⊤ ΛP = tr ΛP P ⊤ = tr (Λ) .
Since all diagonal entries in Λ are 0 or 1, the number of eigenvalues equal to 1 is trace(X).         ■

Linear dynamic system         Suppose we have a linear dynamic system; i.e.,

                                              zt = Xzt−1

for some zt ∈ Rn and X ∈ Rn×n . Repeated substitution yields that

                                    zt = X (Xzt−2 ) = · · · = X t z0 .

Computing X t can be difficult generally. However, if X was diagonal, the computation is easy
because
                                                     t                        
                            (diag (x1 , . . . , xn )) = diag xt1 , . . . , xtn .


                                                  - 69 -
Math Review                                                                                             2024


Even if X was not diagonal, diagonalisation helps computation. To see this, suppose that matrix
X is diagonalisable and that there exists a matrix of eigenvectors P ∈ Rn×n and a corresponding
diagonal matrix of distinct eigenvalues Λ = diag(λt1 , . . . , λtn ) ∈ Rn×n such that X = P ΛP −1 . Then,
                                                           
                             X t = P ΛP −1 × · · · × P ΛP −1 = P Λt P −1 .

Hence,
                                                   zt = P Λt P −1 z0
so that zt is a linear combination of the initial values z0 with the weights given by the eigenvalues
and eigenvectors.
    Defining ẑt := P −1 zt , we can also write

                                ẑt = Λt ẑ0 ⇔ ẑti = λti ẑ0i ∀i ∈ {1, . . . , n} .

That is, each component of ẑt , ẑti , is described by independent equations so that it depends only
on ẑ0,i (and λi ).
   We are also often interested in the long-run behaviour of a linear dynamic system; i.e., given
zt = Xzt−1 , we wish to know
                                                                         
                    lim zt = lim X t z0 = lim P Λt P −1 z0 = P lim Λt P −1 z0 .
                     t→∞        t→∞                t→∞                            t→∞

Since Λ is a diagonal matrix whose diagonal entries are the eigenvalues of X, it follows that the
long-run behaviour of the dynamic system depends on the eigenvalues of X, (λi )ni=1 . Since Λt =
diag(λt1 , . . . , λtn ), for the long-run limit to exist, we must have that |λi | < 1 for any z0i ̸= 0, where
z0 = (z0i )ni=1 .

Example 21. Suppose we have a system of simultaneous first-order difference equations:

                                                  xt+1 = 4xt + 2yt ,
                                                  yt+1 = −xt + yt ,

with initial values x0 and y0 . We can write this in matrix form, xt+1 = Axt :

                                              ⇒ (4 − k) (1 − k) + 2 = 0
                                                       ⇒ 6 − 5k + k 2 = 0
                                                 ⇒ (k − 2) (k − 3) = 0.

Thus, eigenvalues are λ1 = 2 and λ2 = 3. The eigenvector corresponding to λ1 is
                                                              
