8.2.3   Steady state
In the steady state ẋ(t) = ẍ(t) = 0. Substituting this into the EE characterises the steady state x̄:

                              Fx (x̄, 0) + ρFy (x̄, 0) = Fyx (x̄, 0) 0 + Fyy (x̄, 0) 0
                                          ⇒ Fx (x̄, 0) = −ρFy (x̄, 0) .

8.2.4   The Maximum Principle: Hamiltonian
We use the control-state formulation. For this, we have the instantaneous return function h that
depends on the state vector x ∈ X ⊆ Rm and a control vector u ∈ U ⊆ Rn (m need not equal n).
The problem is
                                                           Z T
                                ∗
                             V (x0 ) := max          lim         e−ρt h (x (t) , u (t)) dt
                                            u(t)    T →∞     0
                                                s.t. ẋ (t) = g (x (t) , u (t)) , ∀t ≥ 0
                                                      u (t) ∈ U, ∀t ≥ 0,
                                                      x (0) given.

We will study a procedure to obtain necessary and sufficient conditions for an optimum. This
requires some regularity conditions.
   Let λ be a vector on Rm of co-state variable and H be the current-value Hamiltonian function




                                                        - 114 -
Math Review                                                                                                                      2024


defined as
                                              H (x, u, λ) := h (x, u) + λg (x, u) .
The following conditions are necessary (under regularity assumptions) and sufficient (under regu-
larity and convexity assumptions) for a path of x and u to be optimal:

                                                      Hu (x (t) , u (t) , λ (t)) = 0,                                            (8.3)
                                            ρλ (t) − Hx (x (t) , u (t) , λ (t)) = λ̇ (t) ,                                       (8.4)
                                                               g (x (t) , u (t)) = ẋ (t) ,                                      (8.5)

for all t ≥ 0.
    The state variable(s), x, has an initial value of x0 and the co-state variable(s), λ(t), have a
boundary condition—the Transversality Condition—given by

                                                    lim e−ρT λ (T ) x (T ) = 0.
                                                    T →∞

The initial value of the co-state variable, λ(0), is not predetermined and it has to be solved as part
of the system.
    The interpretation of co-state is that e−ρt λ(t) is the marginal value at time zero of an infinites-
imal increase in the state x at time t.
    The first condition (8.3) says that the derivative of the Hamiltonian with respect to the control
is zero—this gives the optimal choice of u (one for each control variable). To interpret the second
condition (8.4), recall that
                                             rP = D + Ṗ ,
can be interpreted as saying that P is the present value of D with interest rate r (D is the dividends,
Ṗ is capital gain). Here, we see that λ(t) is the present value of Hx given discount factor ρ. The
condition gives the (shadow) marginal value of a unit of x. Finally, the last condition (8.5) is the
feasibility condition.

Heuristic proof        Let us form the Lagrangian, using e−ρt λ(t) for the multiplier of ẋ(t) = g(x(t), u(t)):
                             Z                           Z                                         !
                                    T                                         T
     L (x, u, λ) = lim                  e−ρt h (x (t) , u (t)) dt +               e−ρt λ (t) [g (x (t) , u (t)) − ẋ (t)] dt .
                      T →∞      0                                         0


Consider the problem of maximising L with respect to x and u, and minimise with respect to λ.24

          Z T                                                         Z Th                          i

             0                                                            0

We therefore have
                                    Z T

                           T →∞         0
                                              Z T       h                                                     i

   Since L(x, u, λ) has to be maximised by x, the first-order conditions with respect to x(t) gives

            dL (x, u, λ)       h                                                               i

Since e−ρt > 0, it follows that

                         λ̇ (t) = ρλ (t) − hx (x (t) , u (t)) − λ (t) gx (x (t) , u (t)) .

Note that
                           dH (x, u, λ)

we therefore have that
                                         Hu (x (t) , u (t) , λ (t)) = 0.                               (8.7)

Relation to the EE analysis To see the relation with the classical EE analysis, consider the
special case:

                                                     u = ẋ,
                                              F (x, u) = h (x, u) ,
                                              g (x, u) = u.

In this case,
                                         H (x, u, λ) = F (x, ẋ) + λẋ
so that (8.7) becomes

                              Hu (x, u, λ) = 0 ⇒ Fy (x (t) , ẋ (t)) + λ (t) = 0
                                                 ⇒ λ (t) = −Fy (x (t) , ẋ (t))                        (8.8)

and
                                       Hx (x, u, λ) = Fx (x (t) , ẋ (t)) .
Therefore, (8.6) becomes
                                      λ̇ (t) = ρλ (t) − Fx (x (t) , ẋ (t)) .


                                                     - 116 -
Math Review                                                                                                     2024


Combining the two we get that

                                λ̇ (t) = −ρFy (x (t) , ẋ (t)) − Fx (x (t) , ẋ (t)) .

Thus, −λ̇ (t) is the right-hand side of the EE in (8.2). To confirm, differentiating (8.8) with respect
to t gives

                        d
           λ̇ (t) = −      Fy (x (t) , ẋ (t)) = −Fyx (x (t) , ẋ (t)) ẋ (t) − Fyy (x (t) , ẋ (t)) ẍ (t) .
                        dt
Thus, we have the EE as in (8.2):

                              Fx (x (t) , ẋ (t)) + ρFy (x (t) , ẋ (t))
                              = Fyx (x (t) , ẋ (t)) ẋ (t) + Fyy (x (t) , ẋ (t)) ẍ (t) .

8.3     Neoclassical growl model
8.3.1   Discrete time
In the discrete-time neoclassical growth model, output can be used for consumption, Ct , and in-
vestment, It :
                                        Ct + It = G (kt , 1) ,
where G(·, 1) is a production function that is strictly increasing, strictly concave and satisfies we
assume that limk→0 G′ (k, 1) = ∞ and that limk→∞ G′ (k, 1) = 0. The next-period capital is given
by
                                      kt+1 = kt (1 − δ) + It ,
where δ is the depreciation rate. Combining the two gives the law of motion:

                                       Ct = G (kt , 1) + kt (1 − δ) − kt+1
                                           = f (kt ) − kt+1 ,

where we defined f (k) := G(k, 1) + (1 − δ)k. The consumer then chooses an infinite sequence of
kt+1 to maximise utility (in any given period t, kt is given). Thus, the neoclassical growth model
takes the following form:
                                                              ∞
                                                              X
                                V ∗ (k0 ) :=     max∞                β t U (f (kt ) − kt+1 )
                                               (kt+1 )t=0
                                                               t=0

                                                        s.t. 0 ≤ kt+1 ≤ f (kt ) ,
                                                                k0 given.

We assume that U is strictly increasing and strictly concave. Moreover, we assume that limc→0 U ′ (c) =
∞ and that limc→∞ U ′ (c) = 0. In setting the lower bound for kt+1 to be zero, we are implicitly
assuming that capital can be dismantled at no cost and consumed. If capital is irreversible, then
the lower bound for kt+1 should be set to kt .
   We can fit the problem above into our general notation:

                                 F (x, y) = U (f (x) − y) , Γ (x) = [0, f (x)] .

EE and TC       Since




                                                            - 117 -
Math Review                                                                                   2024




                                    Fx (x, y) = U ′ (f (x) − y) f ′ (x) ,
                                    Fy (x, y) = −U ′ (f (x) − y) ,

the Euler Equations for the neoclassical growth model is given by:

                 −U ′ (f (kt ) − kt+1 ) + βU ′ (f (kt+1 ) − kt+2 ) f ′ (kt+1 ) = 0 ∀t ≥ 0.

The Transversality Condition is given by

                              lim β T U ′ (f (kT ) − kT +1 ) f ′ (kT ) kT = 0.
                             T →∞

                                                                 

8.3.2   Continuous time
In the continuous-time neoclassical growth model, law of motion for capital is given by

                                    k̇ (t) = f (k (t)) − c (t) − δk (t) ,

where we implicitly assume unit inelastic supply of labour as in the discrete time case. Unlike in
the discrete-time case, above f is the production function gross of depreciation. The consumer then
chooses an infinite path of k̇(t) to maximise his utility. Thus, the neoclassical growth model now
takes the following form:
                                            Z ∞                              
                       V ∗ (k (0)) := max       e−ρt U f (k) − δk (t) − k̇ (t)
                                       k̇(t)    0

                                           s.t. k̇ (t) ∈ R ∀t ≥ 0,
                                                k (0) given.

We can fit the problem above into our general notation:
                                                     
                          F k, k̇ = U f (k) − δk − k̇ , Γ (k) = R.

EE and TC      Since
                                                                   
                              Fx = (f ′ (k) − δ) U ′ f (k) − δk − k̇ ,
                                                         
                              Fy = −U ′ f (k) − δk − k̇ ,
                                                                      
                             Fyx = − (f ′ (k) − δ) U ′′ f (k) − δk − k̇ ,
                                                        
                             Fyy = U ′′ f (k) − δk − k̇ ,




                                                    - 118 -
Math Review                                                                                         2024


the EE are

                         (f ′ (k) − δ) U ′ − ρU ′ = − (f ′ (k) − δ) U ′′ k̇ + U ′′ k̈
                                                                            
                         ⇔ (f ′ (k) − δ − ρ) U ′ = − (f ′ (k) − δ) k̇ − k̈ U ′′ .                  (8.9)

The TC is given by
                                                                   
                        lim −e−ρT U ′ f (k (T )) − δk (T ) − k̇ (T ) k (T ) = 0.
                       T →∞


Steady state     The steady state is given by

                               (f ′ (k) − δ − ρ) U ′ = 0 ⇒ f ′ (k) = ρ + δ.

8.3.3   Hamiltonian
We can also analyse the neoclassical model using the Hamiltonian. The period-return function is
U (c) and the law of motion is given by k̇ = f (k) − δk − c (note that k(0) is given). That is,

                                    h (k, c) = U (c) ,
                                     g (k, c) = f (k) − δk − c,
                              ⇒ H (k, c, λ) = U (c) + λ (f (k) − δk − c) .

Then,

                                     Hu = 0 ⇒ U ′ (c) = λ,
                              λ̇ = ρλ − Hx ⇒ λ̇ = λ (ρ − (f ′ (k) − δ)) ,
                                       ẋ = g ⇒ k̇ = f (k) − δk − c.

We therefore have the following dynamic equations:

                                        λ̇ = λ (ρ − (f ′ (k) − δ)) ,
                                        k̇ = f (k) − δk − c.

Phase diagram in (k, λ) space To draw the phase diagram in (k, λ) space, note that

   • λ̇ = 0: f ′ (k̄) − δ = ρ. In (k, λ) space, this is a vertical line. Dynamics: From λ̇ = 0 if k > k̄,
     then f ′ (k) is lower and −f ′ (k) is higher so that λ̇ > 0. And if k < k̄ then λ̇ < 0.

   • k̇ = 0: c = f (k) − δk. Since λ = U ′ (c), we have that λ = U ′ (f (k) − δk). Note that U ′ > 0
     and U ′′ < 0 so that U ′ is a strictly decreasing transformation. Thus, when f (k) − δk achieves
     its maximum (at k̂ such that f ′ (k̂) = δ), U ′ (f (k) − δ) is at its minimum. As k → 0, f (k) → 0
     and U ′ (f (k) − δ) → ∞. As k > k̂, f (k̂) falls so that λ increases. These observations imply
     that k̇ = 0 locus is U-shaped in (k, λ) space. Dynamics: If c > c̄, then k̇ < 0 and if c < c̄,
     then k̇ > 0. Note that c > c̄ represents points below the k̇ = 0 locus.

This gives the following phase diagram.

8.3.4   Deriving the continuous time EE from the discrete time version
Let ∆ denote the length of time between periods when the state is determined. Decisions are taken
whenever the state is determined at times 0, ∆, 2∆, .... The sequence of state is then
                                          ∞
                                   x∆(s+1) s=0 = {x0 , x∆ , x2∆ , . . .} ,

where x0 , as usual, is given. We define the discount factor β for an interval ∆ using (the instant-

                                                    1

We let U denote the instantaneous utility from consuming ct amount of consumption. So, during
an interval of length ∆ in which consumption is given by cs∆ , the total utility is given by

                                                 ∆U (cs∆ ) .

Market clearing condition must hold at all times (including during the interval ∆). Instantaneous
market clearing condition is thus
                                        cs + is = f (ks ) ,
where is denotes investment. Market clearing condition for the interval of length ∆ from period t∆
is thus
                                  ∆cs∆ + ∆is∆ = ∆f (ks∆ ) .
Law of motion for capital is

                               ks∆+∆ = k(s+1)∆ = ∆is∆ + ks∆ (1 − ∆δ) ,

where δ is the instantaneous depreciation rate. Thus, we can write the neoclassical growth model:
as

9       Local stability of optimal paths and speed of convergence
Given an initial state x0 , the solution to a dynamic programming problem completely determines
the evolution of the state through time. The purpose of this section is to study the local dynamics
and stability of the optimal decision rules in discrete and continuous time models.

9.1     Stability of discrete-time linear dynamic systems of one dimension
9.1.1    Linearisation around the steady state
Let xt+1 = g(xt ) be the optimal decision rule (i.e., the equation that describes the saddle path).
Using first-order Taylor approximation around xt = x (x denotes the steady-state value),

                                 xt+1 = g (x) ≃ g (x) + g ′ (x) (xt − x) .

Since g(x) = x,
                                       xt+1 − x ≃ g ′ (x) (xt − x) ,

9.1.2    Condition for convergence
Suppose that x0 < x̄.

    • If |g ′ (x)| > 1, then xt+1 − x > xt − x; i.e. xt diverges.

    • If |g ′ (x)| < 1, then xt+1 − x < xt − x; i.e. xt converges.

Hence, for local stability, we require |g ′ (x)| < 1. See also the figure below.




Remark 19. If −1 < g ′ (x) < 0, then we still converge to the steady state while oscillating above
and below x. This cannot happen in continuous time case since the movements are infinitesimally
small.


                                                  - 126 -
Math Review                                                                                                2024


9.1.3   Obtaining g ′ (x)
Recall the Euler equation (which must hold for any interior solutions):

                                       0 = Fy (x, g (x)) + βFx (g (x) , g (g (x))) .

We want to find out g ′ (x), where x is the steady state; i.e. x solves

                                              Fy (x, x) + βFx (x, x) = 0.

This would allow us to analyse the dynamics of xt close to a steady state.
   We follow the following steps to obtain g ′ (x) close to a steady state.
  (i) Differentiate the Euler equation with respect to x. This yields a quadratic equation in g ′ (x).
 (ii) Evaluate the resulting quadratic equation at the steady state, x.
Differentiating the Euler equation with respect to x yields

            0 = Fyx (x, g (x)) + Fyy (x, g (x)) g ′ (x)
                   + β [Fxx (g (x) , g (g (x))) g ′ (x) + Fxy (g (x) , g (g (x))) g ′ (g (x)) g ′ (x)] .

Evaluating this derivative at the steady state x and using the fact that g(x) = x,

               0 = Fyx (x, x) + Fyy (x, x) g ′ (x)
                         h                                           i
                                                                   2
                     + β Fxx (x, x) g ′ (x) + Fxy (x, x) (g ′ (x))
                                               2
                 = βFxy (x, x) (g ′ (x)) + (Fyy (x, x) + βFxx (x, x)) g ′ (x) + Fyx (x, x)                 (9.1)

Thus, we obtain a quadratic equation in g ′ (x) so that there may potentially be two candidate values
for g ′ (x).
    Let λ = g ′ (x), we can define the quadratic equation:

                                   Q̃ (λ) := βFxy λ2 + (Fyy + βFxx ) λ + Fyx .                             (9.2)

The following proposition shows that the roots of Q̃(λ) come in almost reciprocal pairs.
Proposition 97. If λ1 solves Q̃(λ1 ) = 0, then so does λ2 = 1/λ1 β.
Proof. Suppose Q̃(λ1 ) = 0; i.e.

                                Q̃ (λ1 ) = βFxy λ21 + (Fyy + βFxx ) λ1 + Fyx = 0.

Consider Q̃(1/λ1 β):
                                                            2                       
                                 1                       1                         1
                       Q̃                  = βFxy + (Fyy + βFxx )         + Fyx
                                λ1 β                    λ1 β                      λ1 β
                                                                 
                                         1                    1
                                  = Fxy 2 + (Fyy + βFxx )           + Fyx
                                        λ1 β                 λ1 β
                                     1                                  
                                  = 2 Fxy + (Fyy + βFxx ) λ1 + βFyx λ21
                                    λ1 β
                                     1                                  
                     [Fxy = Fyx ] = 2 βFxy λ21 + (Fyy + βFxx ) λ1 + Fyx
                                    λ1 β
                                     1
                                  = 2 Q̃ (λ1 ) = 0.                                                           ■
                                    λ1 β

                                                               - 127 -
Math Review                                                                                                   2024


   Therefore, if one root is |λ1 | < 1, then the other root, λ2 , must be larger than one in absolute
value. i.e.
                                               1         1
                                      |λ2 | =      =          > 1.
                                              βλ1     β |λ1 |
As an aside, note that λ1 λ2 = 1/β.
   Let x0 be close to the steady state x so that a linear approximation of g is appropriate. Assume
we found that the smaller root has absolute value less than one. Now, consider the following
sequence of (xt+1 )t :
                                 xt+1 = x + g ′ (x) (xt − x) ∀t ≥ 0.                           (9.3)
The sequence, by construction, satisfies the Euler Equations. Since |g ′ (x)| < 1, it converges to
the steady state x and hence it also satisfies the transversality condition. Thus, if the problem is
convex, we have found a solution. If, on the other hand, both roots are bigger than one in absolute
value, then we do not know which one describes g ′ (x), but we do know that the steady state is not
locally stable (local since we are approximating).26
    We now analyse under what circumstances local stability (i.e. |g ′ (x)| < 1) can be obtained in a
one-dimensional discrete-time setting. Recall from (9.2) that

                                Q̃ (λ) := βFxy λ2 + (Fyy + βFxx ) λ + Fyx ,
                                                                           

                                               Q (0) = 1 > 0,

9.1.4   Speed of convergence
The magnitude of |g ′ (x)| describes the speed of convergence. To see this, let λ1 = g ′ (x) such that
|λ1 | < 1.We can write (9.3) as
                                       xt = x + λ1 (xt−1 − x) .
Backwards substituting yields

                                    xt = x + λ1 ((x + λ1 (xt−2 − x)) − x)
                                         = x + λ21 (xt−2 − x)
                                                ..
                                         =       .
                                         = x + λt1 (x0 − x)
                             ⇒ xt − x = λt1 (x0 − x) .

Notice that:

   • limt→∞ xt − x = 0 since limt→∞ λt1 = 0 given |λ1 | < 1;

   • as |λ1 | → 1, convergence is slower. The permanent income hypothesis case is one in which
     g ′ (k ∗ ) = 1. In this case, the convergence speed is so slow that you, in fact, do not move.



                                                      - 129 -
Math Review                                                                                         2024


   • as |λ1 | → 0, convergence is faster. If the cross derivatives, Fxy and Fyx , are zero (so that the
     roots are zero), then we would move to the steady state in the next period.

Hence, we realise that the speed of convergence is decreasing in |λ1 |, or equivalency, it is decreasing
in |g ′ (x)|.

9.1.5     Neoclassical growth model
In the neoclassical model, we have

                                        F (x, y) = U (f (x) − y)

so that

                          Fx = f ′ (x) U ′ (f (x) − y)
                          Fy = −U ′ (f (x) − y)
                                                                 2
                         Fxx = f ′′ (x) u′ (f (x) − y) + [f ′ (x)] U ′′ (f (x) − y)
                         Fyy = U ′′ (f (x) − y)
                         Fxy = −f ′ (x) U ′′ (f (x) − y) .

Recall that the steady-state capital solves 1 = βf ′ (k ∗ ) . Evaluating these at the steady-state values
and substituting into (9.1) gives

       the β discount).

                           Let x ∈ Rn and the function m : Rn → Rn u2
                                                                   define a dynamic system:
                                                                                        c                 0

                                     -1-
                                                                                               xt+1 = m (xt ) , ∀t ≥ 0.                    -1-
                                                                                    Same slope
                                              Same slope
                           Let x be a steady state; i.e.
                                                                                                         x = m (x) .
                           Consider a first-order approximation of m around x:
                                                                                                       -1-
                                                                            -1-        xt+1 = m (x) + m′ (x) (xt − x) .
                                                                    -1-
                                                                                                                                                           -1-
                           Notice that this analysis is valid globally (i.e. for all Rn ) if the system is indeed linear. Alternatively,
                           it is valid in the neighbourhood of the steady state.



                                                                                                              - 132 -

                                                                                        -1-
                                                                                           -1-
Math Review                                                                                       2024


   Since x = m(x), we can write

                                        xt+1 − x = m′ (x) (xt − x)
                                         ⇒ yt+1 = Ayt ,

where yt = xt − x and A is the matrix equal to the Jacobian m′ (x) . In this notation, the steady
state is y∗ = 0.
    Diagonalising the matrix A, we obtain

                                                A = P ΛP −1 ,

where Λ is a diagonal matrix with the eigenvalues of A, denoted by λi (possibly complex), on its
diagonal. As the notation already uses P is invertible and it contains the eigenvectors of A. We
can now write the linear system as

                                      P −1 yt+1 = ΛP −1 yt , ∀t ≥ 0.

Define zt := P −1 yt , which is a linear combination of the deviations from the steady state using the
eigenvectors P . Since P is invertible, there is a one-to-one mapping so that each z corresponds to
a unique x, and vice versa. We can then write the system as

                                   zt+1 = Λzt , ∀t ≥ 0
                              ⇒ zi,t+1 = λi zit , ∀i = 1, 2, . . . , n, ∀t ≥ 0.

We can solve this element by element to obtain that

                                 zi,t = λti zi,0 , ∀i = 1, 2, . . . , n, ∀t ≥ 0.

    Let us consider the case where all the eigenvalues are real. The following proposition says that,
if the sequence generated by the dynamic system is to converge to the steady state, then it must
be that the initial conditions x0 belong to a particular linear subspace. The dimension of this
subspace is equal to the number of eigenvalues that are larger than one in absolute value (n − m in
the notation used above).

Proposition 98. Let λi be such that, for i = 1, 2, . . . , m, we have |λi | < 1 and for i = m + 1, m +
2, . . . , n, we have |λi | ≥ 1. Thus, the eigenvalues of A are ordered so that the first m are smaller
than one. Consider the sequence

                                     xt+1 = x + A (xt − x) ∀t ≥ 0

for some initial condition x0 . Then,
                                                 lim xt = x,
                                                t→∞

if and only if the initial condition x0 satisfies

                                               x0 = P ẑ0 + x,

where ẑ0 is a vector with its n − m last coordinates equal to zero; i.e.

                                   ẑi,0 = 0 ∀i = m + 1, m + 2, . . . , n

and where the remaining elements of ẑ0 are arbitrary.


                                                    - 133 -
Math Review                                                                                                   2024


Proof. Recall that
                                     zi,t = λti zi,0 , ∀i = 1, 2, . . . , n, ∀t ≥ 0.
For any |λi | > 1 with positive initial value, notice that the sequence is exploding. Hence, in order
for the system to converge, it must be that for all i with |λi | > 1, zi,0 = 0. Recall that

                                zt = P −1 yt = P −1 (xt − x) ⇒ P zt = xt − x
                                                                              ⇒ xt = P zt + x
                                                                              ⇒ x0 = P z0 + x.

Therefore, it must be the case that x0 = P ẑ0 + x for the system to converge.                                   ■

Remark 20. If λi can be complex, then we would only consider the real part of the complex root
and the conditions are the same with respect to the real part.

Given that the system converges, it must be the case that, if |λi | ≥ 1, then µi = 0. We say that
the system is saddle path stable if not all eigenvalues are in the unit circle.

9.2.2    Log-linearisation versus linearisation
There is no rule that says how one should choose between log-linearisation or linearisation. Of
course, log-linearisation will not be appropriate if we expect the variables to be negative (e.g. the
real interest rate, the inflation rate). However, in general, the approximation tends to be more
accurate with log-linearisation if variables are positive. Another advantage of log-linearisation is
that we need not worry about the units of the variable as we can interpret in terms of percentage
changes with log-linearisation.
    If we were to linearise (9.4) around the stationary point x, then we obtain
                             m
                             X                                m
                                                              X
                               ∂Gj (x, x)                       ∂Gj (x, x)
                      0≃                    (xi,t − x∗i ) +                   (xi,t+1 − x∗i ) .
                             i=1
                                   ∂xi,t                      i=1
                                                                    ∂xi,t+1

For each t ∈ N, define an m × m matrix G̃∗t as
                                                                     
                            ∗      ∂Gj (x, x)         ∗      ∂Gj (x, x)
                          G̃1 :=                  , G̃2 :=                ,
                                     ∂xi,t     ij             ∂xi,t+1 ij

Then
                                      G̃∗1 (xt − x) + G̃∗2 (xt+1 − x) ≃ 0.
So, if G̃∗2 is invertible,
                                                 −1
                                      xt+1 − x = G̃∗2 G̃∗1 (xt − x)
                                                 = M̃ (xt − x) .

9.3     Stability of continuous-time linear dynamic systems of one dimension
This section complements the derivation of the optimal decision rules for a one-dimensional continuous-
time dynamic problem. We wish to characterise the optimal decision rule k̇ = g(k) to determine
the rate of change of the state as a function of the level of the state. In the counterpart to the
difference equation we obtained in the discrete-time case, we obtain a differential equation for the
function g in the continuous-time case.
    In the continuous-time case, we consider the state representation—i.e. a differential equation
with respect to the state k—as opposed to the standard representation of the Euler Equation as a
(second-order) differential equation on the state at its derivatives with respect to time.
    We then use g to study the local dynamics of k—i.e. dynamics of the state variable k close
to the steady state value k̄, which we summarise by g ′ (k̄). We will find an algebraic (quadratic)
equation for g ′ (k̄) and study sufficient conditions for local stability; i.e. for g ′ (k̄) < 0. We will use
the neoclassical growth model as an illustration of the general principle.
    Notation: We mainly use k instead of x in the subsection.




                                                     - 137 -
Math Review                                                                                        2024


9.3.1   General continuous-time framework
We work with the continuous-time model as defined before where we choose the derivative of the
state with respect to time, ẋ(t), in each time period to maximise:
                                                          Z T
                            V ∗ (x0 ) := max        lim         e−ρt F (x (t) , ẋ (t)) dt
                                           ẋ(t)   T →∞     0
                                                     s.t. ẋ (t) ∈ Γ (x (t)) ∀t ≥ 0,
                                                            x0 given.

   Recall that EE in the continuous time case is given by

                       Fx (x (t) , ẋ (t)) + ρFẋ (x (t) , ẋ (t))
                        = Fẋx (x (t) , ẋ (t)) ẋ (t) + Fẋẋ (x (t) , ẋ (t)) ẍ (t) , ∀t ≥ 0.   (9.6)

To simplify notation, we write EE as

                                      H (ẍ (t) , ẋ (t) , x (t)) = 0, ∀t ≥ 0,

where the function is defined in the obvious way.
   The TC is given by
                               0 = lim e−ρT Fẋ (x (T ) , ẋ (T )) x (T ) .
                                        T →∞


Time domain We first review the analysis of the problem with respect to time. We are looking
for a path of k (t)—i.e. capital as a function of time—that:

  (i) starts at the initial condition
                                                        k (0) = k0 ;

 (ii) satisfies the Euler Equations
                                                                 
                                         H k̈ (t) , k̇ (t) , k (t) = 0, ∀t > 0;                    (9.7)

(iii) converges to the unique steady state (and hence satisfies transversality)

                                                   k (t) → k̄ as t → ∞.

State domain Now we consider the analysis of the problem with respect to the state variable.
From this perspective, we are looking for a function k̇ = g(k)—i.e. the rate of change of capital as
a function of the level of capital—that:

  (i) solves the Euler Equations (note this is not a function of time here any more):

                                          H (g ′ (k) g (k) , g (k) , k) = 0, ∀k;                   (9.8)




                                                       - 138 -
Math Review                                                                                                  2024


 (ii) converges to the steady state (and hence satisfies transversality):27

                                                  g (k) > 0, if k < k̄,
                                                  g (k) < 0, if k > k̄.

To see the equivalence between (9.7) and (9.8), differentiate k̇ with respect to t, which gives

                                 dk̇   dg (k)           dk
                          k̈ =       =        = g ′ (k)    = g ′ (k) k̇ = g ′ (k) g (k) .
                                 dt      dt             dt

9.3.2    Linearisation around the steady state
Using Taylor expansion on the (non-linear) law of motion for capital k̇(t) = g(k(t)) around k = k̄:
                                                                          
                                     k̇ (t) = g (k) ≃ g k̄ + g ′ (k) k − k̄ .

Since g(k̄) = 0, we obtain the following differential equation:
                                                                      
                                               k̇ = g ′ k̄       k − k̄ .

9.3.3    Condition for convergence
Let us focus on values of k close to the steady state k̄. The condition for convergence are

                                               g (k) > 0, if k < k̄,
                                               g (k) < 0, if k > k̄,

which only need to hold in a neighbourhood of k̄. The conditions above simply say that capital
must be increasing when below the steady state, and decreasing if above the steady state. Given
this interpretation, it follows that we can write the equivalent condition for convergence as
                                                       
                                          ′
                                              ∂g k̄
                                         g k̄ ≡          < 0.                                 (9.9)
                                                    ∂k

                                         ∥g (k)∥ is decreasing in      k − k̄ .
More generally, the condition requires that there exists a function L : Rm → R+ with L (k) = 0 if and only if k = 0
and
                                              L (g (k)) g ′ (k) < 0, ∀k.

      9.3.4       Obtaining g ′ (k)
      As in the discrete time case, we use the Euler equation (in state formation) to approximate g ′ (k)
      around a steady state k̄. Recall

                                                          H (g ′ (k) g (k) , g (k) , k) = 0, ∀k.                                            (9.10)

           The steps are the same as before.

         (i) Differentiate the Euler equation with respect to x. This yields a quadratic equation in g ′ (x).

        (ii) Evaluate the resulting quadratic equation at the steady state, x.

      Differentiating (9.10) with respect to k gives
                                                            
                                                           2
                                 g ′′ (k) g (k) + (g ′ (k)) Hk̈ + g ′ (k) Hk̇ + Hk = 0.

      Evaluating this expression at -
                                    the1 -steady state k = k̄, where g(k̄) = k̇ = 0 so that g ′ (k̄)g(k̄) = k̈ = 0,
      the expression above simplifies to
                                                              2                                    
                                   Hk̈ 0, 0, k̄       g ′ k̄         + Hk̇ 0, 0, k̄ g ′ k̄ + Hk 0, 0, k̄ = 0.                               (9.11)

      Notice that this is a quadratic equation
                                         -1-   in g ′ (k̄).
         We now derive the expressions for the coefficients of the quadratic equation. Recall that
                               - 1 -                                                         
                    H k̈, k̇, k = Fk k, k̇ + ρF- 1 k̇-
                                                         k, k̇   − Fk̇k   k, k̇   k̇ − F k̇k̇   k, k̇   k̈ = 0.
                                                                                            -1-
      Differentiating this with respect to the three arguments gives
                                   
                  Hk̈ = −Fk̇k̇ k, k̇ ,                                        - 1 -- 1 -
                                                                                           

                                                                                  

Evaluating the derivatives at the steady state k = k̄ where k̇ = k̈ = 0, they simplify to
                                           
                       Hk̇k̇ = −Fk̇k̇ k̄, 0 ,
                                                                                  
                        Hk̇ = Fkk̇ k̄, 0 + ρF k̇k̇ k̄, 0 − Fk̇k k̄, 0 = ρF k̇k̇ k̄, 0 ,
                                                       
                        Hk = Fkk k̄, 0 + ρFk̇k k̄, 0 .

Hence, we can now write (9.11) as
                                         2                                                
              −Fk̇k̇ k̄, 0       g ′ k̄         + ρF k̇k̇ k̄, 0 g ′ k̄ + Fkk k̄, 0 + ρFk̇k k̄, 0 = 0.

Since the expression is quadratic, there may potentially be two candidate values for g ′ (k).
   Let λ = g ′ (x), we can define the quadratic equation:
                                                                           
                                 Q (λ) := −Fk̇k̇ λ2 + ρF k̇k̇ λ + Fkk + ρFk̇k .                         (9.12)
As in the discrete time case, the roots to Q (λ) come in almost reciprocal pairs.28

Proposition 99. If λ1 solves Q(λ1 ) = 0, then so does λ2 = −λ1 + ρ.

Proof. Suppose that λ1 solves Q(λ1 ) = 0; i.e.
                                                                      
                         Q (λ1 ) = −Fk̇k̇ λ21 + ρF k̇k̇ λ1 + Fkk + ρFk̇k = 0.

Consider Q(−λ1 + ρ):
                                  2                                  
   Q (−λ1 + ρ) = −Fk̇k̇ (−λ1 + ρ) + ρF k̇k̇ (−λ1 + ρ) + Fkk + ρFk̇k
                                                                                          
               = −Fk̇k̇ λ21 + −Fk̇k̇ ρ2 − −Fk̇k̇ 2λ1 ρ − ρF k̇k̇ λ1 + ρ2 F k̇k̇ + Fkk + ρFk̇k
                                                                   
               = −Fk̇k̇ λ21 + 2ρFk̇k̇ λ1 − ρF k̇k̇ λ1 + Fkk + ρFk̇k
                                                        
               = −Fk̇k̇ λ21 + ρFk̇k̇ λ1 + Fkk + ρFk̇k
                  = Q (λ1 ) = 0.                                                                            ■

   The theorem means that, if λ1 < 0, then λ2 > 0. Hence, if we find a solution
                                                                             
                                                Q g ′ k̄        = 0 with g ′ k̄ < 0,

then this is the solution since the other root would give that g ′ (k̄) > 0 which we know is not stable
(recall (9.9)).
    The solution satisfies the EE and TC since it converges to the steady state. The fact that it
converges to the steady state also justifies the use of the approximation (i.e. linearisation) of the
law of motion for capital since k stays in the neighbourhood of k̄. It also means that there is at most
one stable solution, which is reassuring since a convex problem should have at least one solution.
    However, if λ1 > 0 and λ2 > 0, then the system is not locally stable. In this case, local
arguments alone do not suffice to identify which one of the roots of Q is the solution for g ′ (k̄), but
we know that one of them gives the value of g ′ (k̄).
    We now analyse under what circumstances local stability (i.e. g ′ (k̄) < 0) can be obtained in a
one-dimensional continuous-time setting. Recall from (9.12) that
                                                                           
                                 Q (λ) := −Fk̇k̇ λ2 + ρF k̇k̇ λ + Fkk + ρFk̇k .
 28 Reciprocal here means that one root is positive and the other is negative (ignoring the ρ term).

Remark 21. The previous proposition also holds for higher dimensions. If the state is of dimension
m, then there will be 2m roots satisfying

                                   λm+i = −λi + ρ, ∀i = 1, 2, . . . , m.

In the m dimensional case, the roots are not simply ∂gi /∂ki . The roots are eigenvalues of the
matrix of the derivatives of g. In the m dimensional case, we have

                                       k̇i = gi (k) , ∀i = 1, 2, . . . , m,

or, in vector notation,
                                                     
                          k̇ = k̇1 , k̇2 , . . . , k̇m = g (k) = g (k1 , k2 , . . . , km )

and
                          g (k1 , k2 , . . . , km ) = (g1 (k) , g2 (k) , . . . , gm (k)) .
Then, G′ (k) is the matrix
                                                ∂g1            ∂g1          ∂g1
                                                                                   
                                                 ∂k1            ∂k2   ···    ∂km
                                                ∂g2            ∂g2
                                                                      ···    ∂g2   
                                                ∂k1            ∂k2          ∂km   
                                 G′ (k) = 
                                                 ..             ..   ..      ..   .
                                                                                   
                                                  .              .      .     .   
                                                 ∂gm            ∂gm          ∂gm
                                                 ∂k1            ∂k2   ···    ∂km


                                                       - 142 -

The eigenvalues of the matrix above control the (local) behaviour of k around k̄.

9.3.5    Speed of convergence
The magnitude of |g ′ (k̄)| describes the speed of convergence. To see this, recall the linearised law
of motion:
                                                           
                                          k̇ = g ′ k̄ k − k̄ .
Rewriting this as
                                        dk                     
                                           = −g ′ k̄ k̄ + g ′ k̄ k,
                                        dt
which is differential equation in k.

Solving the differential equation          To solve this, guess that the solution is of the form
                                                          
                                        k (t) = exp g ′ k̄ t c (t) .

Then,

                         dk (t)                                            
                                = g ′ k̄ exp g ′ k̄ t c (t) + c′ (t) exp g ′ k̄ t
                          dt
                                                                 
                                = g ′ k̄ k (t) + c′ (t) exp g ′ k̄ t .

Substituting this into the expression for dk/dt,
                                                                                
                       −g ′ k̄ k̄ + g ′ k̄ k (t) = g ′ k̄ k (t) + c′ (t) exp g ′ k̄ t
                                                                          
                                        ⇔ c′ (t) = −g ′ k̄ k̄ exp −g ′ k̄ t .

Integrating both sides with respect to t
                            Z             Z
                                                                
                               c′ (t) dt = −g ′ k̄ k̄ exp −g ′ k̄ t dt
                                                       
                               ⇒ c (t) = k̄ exp −g ′ k̄ t + C.

Hence,
                                                                  
                              k (t) = exp g ′ k̄ t k̄ exp −g ′ k̄ t + C
                                                     
                                    = k̄ + exp g ′ k̄ t C.

To pin down C, we need a boundary condition, which is that k (0) = k0 .

                                       k0 = k̄ + C ⇔ C = k̄ − k0 .

Thus, we obtain that
                                                                 
                                   k (t) = k̄ + k̄ − k0 exp g ′ k̄ t .

Math Review                                                                                                                       2024


Using integrating factor We can solve this first-order differential equation using an integrating
factor.29 The integrating factor is then given by
                                                         Z t              
                                                                                        
                                    I (t) = exp                  −g ′ k̄ dt = exp −g ′ k̄ t .

The solution is then given by
                                                                  Z t

                           exp −g ′ k̄ t k (t) =                      −g ′ k̄ k̄ exp −g ′ k̄ t dt + c
                                                                                 
                                                               = k̄ exp −g ′ k̄ t + c.

Initial condition is that k (0) = k0 . Notice that if t = 0, then above expression simplifies to

                                                     k0 = k̄ + c ⇒ c = k0 − k̄.

This gives us a linearised version of the law of motion for capital:30
                                                                          
                               exp −g ′ k̄ t k (t) = k̄ exp −g ′ k̄ t + k0 − k̄
                                                                           
                                           ⇒ k (t) = k̄ + k0 − k̄ exp g ′ k̄ t .
                                                                           
Thus, the if g ′ k̄ < 0, then k (t) → k̄. Moreover, the more negative g ′ k̄ , the faster is the
convergence.

9.3.6    Neoclassical growth model
Recall that                                                  
                                    F k, k̇ = U f (k) − δk − k̇



                                                 - 145 -
Math Review                                                                                          2024


so that
                                                  
                                         Fk k, k̇ = (f ′ (k) − δ) U ′ ,
                                                  
                                         Fk̇ k, k̇ = −U ′ ,
                                                  
                                        Fk̇k k, k̇ = − (f ′ (k) − δ) U ′′ ,
                                                  
                                        Fk̇k̇ k, k̇ = U ′′ ,

where we add one extra derivative
                                                                        2
                                     Fkk = f ′′ (k) U ′ + (f ′ (k) − δ) U ′′ .
                                                                         
Evaluating them at the steady state k = k̄ using the fact that ρ = f ′ k̄ − δ gives
                                                            
                                      Fk k̄, 0 = f ′ k̄ − δ U ′ = ρU ′ ,
                                                
                                      Fk̇ k̄, 0 = −U ′ ,

                                                
                                     Fk̇k̇ k̄, 0 = U ′′ ,
                                                         
                                     Fkk k̄, 0 = f ′′ k̄ U ′ + ρ2 U ′′ .

                                                                                         
                       Q (λ) = (−U ′′ ) λ2 + (ρU ′′ ) λ + f ′′ U ′ + ρ2 U ′′ − ρ2 U ′′
                              = (−U ′′ ) λ2 + (ρU ′′ ) λ + (f ′′ U ′ )

9.4     Stability of continuous-time linear dynamic systems of higher dimen-
        sions
In continuous time and with higher dimensions, we would have

                                                 ẋ (t) = m (x (t)) , ∀t ≥ 0.

At the steady state,
                                                          0 = m (x) .
Linear approximation of ẋ(t) yields

                                   ẋ (t) = m (x (t)) ≃ m (x) + m′ (x) (x (t) − x)
                                                          ≃ m′ (x) (x (t) − x) .

Define A := m′ (x) and y(t) := x(t) − x so that we may write

                                                        ẏ (t) = Ay (t) .

    As before, we can diagonalise A as A = P ΛP −1 and define z(t) := P −1 y (t) to obtain

                                                  ż (t) = Λz (t) , ∀t ≥ 0,

which can be written as
                                    żi (t) = λi zi (t) , ∀i = 1, 2 . . . , n, ∀t ≥ 0, .
This has the solution:31
                                    zi (t) = eλi t zi (0) , ∀i = 1, 2 . . . , n, ∀t ≥ 0.
  31 To see this, rearrange

                                                                 żi (t)   d
                                                          λi =           =    [ln zi (t)]
                                                                 zi (t)    dt
                                             Z
                                         ⇒       λi dt = λi t = ln zi (t) + C

                                                    ⇒ zi (t) = eλi t e−C .

Given the initial condition zi (0), e−C = zi (0) .




                                                            - 148 -
Math Review                                                                                      2024



      Proposition 100. Let λi be such that for i = 1, 2, . . . , m, we have λi < 0 and for
      i = m + 1, m + 2, . . . , n, we have λi ≥ 0. Thus, the eigenvalues of A are ordered so that
      the first m are negative. Consider the sequence

                                         ẋ = A (x (t) − x) , ∀t ≥ 0

      for some initial condition x(0). Then

                                                 lim x (t) = x
                                                t→∞

      if and only if the initial condition x(0) satisfies

                                             x (0) = P ẑ (0) + x,

      where ẑ(0) is a vector with its n − m last coordinates equal to zero; i.e.

                                  ẑi (0) = 0, ∀i = m + 1, m + 2, . . . , n

      and where the remaining elements of ẑ(0) are arbitrary.


Proof. Recall that
                              zi (t) = eλi t zi (0) , ∀i = 1, 2 . . . , n, ∀t ≥ 0.
For any λi > 0 with positive initial value, the sequence is exploding. Hence, in order for the system
to converge, it must be that, for all i with λi > 0, zi (0) = 0. Recall that

                      z (t) = P −1 y (t) = P −1 (x (t) − x) ⇒ P z (t) = (x (t) − x)
                                                               ⇒ x (t) = P z (t) + x
                                                               ⇒ x (0) = P z (0) + x.

Therefore, it must be the case that x (0) = P ẑ (0) + x for the system to converge.                ■

Remark. Once again, if λi can be complex, we only consider the real part of the complex root and
the conditions are the same with respect to the real part.

9.5    Saddle path for linearised dynamics
Consider a continuous-time problem with state x, controls u, objective function h, law of motion g
and discount factor ρ as done in the notes before. The Hamiltonian is

                                   H (x, u, λ) = h (x, u) + λg (x, u) ,

where λ is the co-state variable. Let x, u, λ ∈ R. The first-order conditions for the Hamiltonian are:

                                          0 = Hu (x, u, λ) ,
                                          λ̇ = ρλ − Hx (x, u, λ) ,
                                          ẋ = g (x, u) .

Using Hu = 0 to solve for u = µ (x, λ) as a function of x and λ, we get

                                          Hu (x, µ (x, λ) , λ) = 0.


                                                   - 149 -
Math Review                                                                                        2024


We then obtain the two dimensional dynamic system:

                                      λ̇ = ρλ − Hx (x, µ (x, λ) , λ) ,
                                      ẋ = g (x, µ (x, λ)) .

Linearising this system, we obtain

                                     dt         =                                     ,

and Θ is a diagonal matrix with the eigenvalues of A, denoted by θi in its diagonal.
    Let us assume that θ1 < 0 < θ2 . For the system to converge to the steady state, we must have

                                                       "        #

                      ϕ′ =      =       ≡           =

                                             dλ       λ̇ (λ, x)

                                     λ̇ = ρλ − Hx (x, µ (x, λ) , λ) ,

                                                                 

One of the two roots of this quadratic equation is the slope of the saddle path. Unlike when we
were analysing convergence, there is no “rule” as to which root will represent the stable steady state.
Thus, when using this method, we would usually plot the phase diagram to know the slope of the
saddle path from the diagram, and pick the appropriate root using the equation above accordingly.
    We can also find the slope of the optimal control rule setting the control as a function of the
state. Let
                                        u∗ (x) = µ (x, ϕ (x)) .

10       Principle of Optimality and Dynamic Programming
Bellman’s Principle of Optimality provides conditions under which a programming problem ex-
pressed in sequence form is equivalent (as defined below) to a two-period recursive programming
problem (called the Bellman equation). The first part of the note discusses this relation and intro-
duces some concepts and techniques used to solve dynamic programming problems in discrete time.
The last part of the note focuses on continuous-time dynamic programming problems and shows
how they relate to the Maximum Principle.

10.1     Principle of Optimality
10.1.1    Recursive problem
Recall that the sequence problem is given by:
                                                         ∞
                                                         X
                               ∗
                             V (x0 ) :=     max                β t F (xt , xt+1 )
                                          (xt+1 )∞
                                                 t=0
                                                         t=0

                                                   s.t. xt+1 ∈ Γ (xt ) ∀t ≥ 0,
                                                           x0 given.

Equivalently, we can write the problem as

                                    V ∗ (x0 ) =        max       u ((xt+1 )) ,                      (10.1)
                                                  x∞ ∈Π∞ (x0 )


where

                                xt := (x0 , x1 , . . . , xt ) ,
                                      
                           Πt (x0 ) := xt : xs+1 ∈ Γ (xs ) , s = 0, 1, . . . , t − 1 ,
                                             T
                                             X
                        u ((xt+1 )) = lim          β t F (xt , xt+1 ) .
                                      T →∞
                                             t=0

We think of xt as the set of states from period 0 to t, Πt (x0 ) as the set of all possible states from
period 0 to period t (thus, Πt (x0 ) is an increasing set as t increases), and u((xt+1 )) is the discounted
(infinite) sum of the utility.

10.1.2    Bellman equation
The Bellman equation, which is a recursive problem, is to find a function V : X → R such that

                              V (x) = max [F (x, y) + βV (y)] ∀x ∈ X.                               (10.2)
                                        y∈Γ(x)


This is a functional equation in which the solution is a function V that must satisfy the equation
above for all x ∈ X. Let g(x) denote the maximiser of the right-hand side of (10.2). Then, g(x)
satisfies
                                 V (x) = F (x, g (x)) + βV (g (x)) .
If the function V were known (or if we know properties of it), then (10.2) is a two-period problem.




                                                       - 155 -
Math Review                                                                                                      2024


10.1.3       Principle of Optimality
The Principle of Optimality states that

                                                V ∗ (x) = V (x) ∀x ∈ X.

That is, the solution to the two-period problem in (10.2) is equivalent to the infinite-dimension
problem in (10.1). Once we have V and g, then we have the solution of (10.1) for any initial
condition x0 ∈ X.

Theorem 22. Suppose Γ(x) is nonempty for all x ∈ X and that, for all x0 ∈ X and all x∞ ∈
                   PT
Π∞ (x0 ), limT →∞ t=0 β t F (xt , xt+1 ) exists in R. Then, V ∗ satisfies (10.2). Moreover, if V is a
solution to (10.2) and
                           lim β T v (xT ) = 0 ∀x∞ ∈ Π∞ (x0 ) ∀x0 ∈ X,
                                T →∞
         ∗
then V = V .

    Here, we sketch the basic reasoning behind the Principle of Optimality (this is not a rigorous
proof!). Take the case where F is bounded so |F (x, y)| ≤ B < ∞ for all (y, x) ∈ gr(Γ). Notice that,
for any x∞ ∈ Π∞ (x0 ), since β ∈ (0, 1),

                                   T
                                   X                                       T
                                                                           X              B
                            lim             β t |F (xt , xt+1 )| ≤ lim           βtB =       .
                           T →∞

That is,
                                                                      B

Splitting the infinite sum into the first T − 1 periods and the remaining periods:
                                        T −1
                                                                          "∞                        #

                     u ((xt+1 )) =             β t F (xt , xt+1 ) + β T          β t−T F (xt , xt+1 ) .

Then, we can write
                                T
