Since β T can be made arbitrarily small by choosing T sufficiently large, we can approximate
                                       PT −1
u((xt+1 ))—i.e. the value of a plan—by t=0 β t F (xt , xt+1 ) for sufficiently large T .
   By definition, (10.2) means that V (x0 ) is the maximum value such that

                          V (x0 ) ≥ F (x0 , x1 ) + βV (x1 ) , ∀ (x0 , x1 ) ∈ Π1 (x0 )                           (10.3)

and for some (x0 , x∗1 ) ∈ Π1 (x0 ), the above holds with equality; i.e.

                                            V (x0 ) = F (x0 , x∗1 ) + βV (x∗1 ) .

Since (10.2) holds for all x ∈ X, it must also be the case that

                        V (x∗1 ) ≥ F (x∗1 , x2 ) + βV (x2 ) , ∀ (x0 , x∗1 , x2 ) ∈ Π2 (x0 ) .




                                                           - 156 -
Math Review                                                                                                2024


Substituting into (10.3),

               V (x0 ) ≥ F (x0 , x∗1 ) + βV (x∗1 )
                       ≥ F (x0 , x∗1 ) + β [F (x∗1 , x2 ) + βV (x2 )]
                       = F (x0 , x∗1 ) + βF (x∗1 , x2 ) + β 2 V (x2 ) , ∀ (x0 , x∗1 , x2 ) ∈ Π2 (x0 ) .

Again, there exists some (x0 , x∗1 , x∗2 ) ∈ Π2 (x0 ) such that

                             V (x0 ) = F (x0 , x∗1 ) + βF (x∗1 , x∗2 ) + β 2 V (x∗2 ) .

Continuing in a similar fashion, we get
                                    T
                                    X −1
                        V (x0 ) ≥          β t F (xt , xt+1 ) + β T V (xT ) ∀xT ∈ ΠT (x0 )
                                    t=0


and, for some xT ∈ ΠT (x0 ),

                                              T
                                              X −1
                                V (x0 ) =            β t F (xt , xt+1 ) + β T V (xT ) .                   (10.4)
                                               t=0


   Since |F | ≤ B, |V | ≤ 1−β
                           B
                              and so

                                                lim β T V (xT ) = 0.
                                               T →∞

Thus, we can approximate the value of V (x0 ) arbitrarily well by choosing T sufficiently large; i.e.
we conclude that
                                      V (x0 ) = V ∗ (x0 ) .

10.2     Bounded Dynamic Programming
We now study the Bellman equation for the case where F is bounded.
   Let (S, ρ) be a metric space and let T : S → S be a self-map. The self-map T is a contraction
with modulus β ∈ (0, 1) if
                                 ρ (T x, T y) ≤ βρ (x, y) ∀x, y ∈ S.
Thus, T is a contraction if it “shrinks” the distance between any two points by more than the
fraction β.
Remark 23. Let S = [0, 1] so that x and y are scalars between zero and one. Let ρ(x, y) = |x − y|
and T be a function. Then, for T to be a contraction with modulus β, it must be that

                                              |T (x) − T (y)| ≤ β |x − y|
                                              |T (y) − T (x)|
                                          ⇒                   ≤ β.
                                                  |y − x|

Hence, this says that the slope of the function T is less than β (and β is the Lipschitz constant).
The key here is the following fix-point theorem.

Theorem 23. (Contraction Mapping Theorem/Banach) If T is a contraction in a metric space
(S, ρ) with modulus β, then:




                                                         - 157 -
Math Review                                                                                  2024


  (i) there is a unique fixed point s∗ ∈ S,

                                                    s∗ = T (s∗ ) ;

 (ii) iterations of T converge to the fixed point

                                     ρ (T n s0 , s∗ ) ≤ β n ρ (s0 , s∗ ) , ∀s0 ∈ S,

     where T n+1 s = T (T n s).

   In our main application, S will be the set of continuous and bounded functions from X to R:
                                                                             
                S := f : X → R, f is continuous, and ∥f ∥ ≡ sup |f (x)| < ∞
                                                                           x∈X

and the metric is the sup norm:

                              ρ (f, g) ≡ ∥f − g∥ := sup |f (x) − g (x)| .
                                                         x∈X

The following proposition allows us to determine if something is a contraction.

Proposition 101. (Blackwell Sufficient Conditions) Let S be the space of bounded functions of X
and ∥·∥ the sup norm. Let T : S → S. Then, T is a contraction if

  (i) T is monotone; i.e. T f (x) ≤ T g (x) for any x ∈ X and g, f such that f (x) ≤ g (x) for all
      x ∈ X;

 (ii) T discounts; i.e. there exists β ∈ (0, 1) such that, for any a ∈ R+ ,

                                  T (f + a) (x) ≤ T f (x) + aβ, ∀x ∈ X, f ∈ S.

Proof. First, we can always write
                                               f − g = f − g.
By the definition of sup norm (∥f (x)∥ = sup |f (x)|):

                                           f − g ≤ ∥f − g∥
                                            ⇒ f ≤ g + ∥f − g∥ .

Since T is monotone, then
                                          T f ≤ T (g + ∥f − g∥) .
Since T discounts, setting a = ∥f − g∥, we can write

                                                T f ≤ T (g + ∥f − g∥)
                                                    ≤ T g + β ∥f − g∥
                                      ⇒ T f − T g ≤ β ∥f − g∥ .

   Of course, we can reverse the roles of f and g to obtain that

                                                T g ≤ T f + β ∥g − f ∥
                                                    = T f + β ∥f − g∥
                                      ⇒ T g − T f ≤ β ∥f − g∥ .


                                                    - 158 -
Math Review                                                                                                 2024


Combining both inequalities,
                                          ∥T f − T g∥ ≤ β ∥f − g∥ ;
i.e. T is a contraction.                                                                                       ■

   We define the Bellman operator T as

                                    (T v) (x) = max [F (x, y) + βv (y)] .                                  (10.5)
                                                 y∈Γ(x)


Theorem 24. Assume that F is bounded and continuous and that Γ is continuous and has range
that is compact. Let T be the Bellman operator as defined in (10.5). Then, T maps the set of
continuous and bounded functions S onto itself. Moreover, T is a contraction.

Proof. That T maps the set of continuous and bounded follow from the Theorem of the Maximum
which we will study in ECON 6701. That T is a contraction follows since T satisfies the Blackwell
conditions:

  (i) Monotonicity. For f ≥ v,

                                (T v) (x) = max [F (x, y) + βv (y)]
                                             y∈Γ(x)

                                          = F (x, g (x)) + βv (g (x))
                                          ≤ F (x, g (x)) + βf (g (x)) ∵ f ≥ v
                                          ≤ max [F (x, y) + βf (y)] = (T f ) (x) ,
                                             y∈Γ(x)


      where the last inequality comes from the fact that T f (x) is the maximum.

 (ii) Discounts. For a > 0,

                                (T (v + a)) (x) = max [F (x, y) + β (v (y) + a)]
                                                      y∈Γ(x)

                                                  = max [F (x, y) + βv (y)] + βa
                                                      y∈Γ(x)

                                                  = (T v) (x) + βa.                                            ■

     We mentioned previously that (S, ρ) is a metric space. Moreover, this space is also complete;
i.e., every Cauchy sequence is convergent.

Corollary 10. Let S be a complete metric space and S ′ ⊂ S be closed. Let T be a contraction on
S and s∗ = T s∗ . If T S ′ ⊂ S ′ (i.e. if s′ ∈ S ′ , then T (s′ ) ∈ S ′ ), then s∗ ∈ S ′ . Moreover, if S ′′ ⊂ S ′
and T S ′ ⊂ S ′′ (i.e. if s′ ∈ S ′ , then T (s′ ) ∈ S ′′ ), then s∗ ∈ S ′′ .

    The first statement of Corollary 10 means that, if applying the operator T to any point in a
closed set S ′ means that the “output” stays within the set, then the fixed point must also be in that
set. The second means that, if applying the operator T to any point in S ′ means that the output
is in the set S ′′ , then the fixed point must also be in S ′′ . Note that S ′′ need not be closed, whereas
S ′ must be closed. Corollary 10 is useful in establishing the properties of the value function V and
the optimal policy g.
    In what follows, we maintain the following set of assumptions.

Assumption 1. X is a convex subset of Rn , Γ(x) is nonempty, compact for all x ∈ X, Γ is
continuous, and F is bounded and continuous, and β ∈ (0, 1).



                                                      - 159 -
Math Review                                                                                             2024


Theorem 25 (Monotonicity). In addition to Assumption 1, assume that F (·, y) is increasing and
that Γ is increasing (i.e. Γ(x) ⊆ Γ(x′ ) for x ≤ x′ ). Then, the fixed point v ∗ satisfying v ∗ = T v ∗ is
increasing. If F (·, y) is strictly increasing, so is v ∗ .

Proof. By Corollary 10, it suffices to show that T f is increasing if f is increasing. Let x ≤ x′ :

                                     (T f ) (x) = max [F (x, y) + βf (y)]
                                                   y∈Γ(x)

                                                = F (x, g (x)) + βf (g (x))
                                                ≤ F (x′ , g (x)) + βf (g (x))

since F (·, y) is increasing (the inequality will hold strictly if F (·, y) is strictly increasing). But by
definition,

                                     (T f ) (x) ≤ F (x′ , g (x)) + βf (g (x))
                                                ≤ F (x′ , g (x′ )) + βf (g (x′ ))
                                                = max′ [F (x′ , y) + βf (y)]
                                                   y∈Γ(x )

                                                = (T f ) (x′ ) .                                          ■

Theorem 26 (Concavity). In addition to Assumption 1, assume and Γ is convex,33 and that F is
concave in (x, y). Then, the fixed point v ∗ satisfying v ∗ = T v ∗ is concave in x. Moreover, if F is
strictly concave, so is v ∗ and the optimal policy correspondence is a single-valued.

Proof. By Corollary 10, it suffices to show that T f is concave if f is concave. That is, we wish to
show that
                               
                     (T f ) xθ ≥ (1 − θ) (T f ) (x′ ) + θ (T f ) (x) , ∀θ ∈ (0, 1) .
   Since F and f are concave then, for any x, x′ ∈ X,
                                            
                                  F xθ , y θ ≥ (1 − θ) F (x′ , y ′ ) + θF (x, y) ,
                                            
                                    f y θ ≥ (1 − θ) f (y ′ ) + θf (y) ,

for all y ∈ Γ (x) and y ′ ∈ Γ (x′ ). Summing the two while multiplying the second by β:
                             
           F xθ , y θ + βf y θ ≥ (1 − θ) F (x′ , y ′ ) + θF (x, y) + β [(1 − θ) f (y ′ ) + θf (y)]
                                     = (1 − θ) [F (x′ , y ′ ) + βf (y ′ )] + θ [F (x, y) + βf (y)]

for all θ ∈ (0, 1). Above holds when y = g (x) and y ′ = g (x′ ); i.e.
                        
      F xθ , y θ + βf y θ ≥ (1 − θ) [F (x′ , g (x′ )) + βf (g (x′ ))] + θ [F (x, g (x)) + βf (g (x))]
                                = (1 − θ) (T f ) (x′ ) + θ (T f ) (x) .
 33 That is, for any y ∈ Γ(x) and y ′ ∈ Γ(x′ ), we have


                            θy ′ + (1 − θ) y ∈ Γ θx′ + (1 − θ) x        ∀x, x′ ∈ X ∀θ ∈ (0, 1) .
                                                                    




                                                          - 160 -
Math Review                                                                                       2024


   By definition,
                                                         
                           (T f ) xθ = max F xθ , y + βf (y)
                                          y∈Γ(xθ )
                                                                
                                       = F xθ , g xθ + βf g xθ
                                                                      
                                       ≥ F xθ , y θ + βf y θ ∀y θ ∈ Γ xθ .

Hence,
                                
                       (T f ) xθ ≥ (1 − θ) (T f ) (x′ ) + θ (T f ) (x) ∀θ ∈ (0, 1) .                 ■

Theorem 27 (Differentiability). In addition to Assumption 1, assume that F (x, y) is strictly con-
cave in (x, y), Γ is convex, and F is continuously differentiable on int(gr(Γ)). If x0 ∈ int(X)
and g(x0 ) ∈ int(Γ(x0 )). Then, the fixed point v ∗ satisfying v ∗ = T v ∗ is differentiable at x0 with
derivatives given by
                                       ∂F
                            vi (x0 ) =     (x0 , g (x0 )) ∀i ∈ {1, 2, . . . , n}
                                       ∂xi
where X ⊆ Rn .

10.2.1   Envelope: Differentiability of the value function
Let us work out a heuristic argument to find an expression for the derivative of the value function.
We will assume that V is differentiable and that the policy function g is also differentiable with
respect to x. Assume that (y, x) ∈ int(gr(Γ)).
   First, the first-order condition of the problem

                                          max F (x, y) + βV (y)
                                            y


evaluated at the optimum, y = g(x), is

                                     Fy (x, g (x)) + βV ′ (g (x)) = 0.

   Now, differentiating both sides of

                                    V (x) = F (x, g (x)) + βV (g (x))

with respect to x gives

                    V ′ (x) = Fx (x, g (x)) + Fy (x, g (x)) g ′ (x) + βV ′ (g (x)) g ′ (x)
                           = Fx (x, g (x)) + (Fy (x, g (x)) + βV ′ (g (x)))g ′ (x) .            (10.6)
                                             |             {z             }
                                                               =0∵FOC

Hence,
                                          V ′ (x) = Fx (x, g (x)) .
This is called the envelope condition.
Remark 24. The formal proof (Benveniste and Scheinkman Theorem) requires that V is concave,
F (·, y) ∈ C 1 and that (g(x), x) ∈ int(gr(Γ)). Strictly speaking, the theorem does not require g to
be differentiable.




                                                     - 161 -
Math Review                                                                                               2024


10.2.2    First-order and the envelope conditions
The first-order and the envelope conditions are respectively given by

                                          0 = Fy (x, g (x)) + βV ′ (g (x)) ,
                                   V ′ (x) = Fx (x, g (x))

for all x such that (g(x), x) ∈ int(gr(Γ)). Notice that combining the two gives the familiar Euler
Equation:
                              0 = Fy (x, g (x)) + βFx (g (x) , g (g (x))) .

10.2.3    Neoclassical growth model
The Bellman equation for the Neoclassical problem is given by

                                V (k) =      max          [U (f (k) − k ′ ) + βV (k ′ )] .
                                          k′ ∈[0,f (k)]


Thus, the first-order condition evaluated at the optimal k ′ = g (k) is

                                     U ′ (f (k) − g (k)) = βV ′ (g (k)) .

The envelop condition, evaluated at the optimal, is given by

                                    V ′ ((k)) = U ′ (f (k) − g (k)) f ′ (k) .

Exercise 22. Show that the neoclassical growth model satisfy the assumptions of Theorem 25.

Exercise 23. Show that the neoclassical growth model satisfy the assumptions of Theorem 26.

10.3     Continuous-time Bellman equation
Consider the following discrete-time Bellman equation,
                                                                        
                                                           1
                          V (xt ) = max ∆h (xt , ut ) +        V (xt+∆ )
                                    ut ∈U               1 + ∆ρ

subject to
                                           xt+∆ = xt + ∆g (xt , ut ) .
We will analyse the continuous-time Bellman equation as a limit of the discrete one.
   Notice that, if we simply take the limit as ∆ goes to zero, we are simply left with V (xt ) = V (xt ),
which is not very useful. Using Taylor expansion (around xt ), we can write

                       V (xt+∆ ) = V (xt + ∆g (xt , ut ))
                                  = V (xt ) + V ′ (xt ) ∆g (xt , ut ) + o (∆g (xt , ut )) ,

where d(z) = o(z) means that limz→0 d(z)/z = 0. Then the Bellman equation is
                                                                                                
                                        1               ′
       V (xt ) = max ∆h (xt , ut ) +        (V (xt ) + V (xt ) ∆g (xt , ut ) + o (∆g (xt , ut )))
                 ut ∈U               1 + ∆ρ

Multiplying both sides by the positive constant 1 + ∆ρ yields

   (1 + ∆ρ) V (xt ) = max [(1 + ∆ρ) ∆h (xt , ut ) + V (xt ) + V ′ (xt ) ∆g (xt , ut ) + o (∆g (xt , ut ))] .
                        ut ∈U


                                                           - 162 -
Math Review                                                                                            2024


We can move V (xt ) inside the max to the left-hand side since it does not depend on ut ,

           ∆ρV (xt ) = max [(1 + ∆ρ) ∆h (xt , ut ) + V ′ (xt ) ∆g (xt , ut ) + o (∆g (xt , ut ))] .
                         ut ∈U

Dividing both sides by ∆,
                                                                                            
                                                     ′                     o (∆g (xt , ut ))
             ρV (xt ) = max (1 + ∆ρ) h (xt , ut ) + V (xt ) g (xt , ut ) +                     .
                        ut ∈U                                                    ∆

Taking the limit as ∆ goes to zero,

                             ρV (xt ) = max [h (xt , ut ) + V ′ (xt ) g (xt , ut )] ,
                                         ut ∈U

where we implicitly assume that the limit as ∆ → 0 of the max with respect to ut is the same
as the max with respect to ut of the limit as ∆ → 0. Removing the time indices, we obtain the
continuous-time version of the Bellman equation,

                                 ρV (x) = max [h (x, u) + V ′ (x) g (x, u)] .                         (10.7)
                                            u∈U

Under the regularity conditions, the max of the RHS can be characterised using the following
first-order condition for u:

                                 0 = hu (x, u∗ (x)) + V ′ (x) gu (x, u∗ (x)) ,

which defines the optimal decision rule u∗ (x). Thus, the following two equations summarise the
dynamic programming problem:

                             ρV (x) = h (x, u∗ (x)) + V ′ (x) g (x, u∗ (x)) ,                         (10.8)
                                                  ∗             ′            ∗
                                    0 = hu (x, u (x)) + V (x) gu (x, u (x))

for all x ∈ X. Notice that these are two functional equations (i.e. solutions are functions). The
functions V and u∗ are both functions of x.

10.4     Bellman equation and the Maximum Principle
We now show the sense in which the Bellman equation and the first-order conditions above imply
the equations for the Maximum Principle (i.e. Hamiltonian) derived previously.
   Recall that in the optimal-control approach, u∗ (t) maximises

                                     H (x, u, λ) = h (x, u) + λg (x, u) .

From (10.7), in the dynamic programming approach, u∗ (t) maximises

                                         h (x, u) + V ′ (x) g (x, u) .                                (10.9)

Hence, the two approaches are consistent only if

                                                  λ ≡ V ′ (x) ;

i.e. the co-state in the calculus of variations approach is the derivative of the value function. This
is consistent with the interpretation for the discounted value of the co-state variables offered before:
the marginal value of an extra unit of the state variable.


                                                      - 163 -
Math Review                                                                                   2024


   Second, using that λ ≡ V ′ (x), we can see that the first-order condition from optimal-control
approach
                                        Hu (x, u, λ) = 0
is equivalent to
                                         hu (x, u) + λgu (x, u) = 0,
which is the derivative of (10.9) with respect to u set to zero.
   Third, differentiating (10.8) with respect to time yields

                         ρV ′ ẋ = hx ẋ + hu u∗′ ẋ + (V ′′ g + V ′ gx + V ′ gu u∗′ ) ẋ
                                                                                

                                = hx ẋ + (V ′′ g + V ′ gx ) ẋ + hu + V ′ gu  u∗′ ẋ
                                                                   | {z }
                                                                        =0∵FOC

                                = hx ẋ + (V ′′ g + V ′ gx ) ẋ.

Outside of the steady state, ẋ ̸= 0, so we can divide through to obtain

                                          ρV ′ = hx + V ′′ g + V ′ gx .                     (10.10)

Differentiating λ ≡ V ′ (x) with respect to time yields and using that ẋ = g (x, u),

                                               λ̇ = V ′′ ẋ = V ′′ g.

Substituting above and λ ≡ V ′ (x) into (10.10) yields

                                           λ̇ = ρλ − (hx + V ′ gx ) ,

which is equivalent to
                                           λ̇ = ρλ − Hx (x, u, λ) ,
which is the law of motion of the co-state variable obtained using the Maximum Principle.

10.5     9-step method
We will focus on stationary problems; i.e. return function and law of motions are time invariant.




                                                     - 164 -
Math Review                                                                                    2024


    9-step method for dynamic programming

      (i) Write the sequence problem (SP)

             • Choose state variables x and state space X
             • Describe the feasibility set Γ(x), return function F , and discounting β

      (ii) Check basic conditions: feasible set always non-empty + discounting

     (iii) Formulate the Bellman equation (BE)

     (iv) Check that the Contraction Mapping Theorem (CMT) applies—check conditions
          for X, Γ, F

      (v) Check properties of v (value function) and G (optimal policy correspondence).

             • Monotonicity of v
             • Concavity of v (if strict, G is single valued, in which case write g)
             • Differentiability of v and g

     (vi) Euler equation.

    (vii) Characterise steady states. Linearise Euler equation to study local stability

    (viii) Global stability

     (ix) Comparative statics
   Consider the following problem:

   • Discount factor β ∈ (0, 1), where β = 1/(1 + ρ) and ρ reflects time preference (not interest
     rate).

   • Demand curve given by p(q) that is stationary and satisfies law of demand (i.e. downward
     sloping). Utility S(q) is given by the area under the demand curve:
                                                        Z q
                                              S (q) =         p (z) dz.
                                                          0


   • State variable is x—the stock of “fish” at the beginning of the period.

   • Timing: (i) Beginning-of-period stock is x; (ii) q is harvested.

   • Law of motion xt+1 = ψ(xt − qt ) gives the next period’s stock, where ψ is continuous, strictly
     increasing, weakly concave and differentiable with ψ(0) = 0.

10.5.1   Step 1: Write SP
The sequence problem is given by
                                                  ∞
                                                  X
                              v̄ (x) := max
                                          ∞
                                                        β t S (qt )
                                       {qt }t=0
                                                  t=0

                                              s.t. xt+1 = ψ (xt − qt ) , ∀t
                                                    qt ∈ [0, xt ] ,

where [0, xt ] is the feasibility set, and we have discounting according to β.


                                                  - 165 -
Math Review                                                                                  2024


    In each period, the agent can consume all stock of fish or consume none. So, the feasibility
set that describes the next-period stock is given by Γ(x) = [ψ(0), ψ(x)] = [0, ψ(x)]. Since ψ is
continuous and strictly increasing φ := ψ −1 is well defined. We can therefore write

                                 φ (xt+1 ) = xt − qt ⇔ qt = xt − φ (xt+1 ) .

We can interpret φ as the amount we leave for reproduction tomorrow. Define the period-return
function as
                              F (xt , xt+1 ) = S (xt − φ (xt+1 )) .

10.5.2    Step 2: Check basic conditions
For each x ∈ X, the feasible set is Γ(x) is nonempty. This is sufficient if F is bounded. However,
if F is unbounded, some additional conditions are needed to ensure that growth is not “too fast”
(relative to β).

10.5.3    Step 3: Formulate BE
                                  v (x) := max {S (x − φ (y)) + βv (y)} ,
                                           y∈Γ(x)

where βv(y) is the discounted continuation value.

10.5.4    Step 4: Check that CMT applies
Let C(X) be the space of bounded, continuous function, ṽ(x) ∈ C(X) for all x ∈ X. Define the
Bellman operator as
                          (T ṽ) (x) := max {S (x − φ (y)) + βṽ (y)} .
                                             y∈Γ(x)

Suppose ṽ is bounded. For CMT to apply, we need that (i) T ṽ is bounded and continuous; (ii) T
is a contraction.

   • T ṽ is bounded:

         • Either, period return S is bounded—limq→∞ S(q) ≤ S̄ (S is bounded below by zero by
           definition);
         • Or, state space X is bounded—e.g. there exists some x̄ > 0 such that

                                                      ψ (x) < x, ∀x > x̄.

           We can think of this condition as saying that ψ(x) crosses the 45 degree line at some
           finite point.34
 34 Think of x as δk and ψ (x) as f (k) in the neoclassical growth model.




                                                      - 166 -
Math Review                                                       Takuma Habu                        2024


                                  (x)
                                  x
                                  k̄                                                        Takuma Habu
                                  k
                                  k
                                f (k)                                    (x)
                                                                         x
                                                                         k̄                               Takuma Habu
                                                                         k
                                                                         k
                                                                     f (k)       (x)
                                                                         x̄      x
                                                                                 k̄
                                                                                 k
            Define X = [0, x̄] as the state space.
                                                                                  k
   • T ṽ is continuous                                                         f (k)

         • F varies continuously with x and y—follows from the definition of S as an integral (which
           guarantees that S is continuous), and φ being continuous.
         • Γ (x) varies continuously with state (i.e. Γ is continuous as a correspondence)—follows
           from the fact that ψ is continuous.

   • T is a contraction: Check Blackwell’s sufficient condition (T monotone and discounts).

We therefore established that CMT applies, which means that

   • Principle of optimality holds; i.e. v = v̄.

   • T has a unique fixed point v such that T v = v.

10.5.5    Step 5: Check properties of v and G
Monotonicity:

   • Requirement 1. Γ(x) monotone (i.e. x′ ≥ x ⇒ Γ(x) ⊆ Γ(x′ ))—holds, since Γ(x) = [0, ψ(x)]
     and ψ is strictly increasing,

   • Requirement 2. F (x, y) weakly increasing in x—let Ĉ(X) ⊂ C(X) be the set of weakly
     increasing functions (so the set is closed). Want to show that ṽ ∈ Ĉ(X) ⇒ T ṽ ∈ Ĉ(X)—i.e.
     T : Ĉ(X) → Ĉ(X). Holds since the law of demand holds for p(q) so that S is weakly increasing
     in x.35               -1-


   • If F (x, y) is strictly increasing in x, then T ṽ is strictly increasing (i.e. the fixed point v lies
     in the interior of Ĉ(x)).

Concavity:
                                                                     -1-
   • Let C̃(x) ⊂ C(x) be the set of functions that are weakly concave (so the set is closed). We
     want to show that ṽ ∈ C̃ ⇒ T ṽ ∈ C̃—i.e. T : C̃(X) → C̃(X).

   • Requirement 1. F (x, y) weakly concave in (x, y)—holds since ψ is weakly concave (which
     implies that φ is weakly convex and −φ is weakly concave).
                                                                                -1-

                      10.5.6     Step 6: Euler equation
                      Write the first-order conditions and the envelope condition.

                      First-order condition The first-order condition with respect to y is given by

                                                                 −S ′ (x − φ (y ∗ )) φ′ (y ∗ ) + βv ′ (y ∗ ) ⪋ 0,              -1-

                                                                                                                                  -1-
                      where: (i) ≤ if y ∗ = 0 = ψ(0) (i.e. q = x); (ii) = if y ∗ ∈- 1 (0,- ψ(x)); and (iii) ≥ if y = ψ(x)
                                                                                                                  ∗
                                                                                      -1-
                      (i.e. q = 0). Notice that the first-order condition equates the marginal value of consumption today
                      −S ′ (·)φ′ (·) against the marginal value of stock in the future βv ′ (y ∗ ).
                                                                                                                        -1-
                      Envelope condition Substituting in y ∗ = g(x) into the BE:

                                                                  v (x) = S (x − φ (g (x))) + βv (g (x)) .
                                                                                  1

                      Differentiating- above
                                       1-
                                             with respect to x, while
                                                                1     noting that the Envelope
                                                                                      -1-
                                                                                               Theorem implies that we
                      need not consider derivatives with respect to g(x):

                                                                          v ′ (x) = S ′ (x − φ (g (x))) .

                      Euler equation Euler equation is given by combining the first-order and the envelope conditions.
                      From the envelope condition:

                                                                   v ′ (g (x)) = S ′ (g (x) − φ (g (g (x)))) .

                      Substituting this into the first-order condition gives the Euler equation:

                                                     S ′ (x − φ (g (x))) φ′ (g (x)) = βS ′ (g (x) − φ (g (g (x)))) .

                      Corner solutions We can think about what conditions would rule out corner solutions.
                        Consider first the case y ∗ = g(x) = 0. This is optimal if
                                                                                      -1-                                                                    -1-
                                                    ′        ′             ′                                        ′
                                                   S (x) φ (0) ≥ βS (g (x) − φ (g (g (x)))) = βS (−φ (g (0))) .


                                                                                            - 169 -
Math Review                                                                                          2024


where we also used the fact that φ(0) = 0 and g(x) = 0. If the inequality does not hold, then we
can rule out this corner solution.
   Now consider the case y ∗ = g(x) = ψ(x). This is optimal if

                      S ′ (x − φ (ψ (x))) φ′ (ψ (x)) ≤ βS ′ (ψ (x) − φ (g (ψ (x))))
                                ⇔ S ′ (0) φ′ (ψ (x)) ≤ βS ′ (ψ (x) − φ (g (ψ (x))))

where we used the fact that φ = ψ −1 . Again, if the inequality does not hold, then we can rule out
this corner solution. For example, if we have an Inada condition so that S ′ (0) = +∞, then we can
rule out this corner solution.

10.5.7    Step 7: Characterise steady states
Steady state: Interior In the steady state, x = g(x) = x̄, then the Euler equation becomes

                                S ′ (x̄ − φ (x̄)) φ′ (x̄) = βS ′ (x̄ − φ (x̄)) ,

which simplifies to
                                                 φ′ (x̄) = β.
Above pins down the steady-state value of stock. This can be seen as a rate of return condition—the
steady stock is such that the reproduction rate offsets discounting.

Steady state: Corner solutions           Steady states at corner solution are possible. If

                                          S ′ (x) φ′ (0) > βs′ (0) ,

then
                                                    x̄ = 0.
Similarly, if
                               S ′ (0) φ′ (ψ (x)) < βS ′ (ψ (x) − φ (ψ (x)))
then x̄ = ψ(x̄).

10.5.8    Step 8: Global stability
N/A.

10.5.9    Step 9: Comparative statics
Recall that we are not guaranteed that v is twice differentiable or that g(x) is differentiable. So we
need to conduct comparative statics without taking derivatives.
     Notice that, from the first-order condition, y ∗ = g(x) is given by the intersection of βv ′ (y) and
S ′ (x − φ(y))φ′ (y), and

   • βv ′ (·) is strictly decreasing in y since v is strictly concave;

   • S ′ (·)φ′ (·) is increasing in y since: (i) −φ is strictly decreasing in y, and we assume that S is
     strictly concave; (ii) S ′ > 0 since S is (assumed to be) strictly increasing; and (iii) φ is strictly
     increasing and strictly convex so that φ′ > 0 and φ′ is increasing in y.

We can plot the two curves.

Part III
Probability and statistics
11      Foundation
11.1      Probability space
We use probability to describe uncertainty, variability, and randomness. By uncertainty, we mean
that some outcome, ω, is unknown. We refer to the set of all possible outcomes as the sample space,
Ω, and any subset of outcomes, E ⊆ Ω, as an event.

Example 35. Consider a single coin flip. The set of all possible outcomes of a coin flip consists of
Heads (H) and Tails (T ); i.e., Ω = {H, T }. For example, {H} is an event, as is {T }.

Example 36. Consider flipping a coin twice. The set of all possible outcomes is now Ω =
{HH, HT, T H, T T }. For example, {HH} and {T H, HH, T T } are both events.

Example 37. Consider surveying the wage of a worker selected randomly from Ithaca. The
sample space is Ω = [wmin , ∞), where wmin is the minimum hourly wage. An example of an event
is {w ∈ R : w ≥ 2wmin }.

   We formalise the set of events as follows. A σ-algebra (or a σ-field ) on a set X is a collection of
subsets of X, denoted F, that satisfies the following conditions.38

  (i) The set itself belongs in F; i.e., X ∈ F.

  (ii) F is closed under complementation; i.e., E c ∈ F ∀E ∈ F.
                                                S∞
 (iii) F is closed under countable unions; i.e., k=1 Ek ∈ F ∀{Ek }∞
                                                                  k=1 ⊆ F.

Observe that above implies that ∅ ∈ F and that F is closed (why?) under countable intersections
(why?). The pair (X, F) is called a measurable space.
Remark 25 (Comparison with topology on X). Recall that a collection of subsets of X, T , is a
topology for X if (i) ∅, X ∈ T ; (ii) T is closed under finite intersections; (iii) T is closed under
arbitrary unions.

Example 38. Given a nonempty set of outcomes Ω, we can have many different σ-algebras. For
example, the smallest σ-algebra is F = {∅, Ω}, and the largest σ-algebra is F = 2Ω (the set of all
subsets of F). In case Ω consists of outcomes of two coin tosses, the set

                                             {∅, {HH} , {T T } , Ω}

is not a σ-algebra (why?); however, the set

                                       {∅, {HH} , {T H, HT, T T } , Ω}

is a σ-algebra.

   By giving a topology T on the sample space Ω, we can define the Borel algebra on Ω, denoted
B(Ω, T ), as the smallest σ-algebra that contains T .
 38 Incidentally, an algebra on X is a collection of subsets that contains the whole set and is closed under comple-

mentation and finite unions.




                                                      - 173 -
Math Review                                                                                                   2024


Example 39. When Ω is countable and T = 2Ω , then B(Ω, T ) = 2Ω . If Ω = R, then B(Ω, T ) is the
collection of all open and closed intervals, their countable unions, intersections, and complements.

   We say that an event E occurs if the outcome ω is in E; i.e., if ω ∈ E. Probabilities describe
how likely it is for an event to occur. Formally, given a sample space Ω and a σ-algebra, F, a
function P : F → [0, 1], is a probability function if

  (i) P ({E}) ≥ 0 for all E ∈ F;

 (ii) P (Ω) = 1;
                                                                                   S∞         P∞
(iii) For any countable collection of disjoint events {E1 , E2 , . . . , } ⊆ F, P ( i=1 Ei ) = i=1 P (Ei ).

The function P is sometimes also called a probability measure. We refer to the tuple (Ω, F, P ) as a
probability space. These conditions of probability function imply that, for any event E and E ′ :

                                        P (E c ) = 1 − P (E) ,
                                         P (∅) = 0,
                                         P (E) ≤ 1,
                                       E ⊆ E ′ ⇒ P (E) ≤ P (E ′ ) .

Exercise 24. Show that

                              P (E ∪ E ′ ) = P (E) + P (E ′ ) − P (E ∩ E ′ ) ,
                              P (E ∪ E ′ ) ≤ P (E) + P (E ′ ) ,
                              P (E ∩ E ′ ) ≥ P (E) + P (E ′ ) − 1.

    Given an event F with P (F ) > 0, the conditional probability of E given F , denoted P (E|F ), is
defined
                                                  P (E ∩ F )
                                      P (E|F ) :=            .
                                                    P (F )
Two events E and F are (statistically) independent, denoted E ⊥
                                                              ⊥ F , if

                                        P (E ∩ F ) = P (E) P (F ) .

Two events are dependent if they are not independent. For any two independent events E and F
with P (E), P (F ) > 0,
                            P (E|F ) = P (E) and P (F |E) = P (F ) .
Moreover, when events E and F are independent, the following pairs are also independent: E ⊥
                                                                                           ⊥ F c,
   ⊥ F and E c ⊥
Ec ⊥           ⊥ F c.

11.2     Random variables
A random variable is a real-valued outcome and is a function from the sample space Ω to the real
line R. You will often see that a random variable written using the upper case and the realisation
of a random variable using the lower case.

Example 40. In case of a coin flip, Ω = {H, T } and we may define a random variable as X : Ω → R,
ω 7→ 1{ω=H} .

   Let Ω be a sample space with a probability function P . A random variable X induces a new
sample space in R and we can define a probability function PX : B(Ω) → [0, 1] as

                                 PX (X ∈ B) := P ({ω ∈ Ω : X (ω) ∈ B})

for any set B in the Borel algebra of R.

Example 41. Consider three coin flips and let Ω = {H, T }3 . We can define a random variable X
as the total number of heads. The sample space X is X = {0, 1, 2, 3}. Then, B = 2X and

                               PX (X = 2) = P ({ω ∈ Ω : X (ω) = 2})
                                            = P ({HHT, HT H, T HH}) .

A random variable X always has a cumulative distribution function (CDF ), denoted FX , given by

                                    FX (x) = PX ({X ≤ x}) ∀x ∈ R.

The CDF FX has the following properties:

  (i) FX (·) is nondecreasing;

 (ii) limx→−∞ FX (x) = 0 and limx→∞ FX (x) = 1;

(iii) FX (·) is right continuous; i.e., limx↘x0 FX (x) = F (x0 ) for all x0 ∈ R.

Moreover, we have

                   P (a < X ≤ b) = FX (b) − FX (a) and P (X > b) = 1 − FX (b) .

From now on, we simply write P instead of PX as the probability function of X, and write F (x)
instead of FX (x) as the CDF of X. We write X ∼ F to mean that the random variable X is
distributed according to CDF F .
    We say that a random variable X is continuous if F is continuous and that X is discrete if F
is a step function. Note that a random variable can be neither continuous nor discrete. Given the
properties of CDF, the CDF of discrete random variables have jumps. A discrete random variable
can only take finite or countably infinite number of values.


                                                  - 175 -
Math Review                                                                                                  2024


    Suppose X is a discrete random variable. Its probability mass function (pmf ), denoted f , is
given by
                                     f (x) := P (X = x) .
To compute the probability of an event, we can sum over all points in that event; e.g.
                                                 X
                                P (X ≤ x) =               f (x̃) ,
                                                x̃∈{x′ ∈X:x′ ≤x}

density function (pdf ) of X, also denoted f , which is given by

                                                      d

properties:

   Given a random variable X with pmf or pdf, f , the support of X, denoted supp(X), is given by

                                   supp (X) := {x ∈ R : f (x) > 0} ;

i.e., supp(X) is set of realisations that can occur with positive probability.

11.2.1   Transformation of random variables
Given random variable X and a function g : R → R, Y = g(X) is also a random variable; Y is a
transformation of X via g. Observe that given any subset B ∈ B,

                                     P (Y ∈ B) = P (g (X) ∈ B)

and so the distribution of Y depends on F and g. Let sample spaces for X and Y as

                                     X := supp (X) Y := g (X ) .


                                                - 176 -
Math Review                                                                                         2024


The function g then defines a mapping g : X → Y. The distribution of Y can then be found as

                                P (Y ∈ E) = P (g (X) ∈ E)
                                              = P ({x ∈ X : g (x) ∈ E})
                                                                
                                              = P X ∈ g −1 (E) ,

where g −1 is the inverse mapping of g. Note that if X is a discrete random variable, X and Y are
both countable; i.e., Y is also a discrete random variable. Its pmf is given by
                     (            P                      P
                      PY (Y = y) = x∈g−1 (y) PX (X = x) = x∈g−1 (y) fX (x) if y ∈ Y

                      0                                                              if y ∈
                                                                                          /Y

That is, to obtain the pmf of Y we can proceed as follows: for each y ∈ Y, identify elements in the
set g −1 (y) and sum appropriate probabilities.

11.2.2     Expectations
The expectation of a random variable X, denoted E[X], is given by all its possible realisations
averaged according to the probability of each realisation occurring. We think of it as the typical
(or expected) value of an observation from the random variable. Provided that it exists, E[X] is
defined as                              (P

Since integration is a linear operation, expectation is a linear operator; i.e., for any a, b ∈ R,

                                         E [aX + b] = aE [X] + b.

You will use the following inequality many times in your first year.

Theorem 32 (Jensen’s inequality). Suppose g : R → R is convex, then for any random variable
X,
                                   g (E [X]) ≤ E [g (X)] .

   Clearly, above implies that if g is concave, then the inequality reverses (right?).

11.2.3   Moments
Given any m ∈ N and a random variable X, the mth moment of X, denoted µ′m , is defined

                                              µ′m := E [X m ] ,

and the mth central moment of X, denoted µm , is given by
                                                                  m
                                      µm := E [(X − E [X]) ] .

The second central moment is simply called the variance, denoted Var[X] or σX         2
                                                                                        . Thepstandard
deviation of X is the positive square root of the variance; i.e., std(X) (or σX ) is given by Var[X].

Exercise 29. Prove that Var[X] = E[X 2 ] − (E[X])2 and that Var(aX + b) = a2 Var[X] for any
a, b ∈ R.

   The first result, in particular, implies

                                              Var[X] ≤ E[X 2 ].

Thus, existence of variance is guaranteed if E[X 2 ] < ∞.

11.2.4   Quantiles
Expectation is not the only way to measure the “centre” of distributions. Let X have CDF FX .
Given any α ∈ [0, 1], the α-quantile of FX , denoted Q(α) , solves

                               FX (Q (α)) = α ⇔ P (X ≤ Q (α)) = α.

In particular, we let
                                 Q (α) := min {x ∈ R : FX (x) ≥ α} .
(why inf?). The 0.5-quantile for a random variable is called the median, and upper and lower
quantiles refer to the 0.75- and 0.25-quantiles, respectively.

11.2.5   Moment generating and characteristic functions
The moment generating function (gmf ) of a random variable X, denoted MX , is defined as

                                        MX (t) := E [exp (tX)]

provided that the expectation exists (oi.e., is finite) for t in a neighbourhood of 0. For the mgf to
exist, the density of X must have thin tails. The following shows that the curvature of MX at t = 0
encodes all moments of the distribution of X.

Theorem 33. Let MX be the mgf of a random variable X, then M (0) = 1, and for any m ∈ N,

                                      dm

11.3     Bivariate random vector
We first study the bivariate ran case in which n = 2. In this case, a random vector is a pair (X, Y )
that represents mapping from a sample space to a pair of numbers (x, y) ∈ R2 .

Example 43. Consider the “experiment” of flipping two coins. The sample space is

                                      Ω := {HH, HT, T H, T T } .

We can define a bivariate random vector (X, Y ) via

                        X = 1{H on the first coin} , Y = 1{H on the second coin} .

Hence, each sample pint in Ω is associated with a pair of numbers; e.g., HH is associated with
(1, 1).

11.3.1   Joint distributions
The joint distribution function of a random vector (X, Y ) is

                   FX,Y (x, y) = P (X ≤ x, Y ≤ y) = P ({X ≤ x} ∩ {Y ≤ y}) .

It has properties that are analogous to the univariate case:

  (i) F (x, y) is non-decreasing in each argument;

 (ii) limx→−∞ F (x, y) = limy→−∞ F (x, y) = 0 and limx→∞, y→∞ F (x, y) = 1;

(iii) 0 ≤ F ≤ 1;

(iv) P (a < X ≤ b, c < Y ≤ d) = F (b, d) − F (b, c) − F (a, d) + F (a, c).


                                                 - 180 -
Math Review                                                                                      2024


As before, a random vector (X, Y ) is continuous if its joint distribution function is continuous, and
is discrete if it is a step function.
    When (X, Y ) is a discrete bivariate random vector, its joint probability mass function (joint
pmf ) is given by
                                     fX,Y (x, y) = P (X = x, Y = y) .
Given a subset E ⊆ R2 ,                               X
                                 P ((X, Y ) ∈ E) =             fX,Y (x, y) .
                                                     (x,y)∈E

When (X, Y ) is a continuous bivariate random vector, its joint probability density function (joint
pdf ) is given by
                                                ∂2
                                 fX,Y (x, y) =      FX,Y (x, y)
                                               ∂x∂y
                          R∞ R∞

where the last line uses the Leibniz rule for differentiating integrals.
   Given a g that maps (X, Y ) to R, the expectation of g(X, Y ) is

11.3.3   Conditional distributions
When observing a random vector (X, Y ), the values of X and Y might be related (e.g., X is years
of education and Y is wage). In such case, knowledge of the value of X might gives us information
about Y (or not). Conditional distribution is a way to formalise what we can learn about one
dimension when we know something about the other dimension.
    Let (X, Y ) be a bivariate random vector. Let us consider the conditional distribution of Y given
X = x, denoted FY |X (·|x). When X has a discrete distribution,

                                                                     P (Y ≤ y, X = x)
                        FY |X (y|x) = P (Y ≤ y|X = x) =
                                                                         P (X = x)

for any x such that P (X = x) > 0 (check that FY |X is a valid cumulative distribution function).
When Y is also discrete, the conditional probability mass function of Y given X = x, denoted
fY |X (y|x), is

                                                           P (Y = y, X = x)   fX,Y (x, y)
                 fY |X (y|x) = P (Y = y|X = x) =                            =
                                                               P (X = x)        fX (x)

provided that fX (x) > 0 (check that fY |X is a valid pmf ). When instead FY |X is differentiable
with respect to y and P (X = x) > 0, the conditional probability density function of Y given X = x
is
                                                 ∂
                                  fY |X (y|x) =     FY |X (y|x) .
                                                 ∂y
If (X, Y ) is a continuous bivariate random vector with joint probability density function fX,Y and
marginal probability density functions fX and fY , then

                                                            fX,Y (x, y)
                                          fY |X (y|x) =
                                                              fX (x)

provided that fX (x) > 0 (check that fY |X is a valid pdf ). In this case, fY |X should be understood
as
                                                   ∂
                                   fY |X (y|x) =     FY |X (y|x) ,
                                                  ∂y

                                                       - 182 -
Math Review                                                                                      2024


where
                          FY |X (y|x) = lim P (Y ≤ y|x − ϵ ≤ X ≤ x + ϵ) .
                                        ϵ↘0

Since fY |X as a function of y is a pdf (or a pmf), we can use it in the same way as we did previously
to derive conditional expectations of Y |X or some function g of Y |X. Similarly, one can derive
conditional moments (e.g., variance) as before. Importantly, whoever, conditional moments such as
E[Y |X = x] and Var[Y |X = x] are both functions of x and hence E[Y |X] and Var[Y |X] are both
random variables, the values of which depend on the realisation of X.

Proposition 102. Let X and Y be two random variables. For any r ≥ 1 such that E[|Y |r ] < ∞,
                                                     r          r
                                      E [|E [Y |X]| ] ≤ E [|Y | ] .

Proof. Note g(u) := |u|r is convex for r ≥ 1. Hence, Jensen’s inequality says
                                                r           r
                                      |E [Y |X]| ≤ E [|Y | |X] .

Taking expectation of both sides with respect to X yields the result.                               ■

11.3.4   Independence
We say that two random variables X and Y are (statistically) independent, denoted X1Y , if

                                 FX,Y = FX (x) FY (y) ∀x, y ∈ R2 .

Suppose that X and Y are independent.

   • fX,Y (x, y) = fX (x) fY (y) ∀x, y ∈ R2 and hence

                                                    fX (x) fY (y)
                                  fY |X (y|x) =                   = fY (y) ;
                                                       fX (x)

     i.e., knowledge that X = x gives no information about Y .

   • For any functions g, h : R → R, g(X) and h(Y ) are independent.

   • For any functions g, h : R → R such that E[|g(X)|], E[|h(X)|] < ∞,

                                   E [g (X) h (Y )] = E [g (X)] E [h (Y )] .

   • Letting MX and MY denote the moment generation functions of X and Y , respectively, the
     MGF of a random variable Z := X + Y is

                                         MZ (·) = MX (·) MY (·) .

Theorem 34 (Law of Iterated Expectations). Suppose E[|Y |] < ∞. Then,
                                                             
                                   EY,X [Y ] = EX EY |X [Y |X] .

                              Var [Y ] = E [Var [Y |X]] + Var [E [Y |X]] .

11.3.5   Covariance and correlation
Given two random variables X and Y such that E[|X|2 ], E[|Y |2 ] < ∞, the covariance between X
and Y , denoted Cov(X, Y ), is given by

                              Cov [X, Y ] := E [(X − E [X]) (Y − E [Y ])]
                                           = E [XY ] − E [X] E [Y ] .

(make sure you can derive the second line from the first!) The correlation between X and Y ,
denoted Corr(X, Y ), is given by

                                                     Cov [X, Y ]
                                   Corr [X, Y ] := p
                                                    Var [X] Var [Y ]

If Cor[X, Y ] = 0 ⇔ Cov[X, Y ] = 0, we say that X and Y are uncorrelated. Otherwise, they are
correlated. Covariance and correlation measure a particular kind of linear relationship between X
and Y .

Theorem 35. Let X and Y be two random variables X and Y such that E[|X|2 ], E[|Y |2 ] < ∞.

  (i) −1 ≤ Corr[X, Y ] ≤ 1;

 (ii) |Corr[X, Y ]| = 1 if and only if there exists a nonzero a ∈ R and b ∈ R such that aX + b = Y
      holds with probability 1. Moreover, if Corr[X, Y ] = 1 (resp = −1), then a > 0 (resp. a < 0).


                                                  - 184 -
Math Review                                                                                   2024


Theorem 36. Let X and Y be two random variables X and Y such that E[|X|2 ], E[|Y |2 ] < ∞.
For any a, b ∈ R,

                     Var [aX + bY ] = a2 Var [X] + b2 Var [Y ] + 2abCov [X, Y ] .

If X and Y are uncorrelated, then

                             Var [aX + bY ] = a2 Var [X] + b2 Var [Y ] .

11.4    n-dimensional random vectors
An n-dimensional random vector is a function from a sample space to Rn for some n ∈ N.
    Let X be an n-dimensional random vector. The joint distribution function, denoted FX , of X
is given by
                   F (x) := P (X ≤ x) = P (X1 ≤ x1 , X2 ≤ x2 , . . . , Xn ≤ xn ) .
When X is discrete, the joint pmf is f (x) = P (X = x). When X is continuous and differentiable,
the joint pdf is given by
                                                 ∂n
                                 f (x) =                     F (x) .
                                           ∂x1 ∂x2 · · · ∂xn
The joint pdf satisfies The expectation of an ndimensional random vector X is the (column) vector
of expectation of its elements; i.e.,
                                                        
                                                 E [X1 ]
                                                E [X2 ] 
                                                        
                                       E [X] =     ..   .
                                                    .   
                                                    E [Xn ]

The variance-covariance matrix of X, denoted ΣX , is given by
                               h                           i
                                                         ⊤
            ΣX := Var [X] = E (X − E [X]) (X − E [X])
                                                                                     
                                  Var [X1 ]    Cov [X1 , X2 ] · · ·    Cov [X1 , Xn ]
                              Cov [X2 , X1 ]    Var [X2 ]    ···      Cov [X2 , Xn ] 
                                                                                     
                          =          ..             ..        ..            ..       .
                                      .              .           .           .       
                                Cov [Xn , X1 ] Cov [Xn , X2 ] · · ·     Var [Xn ]

Theorem 37. ΣX is symmetric and positive semi-definite

Proof. Symmetry of ΣX follows from symmetry of Cov(·, ·) (check). For positive semi-definiteness,
note that for any column vector z ∈ Rn ,
                                       h                        i

Theorem 38. Suppose an n-dimensional random vector X has expectation µ and variance-covariance
matrix ΣX . For any A ∈ Rq×m , the random vector AX is a random vector with mean Aµ and
variance-covariance matrix AΣX A⊤ .

   The marginal pdf (resp. pmf) of any subset of coordinates of X = (X1 , X2 , . . . , Xn )⊤ can be


                                               - 185 -
Math Review                                                                                    2024


computed by integrating out (resp. summing) the joint pdf (resp. pmf) over all possible values of
other coordinates.
   The conditional pdf (resp. pmf) of any subset of coordinates of X = (X1 , X2 , . . . , Xn )⊤ is
obtained by dividing the joint pdf (resp. pmf) by the marginal pdf (resp. pmf) of the remaining
coordinates.

Proposition 104 (Holder’s Inequality). Let p > 1 and q > 1 such that p1 + 1q = 1, then for any
random vectors X and Y
                                               p 1        q 1
                           E X⊤ Y ≤ (E [∥X∥ ]) p (E [∥Y∥ ]) q .

   Setting p = q = 2 gives the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality.

Proposition 105 (Minkowski’s Inequality). For any random vectors X and Y and p > 0,
                                            1                          1                   1
                                        p                      p                   p
                         (E [∥X + Y∥ ]) p ≤ (E [∥X∥ ]) p + (E [∥Y∥ ]) p .

   In general, for any random vector X and a constant vector a and p > 0,
                                            p                              p   p
                              E [∥X + a∥ ] ≤ C · (E [∥X∥ ] + ∥a∥ ) ,

where C is a constant that depends on p. For example, if a = −E[X], then
                                                p                          p           p
                           E [∥X − E [X]∥ ] ≤ C (E [∥X∥ ] + ∥E [X]∥ )
                                                                           p
                                                    ≤ 2CE [∥X∥ ] .

We can also about
                                            p                      p           p
                            E [∥X + Y ∥ ] ≤ C (E [∥X∥ ] + E [∥Y ∥ ]) ,
where C depends on p.

independent.

useful when you have a constant absolute risk aversion utility function, i.e., u(x) = −a exp(−ax)

x1−a /(1 − a) with a > 0, and a log-normally distributed risk.

12       Relationships between common distributions
12.1     Uniform distribution and other distributions
Let F be a CDF. Then, the quantile function of F is F −1 : R → R defined as

                               F −1 (α) := min {z ∈ R : F (z) ≥ α} .

Observe that F −1 is well-defined because F is nondecreasing and right-continuous, which imply
that the set of values with cumulative probability equal to or higher than α is closed.
    The following tells us that we can build random variable with any CDF F from a uniformly
distributed random variable.

Proposition 110. Let U ∼ Uniform[0, 1] and F be a CDF. Then, Z = F −1 (U ) is a random
variable with CDF F .

Exercise 32. Consider a logistic random variable with CDF

                                                      1
                                         F (x) =           .
                                                   1 + e−x

Show that X = ln( 1−U
                   U
                      ) is a logistic random variable when U is uniformly distributed on [0, 1].

12.1.1    Bernoulli, Binomial and Poisson distributions
A random variable Zi follows a Bernoulli distribution with parameter p, Z ∼ Bernoulli(p), if it
takes the value 1 with probability p and 0 with probability 1 − p. Its probability mass function is
given by
                                         1−z
                     fZi (z) = pz (1 − p)    = pz + (1 − p) (1 − z) ∀z ∈ {0, 1}
and the cumulative distribution function is given by
                                           
                                                    if z < 0
                                           0
                                           
                                 FZi (z) =     1 − p if 0 ≤ z < 1 .
                                             
                                             
                                             1      if z ≥ 1

We think of Bernoulli distribution describing a single coin toss and p as the probability that we see
(say) head. More generally, we think of Bernoulli as describing the outcome of a single experiment
with binary outcomes. The first two moments are:

                     E [Z] = 1 · p + 0 · (1 − p) = p,
                                              2
                    Var [Z] = E Z 2 − (E [Z]) = E [Z] (1 − E [Z]) = p (1 − p) ,

   A random variable Z follows a Binomial distribution with parameters (n, p), denoted Z ∼
Binomial(n, p), if its probability mass function is given by
                                                
                                                n z           n−z
                                     fZ (z) =       p (1 − p)
                                                z

and the cumulative distribution function is given by

                                             ⌊z⌋  
                                             X    n                 n−i
                                  FZ (z) =             pi (1 − p)         .
                                             i=1
                                                   i


                                               - 191 -

We think of binomial distribution as describing the distribution of the number of heads when we

12.1.2   Poisson and exponential
A random variable Z follows an exponential distribution with parameter λ if its probability density
function is given by
                                        fZ (z) = λe−λz
and and the cumulative distribution function is given by

                                          FZ (z) = 1 − e−λz .

Its first two moments are given by

                                                 1             1
                                       E [Z] =     , Var [Z] = 2 .
                                                 λ            λ



                                                  - 193 -
Math Review                                                                                                  2024


Recall that Poisson distribution describes the probability of a given number of occurrences of an
event in a fixed amount of time. The exponential distribution describes the length of time between
the occurrences of these events (which is constant).
   To see this, let ∆ be the “fixed amount of time” and suppose that the event occurs at rate λ at
any point in time. Then, on average, there will be λ∆ occurrences of events per ∆ units of time.
The Poisson distribution associated with this is given by
                                                                  y
                                                           (λ∆) e−λ∆
                                          Pr (Y = y) =
                                                               y!

and Pr(Y = 0) = e−λ∆ is the probability of no occurrence during ∆ units of time. Now let Z
describe the time it takes for the first occurrence of the event. Then, the probability that the event
occurs after ∆ units of time is equivalent to the probability of no occurrence of the event during
∆ units of time; i.e., Pr(Z > ∆) = Pr(Y = 0). Thus, we can write the probability that the event
occurs by period ∆ is

                     Pr (Z ≤ ∆) = 1 − Pr (Z > ∆) = 1 − Pr (Y = 0) = 1 − e−λ∆ .

Hence, Z follows an exponential distribution with parameter λ. Note that since Poisson is memory-
less (i.e., the rate of arrival is constant), time between any two events follows an exponential
distribution with parameter λ.
Remark 26. Incidentally, suppose that Z1 and Z2 follows Poisson distribution with parameter λ1
and λ2 respectively and they are independent. We can interpret Z1 and Z2 as describing the time
until first occurrence of event of types 1 and 2 respectively. Let Z := Z1 + Z2 , then

12.2       Gamma distribution
Recall the gamma function:                             Z ∞

                                                       e−t tα−1

                                                        Γ (α)

is a probability density function. Now apply the change of variables via x := βt ⇔ t = x/β so that
dt = β −1 dx. Hence,
                              Z ∞                 Z       α−1
                                                 1 ∞ − βx x

for all x ∈ (0, ∞), α > 0 and β > 0. Above is the probability density associated with gamma
distribution with parameters (α, β). We refer to α as the shape parameter since it mostly influences
the peakedness of the distribution and β as the scale parameter since it mostly influences the spread
of the distribution.
    The first two moments of a random variable Z that follows a gamma distribution with parameters
(α, β), denoted Z ∼ Gamma(α, β), are given by

                                            E [Z] = αβ, Var [Z] = αβ 2 .

Gamma distribution relates to many commonly used distributions. Let Z ∼ Gamma(α, β).

   • Suppose α is an integer and Y ∼ Poisson(x/β). Then, for any x ∈ (0, ∞),

                                                 Pr (Z ≤ z) = Pr (Y ≥ α) .

   • Suppose α = p/2 for some integer p and β = 2, then Z follows a χ2 -distribution with p degrees
     of freedom.

   • Suppose α = 1, then Z follows an exponential distribution with parameter 1/β.

12.3     Conjugate distribution property
Consider a problem of inferring a distribution for a parameter θ ∈ Θ given some data Z. The
posterior belief is given by f (θ|Z), which, by Bayes rule, can be written as

                                                       f (z|θ) π (θ)
                                 f (θ|Z = z) = R             ,
                                                  θ̃∈Θ
                                                       f   z|θ̃ π θ̃ dθ̃

where π is the prior belief over Θ and f (Z|θ) is the conditional density of Z given θ. If the posterior
distribution, f (θ|Z = z), is in the same probability distribution family as the prior probability
distribution, π(θ), the prior and posterior are then called conjugate distributions and the prior is
called a conjugate prior. We will demonstrate below that normally distributed prior gives rise to
normally distributed posterior.
    At each date t ∈ N, suppose that a Bayesian decision maker observes Zt = θ + Wt , where each
                                                                                    
Wt are identically and independently distributed according to N 0, σ 2 . Let the decision maker’s

Thus, we see that the posterior mean is a weighted average of the observed Zt+1 = z and the prior,
where the weights are given by the ratio of precision of between the prior and Zt+1 |θ. If the prior
is relatively more (less) precise, then the weights placed on Zt+1 = z is smaller (larger).

13      Stochastic dominance
Our goal is to give a partial ordering of a set F of CDFs. All of the ordering we introduce below are
partial orders on F (or equivalently, on the set of random variables distributed according to some
F ∈ F).

13.1     First-order stochastic dominance
Proposition 111. Let F and G be two CDFs with a common support [a, b]. The following are
equivalent.

  (i) F first-order stochastically dominates (FOSD) G; i.e., F ≥F OSD G.39

 (ii) F (t) ≤ G(t) ∀t ∈ [a, b].

(iii) E[u(Z)] ≥ E[u(Y )] for any increasing function u : [a, b] → R, where Z ∼ F and Y ∼ G.

   Let Z ∼ F and Y ∼ G. To make sense of (ii), rewrite the condition as 1 − F (t) ≥ 1 − G(t)
and recall that 1 − F (t) is the probability that random variables are greater than t. Hence, Z first-
order stochastically dominates Y means that, for each possible realisation of the random variables
t ∈ [a, b], the probability that Z has a realisation greater than t is larger than that of Y . (ii) also
implies that the F lies everywhere (weakly) below that of G on the support. For (iii), note that the
identity function is (weakly) increasing so that FOSD implies that the mean of X is greater than
that of Y . The third characterisation can be generalised to deal with random vectors.

13.2     Second-order stochastic dominance
We now introduce a partial order among random variables with a common support and a common
mean. We first define the idea of a mean-preserving spread. Let F and G be two CDFs with a
common support and suppose Z ∼ F and Y ∼ G.

Proposition 112. Let F and G be two CDFs with a common support [a, b] and a common mean.
The following are equivalent.

  (i) G is a mean-preserving spread of F , denoted F ≥M P S G.

 (ii) E[Z|Y ] = Y , where Z ∼ F and Y ∼ G.

(iii) Y is a garbling of Z; i.e., there exists a random variable W with E[W |Z] = 0 such that
         d
       Y = Z + W .40

    (iii) implies that if F ≥M P S G, then we can obtain Z ∼ F from Y ∼ G in the following way: (i)
draw z from Z; (ii) draw w from W with E[W ] = 0; and (iii) z := y + w. The figure below shows
an example of a mean-preserving spread of a random variable Z, which has equal probability mass
on three equidistant points. In this example, the distribution of W depends on the realisation of
Z. If the left-most point is realised, then w = 0 so that probability mass of Y = Z + W lies in the
same location as Z (purple). In contrast, if the middle point is realised, then W ’splits’ this point
into three equidistance points with equal masses (red). Finally, if the right-most point is realised,
W splits the mass into two with weight 1/4 on the left and weight 3/4 on the right. The distances
are such that the mean is preserved.
 39 Given Z ∼ F and Y   ∼ G, we also often say that Z first-order stochastically dominates Y if F ≥F OSD G.
    d
 40 = means that the two sides have the same associated CDFs.

13.2.1   Hazard rate
                                                                  1

Let T be a random variable that represents the time until a particular event (e.g., T is the length
of unemployment period and the event is finding a job). Let F denote its associated cumulative
distribution function so that F (t) = Pr(T ≤ t) denotes the probability that the event has occurred
by time t. The survivor function
                             1     is the probability of surviving past t and define

                                        S (t) := 1 − F (t) = Pr (T > t) .
                                        1
               1
                      1
The conditional probability of leaving the initial state within the time interval t until t + h, given
                                                             1
survival up to time t is given by               1
                                                     1
                                                                                           1


                                             Pr (t ≤ T < t + h|T ≥ t) . 1 1
                                                                                          1
            1
Dividing 1 above by h gives the average probability of leaving per unit time period over the interval
t until t + h. Taking the limit as h ↘ 0 gives the hazard rate function, denoted λ(t); i.e.,

                                                     Pr (t ≤ T < t + h|T ≥ t)
           1                         λ (t) := lim             1               .
                                        1     h↘0               h                               1
                           1                                                          1
At each time t, the hazard rate function is the instantaneous rate of event occurring per unit of
time. Observe that 1                                       1
                                                 1                                                    1
                               Pr ({t ≤ T < t + h} ∩ {t ≤ T })   Pr (t ≤ T < t + h)   F (t + h) − F (t)
Pr (t ≤ T < t + h|T ≥ t) =                                     =                    =                   .
                                         Pr (t ≤ T )                 Pr (t ≤ T )          1 − F (t)

Recalling that F is right-continuous,

                          Pr (t ≤ T < t + h|T ≥ t)           1     F (t + h) − F (t)   f (t)
          λ (t) = lim                              = lim                             =       .
                    h↘0              h               h↘0 1 − F (t)         h           S (t)

There exists a bijection between the hazard rate function and the CDF of T . To see this, observe
that
                                         f (t)      d ln (1 − F (t))

Integrating both sides over [0, z] gives
              Z z                  Z z

where we used that F (0) = 0. Therefore, it follows that
                                                  Z z           
