Observe that if we choose the hazard rate function to be constant so that λ(t) := λ, then

                                                F (z) = 1 − exp (−λz) ,

which is the CDF for exponential distribution.

13.3     Domination in terms of hazard rate and reverse hazard rates
Given two CDFs F and G, we say that F dominates G in terms of the hazard rate, denoted
F ≥HR G, if
                               f (z)       g (z)
                    λF (z) =           ≤           = λG (z) ∀z ∈ R.
                             1 − F (z)   1 − G (z)
Similarly, we say that F dominates G in terms of the reverse hazard rate, denoted F ≥RHR G, if

                                                f (z)   g (z)
                                σF (z) :=             ≥       =: σG (z) ∀z ∈ R.
                                                F (z)   G (z)

Note that (F, ≥HR ) and (F, ≥RHR ) are both partially ordered sets.
   Domination in terms of hazard rate and reverse hazard rate both imply first-order stochastic
dominance.

Proposition 114. Suppose that F ≥HR G or F ≥RHR G for some CDFs F and G. Then,
F ≥F OSD G.

Proof. Recall 13.1. Then, by monotonicity of integral, it follows that if F ≥HR G, then
                          Z z                        Z z             
         F (z) = 1 − exp −      λF (t) dt ≤ 1 − exp −          λG (t) dt = G (z) ∀z ∈ R.
                                    0

Hence, F ≥F OSD G.
   Observe that
                                   Z ∞                                            Z ∞          
                 d ln F (z)
        σF (z) =            ⇒                σF (t) dt = − ln F (z) ⇔ F (z) = exp −    σF (t) dt ,
                     dt                 z                                                 z



                                                         - 202 -
Math Review                                                                                        2024


where we used the fact that limz→∞ F (z) = 1. Hence, if F ≥RHR G, then
                          Z ∞                  Z ∞             
            F (z) = exp −        σF (t) dt ≤ exp −       σG (t) dt = G (z) ∀z ∈ R.
                                z                         z

Hence, F ≥F OSD G.                                                                                    ■

13.4     Relation with domination in terms of the likelihood ratio
Given two CDFs F and G, we say that F dominates G in terms of the likelihood ratio, denoted
F ≥LR G if
                             f (z)   f (y)
                                   ≤       ∀z, y ∈ R : z < y.
                             g (z)   g (y)
Equivalently, F ≥LR G is f /g is nondecreasing. If F ≥LR G, then the graph of densities f and g
can intersect at most once. Note that (F, ≥LR ) is a partially ordered set.
   We will show that domination in terms of the the likelihood ratio implies domination in terms
of both hazard rate and reverse hazard rate. Combined with Proposition 114, it follows that
dominance in terms of the likelihood ratio implies first-order stochastic dominance.
Proposition 115. Suppose that F ≥LR G for some CDFs F and G. Then, F ≥HR G and
F ≥ RRHR G.
Proof. Suppose F ≥LR G. Then for all z < y,

                                    f (z)   f (y)   f (y)   g (y)
                                          ≤       ⇔       ≥       .
                                    g (z)   g (y)   f (z)   g (z)

Hence, by monotonicity of integration,
                                  Z ∞             Z ∞
             1       1 − F (z)         f (y)          g (y)      1 − G (z)     1
                  =             =            dy ≥           dy =           =        .
           λF (z)      f (z)        z  f (z)       z  g (z)        g (z)     λG (z)

It follows that λF (z) ≤ λG (z) for all z ∈ R; i.e., F ≥HR G.
    We also have that, for all z < y,

                                    f (z)   f (y)   f (z)   g (z)
                                          ≤       ⇔       ≤       .
                                    g (z)   g (y)   f (y)   g (y)

Hence, by monotonicity of integration,
                                   Z y            Z y
                  1        F (y)       f (z)          g (z)      G (y)     1
                       =         =           dz ≥           dz =       =        .
                σF (y)     f (y)    0 f (y)        0 g (y)       g (y)   σG (y)

It follows that σF (y) ≥ σG (y) for all y ∈ R; i.e., F ≥RHR G.                                        ■

   To recap, we have shown that

                               F ≥LR G ⇒ F ≥HR G and F ≥RHR G,
                               F ≥HR G ⇒ F ≥F OSD G,
                              F ≥RHR G ⇒ F ≥F OSD G.

13.5     Order statistics
Let (Zi )ni=1 be a collection of n independent random variables with associated CDF and density
given by F and f , respectively. Let (Z(k) )nk=1 be a rearrangement so that Z(1) ≥ Z(2) ≥ · · · ≥ Z(n) .


                                                - 203 -
Math Review                                                                                           2024


Each random variable Z(k) is referred to as the kth order statistics. Let F(k) denote the distribution
of Z(k) with corresponding density function f(k) . Our task is to express f(k) as a function of f .

13.5.1    Highest-order statistic
We derive the expression for F(1) and f(1) . The event Z(1) ≤ z is the same as the event that Zi ≤ z
for all i ∈ {1, . . . , n}. Since each Zi is an independent draw from F , we have
                                                          n
                                        F(1) (z) = (F (z)) ≡ F n (z) .

Hence, using the chain rule, the associated density is

                                         f(1) (z) = nF n−1 (z) f (z) .

The following is then immediate.

Proposition 116. If F ≥F OSD G, then F(1) ≥F OSD G(1) .

13.5.2    Second-order statistic
We now derive the expression for F(2) and f(2) . The event that Z(2) ≤ z is the union of the following
disjoint events: (i) Zi ≤ z for all i ∈ {1, . . . , n}; (ii) n − 1 of Zi ’s are less than or equal to z and
exactly one is greater than y. There are n different ways in which (ii) can occur. Hence,

                               F(2) (z) = F n (z) + nF n−1 (z) (1 − F (z))
                                          = nF n−1 (z) − (n − 1) F n (z) .

The associated density function is

                            f(2) (z) = n (n − 1) (1 − F (z)) F n−2 (z) f (z) .

Proposition 117. If F ≥F OSD G, then F(2) ≥F OSD G(2) .

13.5.3    kth-order statistic
In general, we have the following for the density of the kth order statistic:

                                         n!                              k−1
                     f(k) (z) =                     F n−k (z) (1 − F (z))     f (z) ,
                                  (k − 1)! (n − k)!

where the n!/(k−1)!(n−k!) is the number of different ways in which any draws of {Zi }ni=1 could have
been the kth order statistic. F n−k (z) is the probability that n − k draws are below z, 1 − F k−1 (z)
is the probability that k − 1 draws are above z and f (z) is the density associated with drawing z.
Remark 27. The tradition in statistic is to worry about the smallest order statistics; i.e., Z (k) means
the kth smallest among (Zi )ni=1 . The density of the kth order statistic is defined as

                                            n!                k            n−k
                      f (k) (z) :=                     (F (z)) (1 − F (z))     f (z) .
                                     (k − 1)! (n − k)!
